Tho Crowd
Gustave le Bon practically discovered for
us the crowd, and since his first book various
author! have investigated its characteristics.
no of the best of such studies is Martin
yonway s The Crowd in Peace and War
(lionginans, (freen, per Dymock’s Library).
e all. are part of crowds; we all contribute
to that extra-human thing called the crowd-
soul. Lor the crowd has a soul of its own.
Lvery Australian is part of all sorts
or crowds: the Australian crowd, the school
ciowd, the Empire crowd, the temporary
crowd in the street, the theatre crowd, the
political crowd, the religious or anti-reli-
gious crowd, the social crowd, the trades
union, the professional crowd, the
Argus crowd, Tiie Bulletin crowd, the
poultry-farmers’ crowd, the Win-the-War
crowd, the Anzac crowd. The minimum
number of individuals that can form a
crowd, that can merge itself into the one
organisation, is . 12. The author suggests
that our jury of 12 has been arrived at as
the minimum number that can normally be
relied upon to act . collectively as a crowd.
A cr»wd hypnotises its members to behave
as one organism. The wdiole aim of drill is
to absorb the soldier’s, individuality, when
necessary, into the crowd-squl. The Hun
is the finest crowd-maker of them all; he is
trained to become a crowd; when he mur-
ders or rapes he is no longer an individual:
he is part of the sort of crowd he has been
carefully drilled into being.
The reason why a collective body of
human beings differs from so many indivi-
duals is because no two individuals can ever
think alike, whilst any number can feel
alike. For a crowd has all the emotions,
but no intellect. It can feel, but it cannot
think. People are absorbed into a crowd
by a process purely emotional. Political
parties are not the incorporation of any
reasoned set of opinions or theories, but
only of a group of emotions. Views and
theories can reside only in a brain, and that
no crowd possesses.
And this new kind of beast, the crow T d, acts
according to its instinct. It is always
intolerant; it has highly developed the in-
stinct of self-preservation ; it fears freedom
of speech, for an orator might be able to
create a crowd adverse to it. It need's ~a
leader, since only through a voice can it
t -pr/vK S itse]f. Conway defines, tliree kinds
of crowd-leaders; .crowd-coinpellers, crowd-
exponents, crowd-representatives. There are
few of the first class, the men that create
and make the crowd do their bidding—
Napoleon will do for an easy example. The
erowd-eompeller gives a brain to the brain-
less body of his crowd. So far, the species
does not seem to have been evolved in Aus-
tralia. But we possess several specimens
of the crowd-exponent. “The crowd-expo-
nent is the man who feels by sympathetic
insight and mere sensitiveness of nature as
the crowd feels or is going to feel, and who
expresses in clear language the emotion of
the dumb organism. For all the ideas of a
crowd are necessarily of a vague emotional
sort and can only be expressed by them
in the form of shouts or actions of approval
or dissent.” He may be a speaker, or a
writer, or a group of newspaper writers —
but whatever he is he is the voice of the
crowd, and his utterance is really theirs.
“He is by nature akin to an artist.” His
job is not to think out the solution of some
difficult problem in the privacy of his study,
and then go forth and proclaim a new
gospel to an unwilling world. He waits
till he senses the vague feeling of the crowd,
and then says with eloquence and enthu-
siasm what they are unable to say. “Where-
upon they raise him aloft with loud applause
and worship him like a god because his voice
ha° 'giver, Lhem words and enabled the
crowd to realise its own mighty, if vague
and ill-defined, existence and power.... The
crowd merely as it were sniffed around Win-
ston Churchill, recognised him immediately
as one of its own sort, wagged its tail, and
game to heel.” So Lloyd George is the most
prominent and powerful crow’d-exponent in
our day. “He has never manifested any
trace of an individual mind or of indepen-
dent thought.” But he has perfectly voiced
the ideas of his crowd. One of the charac-
teristics of a crowd-exponent is that his
emotions respond as sensitively to those of
a crowd as ever a barometer to changes in
atmospheric pressure. And another is that
he is the typically ambitious man. The
approval of the crowd is the breath of his
life. Once he has tasted its savor he cannot
live without the incense of crowd-approval.
Anybody mention Hughes? Or Holman?
Crowd-representatives are less interesting.
The King is one ; his job is merely to ex-
press the crowd emotion. Accidents occur
every day, but the crowd takes no notice.
Let a great accident kill at once enough
men to look like a crowd, the public feels
the wound, and its royal spokesman ex-
presses the public emotion. “The publication
by Darwin of The Origin of Species was a
far more important event in the world’s his-
tory than the opening of docks at Liverpool;
but the one was the act of an individual
addressing indi iduals. the other the con-
cern of the crowd.” So royalty gave public
recognition only to the latter. Hughes and'
Woodrow Wilson have both expressed the
view that their job is to do as democracy
tells, them- And government by public
opinion, crowd-government, must possess all
the qualities of a crowd. As this is the sort
of government Australians ..suffer under,
there is no need to stress this point. The
instinct of all crowds is to dominate, to
capture and overwhelm the individual, to
make him its slave, to absorb all his life for
its service. An organised crowd sets the
interests of the many before those of the
individual, and it is always the superior
individual whom the crowd destroys and the
inferior whom it fosters. “If some are not
to drink, all must not drink. If some are
to be slow workers, all must be slow workers.
AH must be depressed to the level which all
can reach, inevitably a low level.”
As one of the fundamental characteristics
or crowds is~ to expand indefinitely, like a
living organism, every crowd hates all other
similar crowds. Conway cites the hostility of
organised Christian religious bodies. Also
neighboring towns.” The love that is mislaid
between Sydney and Melbourne is an apt
Australian illustration of crowd hostility. So
ail independent nations are mutually hostile
u° another. I n times of peace this
hostility is latent; but this inherent hostility,
a crowd-characteristic, is the cause of war.
If all the nations of the earth are not
always fighting it is not because they don’t
want to, but because they have other' things
to do and. also because they are restrained
by the action of contrary forces.” A state
of war is the natural condition of independ-
ent crowds. That is the difference between
the individual and a crowd. If these crowds
are united by alliance under any kind of
over-crowd, war would not occur.' Thus so
j, on B as the crowds known as Dominions
teel themselves united under the over-crowd
called Empire, there will be no war within
the Empire. “Armageddon will be the last
battle between the last two over-crowds into
which the world will some day consolidate.”
Enthusiasm for humanity shows no signs of
becoming a crowd characteristic and there-
fore a guarantee for world peace. The only
event that would, for centuries yet, unite
the whole race into one over-crowd would
be the threat of invasion from another
planet.
a. book for the “Red Page” crowd.
According to Gerald Stanley Dee, author
of We (Allen, per Dymock’s Library), there
are differences in English, American and
German crowds. “An English crowd is
round, and shaped a good deal like a bomb.”
An English crowd refuses to stay a crowd.
It explodes into individuals. “The only
really safe crowd on this earth is an Eng-
lish crowd. It is safe because no English-
man proposes to be mixed up with any other
Englishman more than a minute if it can
possibly be helped.” An American crowd
is wedge-shaped, and any force applied to
it only drives all of it together, further and
harder together. “An American crowd is
like a cartridge. A German crowd is shaped
like a hammer, with the Kaiser for a handle.
Germans allow themselves to be placed in
neat, orderly squares fitting closely to-
gether, where they can be easily handled.
A German crowd is comparatively safe be-
cause it can be counted on and because it
has a handle. An English crowd is com-
paratively safe because the first thing an
English crowd does is to blow itself apart.
An American crowd blows itself into a
thousand thousand smithereens of being
more of a crowd than ever.” The emotional
American never feels himself happy unless
he is a crowd.
Harold Bell Wright, the super-best-seller
of the U.S.A., hastens to proffer his own ver-
sion, of “How to Write a Best-Seller.” In
N.Y. Bookman, we learn that When a
Man’s a Man has taxed the capacity of “the
largest fiction factory in Chicago,” over
5000 copies having been sent out and sold
daily. The largest fiction factory himself
attributes his wqnderful popularity to the
alleged fact that his work—and he says it
with all modesty—is “the structural steel,
the anatomy, and this I clothe with what
I call life. If you put solid bones in your
story, have real ribs and real femurs and
real tibias, you have a real groundwork to
build upon. With such a groundwork there
is no difficulty in fiction-writing, provided
you use good understandable English.
People will recognise this instantly and you
will be a successful novelist. .. .After my
first novel the stories kept crowding in upon
me. I could hardly keep my mind off them,
they clamored so for attention. I wrote
The Calling of Dan Matthews with one hand
and The Winning of Barbara Worth with
the other.” He had been a pastor, “but
when I came to see that I could reach a
larger congregation with my books than
from my pulpit I gave up the ministry.”
When a Man’s a Man was written in 13
weeks, against doctor’s orders, for his ribs
had been broken in a motor-accident. “By'
working very hard and fast I finished the
135,000 words, and all the pictures, went
out to the- cross-triangle ranch, where the
scenes were laid, read the story to the Dean
and the cowboys for their technical criticism,
made the corrections they suggested”—"and
thus fulfilled his publisher’s contract. You
see how easy it is!
Australian
truest Scott, History Professor at Mel-
bourne University, writes a lucid and well-
balanced Short History of Australia (Ox-
ford University Press, 3s. 6d.). It “begins
with a blank space on the map, and ends
with the record of a new name on the map,
that of Anzac.” The author, however,
opens up a debatable question when he states
that: “History is a record of the doings
of men living in communities, not of blind,
nerveless forces.” Scott’s recognition of
the importance of literature in history is
given emphasis in his chronology.
In 1859, the only two outstanding events
mentioned are: Colony of Queensland pro-
claimed and the publication of Kingsley’s
Geoffrey Hamlyn. In 1870 the British
troops were withdrawn from Australia and
Adam Lindsay Gordon died. One of the
four events of 1574 was the publication of
Clarke’s For the Term of His Natural Life;
and in the three events mentioned for 1877,
the publication of Bruuton Stephen’s poem,
The Dominion of Australia, has place. In
1895 the Victorian Wages Board system
was established, and Paterson published The
Man from Snowy River; while in 1896 the
only interesting event that is worthy of men-
tion in Australia was the publication of
Lawson’s In the Days When the World Was
Wide. In the history itself, however, Aus-
tralian literature is but briefly discussed, and
a silence is preserved of the recent develop-
ments of our literature—in this respect
Arthur W. Jose’s History of Australasia,
though published earlier, is more up-to-date
- —while Scott introduces us to a new Aus-
tralian poet, Bargrave Boake. The early
maps of Australia, or of the place where
Australia ought to have been, are a valu-
able feature of the little volume. In one
16th century map the cartographer has
filled in the blank space of Terra Australis
by a white elephant, evidently foreseeing the
Territory. But none of these early guesses
about Australia is so unlike the real place as
the small map of Australia that forms part
of the decoration of the cover. This blotch
of black, surrounded by a bright yellow sea,
may also have a prophetic aspect, though the
designer might have remembered that Bass
and r finders proved that Tasmania was an
island, and that no bridge has been built
across Bsss Strait. However, the history is
admirably written, and with the aid of an
engaging and iliuminativ- style SfiQ.tt makes
our romantic history even more
Another Professor of History, G. C. Hen-
derson, of Adelaide University, publishes a
course of lectures recently delivered in S.A.
upon The British Navy at War (Hassell,
Adelaide; Is., proceeds for a patriotic fund).
The subject is rapidly but carefully covered
in 12 chapters. The author writes with
clarity and a comprehensive knowledge of
his subject, and though he brings no new in-
formation to bear, nor discusses naval
strategy in any detail, he admirably pictures
for his readers the composition and value of
the navy, the new developments arising
from Germany’s violation of the laws of
humanity, and the meaning and value of the
British control of the sea.
A third Professor, R. F. Irvine, who holds
the chair of Economics at Sydney Univer-
sity, advances a remarkable theory of the
meaning of money— The Veil of Money
(The Review, Sydney), a paper read
before the N.S.W. Actuarial Society. He
points out that modern banking is built
wholly on a principle which in the 17th and
18th centuries was stigmatised as dishonest
and illegal; and that modern bankers still
are mistaken in maintaining that all bank
currency is ultimately based on their hold-
ing of gold. “We have reached a stage in
the evolution of money, in which the greater
part of the money-work of the world is done
by devices which have little in common with
the commodity money with which we started.
.... Gold used as a medium of exchange is
only an alternative, used on rare and special
occasions, for credit.... Money to-day is
immaterial and almost ideal. It can be
created and cancelled in millions without
any risk or loss, and with a rapidity and
ease impossible to any material money....
The real substance of credit, and therefore of
the greater part of modern money, is not
gold, but simply the wealth of individuals
subject to legal claim. Ido not deny that
some portion of any particular monetary
system may be based on holdings of gold.
I admit that in certain cases gold is still
necessary as a medium of exchange, but
compared with the vast sum of exchange
transactions effected without the use of gold,
these cases become almost negligible. But I
do deny that gold can in any real sense be
regarded as the basis of modern money, and
I am sure that its use as currency will be
more and more restricted.” The development
of this theory, which the author asserts is
not novel to advanced students, is extremely
interesting, since R. F. Irvine discusses its
relation to the huge war loans now being
raised, their meaning and their bearing upon
the financial and material stability of the
warring nations, and analyses exactly what
happens when a man puts, say, £lOOO in the
Australian War Loan. The pamphlet cer-
tainly demands the attention of our finan-
ciers, bookies’ clerks and presidents of two
up schools. LWO '

~C f - L Beeston, who commanded
the 4th I leld Ambulance, A.1.F., tells of his
Five Months at Ansae (Angus and Robert
son; 2s. (id.). Necessarily he covers familiar
ground, but his comments are shrewd and
terse, and he fully appreciates the humor of
Billzac. One of the curious things that
struck him was a new sort of shelf. This
shell came over the Red Cross quarters and
it looked different, being seeminglv formed of
paper and black rag. A shell* had burst
among the mules near by and the resultant
hurtling mass was merely mule. Luckily it
did not explode.
There are interesting photographs.

A. B. Piddington collects his Spanish
Sketches (Oxford University Press; 55.)
which appeared some years ago in a Sydney
daily. The author writes charmingly of
Spanish and Moorish art and architecture,
while the photographic illustrations give the
volume a value of their own. Of Velasquez
he has much to say that is illuminative. The
wonderful architecture of Toledo, Cordova,
Seville and Granada is fully illustrated and
interpreted, while an essay upon Columbus
in Seville is full of matter. It is interesting to
recall, thanks to the author, that Columbus,
since his death, has hardly stopped travel-
ling. Columbus wished to be buried in Santo
Domingo. But he was buried at Valladolid,
then moved to the Chartreuse in Seville, then
to Santo Domingo, and when the French
took Ilayti the body was taken to Havana
Cathedral. Now he has come back to Seville.
As Piddington says : “What a voyager!”
(G. Innes, who forwards this fine example
of Billzac verse, states that it was composed
in a trench in France, not for publication
but for the eyes of the author’s parents. The
author is Corporal G. B. Crerar, Machine
Gun Section, 6th Infantry Brigade, A.1.F.)
WHAT BILLZAO READS.
John O. Cannon, somewhere in France: Your
opinion that the bulk of the reading matter is
unsuitable is correct. I have unpacked many cases
marked “Literature.” In the newspaper class the
publications most commonly found are: The Aus-
tralian Journal, The Family Herald., Tiie War
Cry. These appeal only to about 2 per cent, of the
fellows. There are also a few copies of The Bulle-
tin, The Referee and Australian dailies. As pack-
ages of this kind reach us some months after the
mail, copies received by post have rendered them
out of date. The novels are mostly English of the
pre-war era. Hocking, Garvice and Effie Adelaide
Somebody are the favorites of the senders. The
man who would read this stuff has read it avant la
guerre. The ordinary man thinks more here than
he did in civil life. He hasn’t a girl to take out or
a football match to watch. He sees a man blown
up and perhaps an identity disc buried to represent
him. This makes him think about the mystery of
death. I’ve lent my copy of Omar Khayyam to
men whom I didn’t expect to read it, but they have.
Extracts from Tennyson are also sought after. Aus-
tralian poetry -would be read by those who merely
glanced at it in civil life, and glanced at, at least,
by those who had not even heard of it before. I’ve
not met The Sentimental Bloke in a box of “litera-
ture” yet, but over 70 per cent, of our fellows have
read the one copy we possess. The chap who
received a copy of Ginger Mick has it promised to
over 20 already. Nobody has been guilty of book-
ing The War Cry in advance. If the kind
people who pack the eases of literature were
to send modern novels, Australian poetry and an
occasional volume of classical English, their choice
would be appreciated in addition to their kindness.
Fritz sends his love.
UP AND DOWN THE DUCK-BOARDS.
Up and down the duck-hoards, up and down
again,
Blinkin’ at the star-shells failin’ in the rain;
Thinkin’ of the rations, if they’re gettin’ wet;
Thinkin’ if there’s any rum, and how much
we will get;
Thinkin’ if a bullet hurts —if there’s any
pain—
Yowl Here comes a hleedin’ homb
Up and down again!
Up and down the duck-hoards, screwin’ at
the moon,
Musin’ on the bally strafe we got this after-
noon;
Thinkin’ how explosives make you jump and
shake and sweat j -
Thinkin’ how you duck and run and hug the
parapet;
Thinkin’ of the next one—-if it’s joy or
pain. ...
Hell, it’s gettin ’ hotter!
Duck, and off again!
Up and down the duck-hoards, good and had
and worn.
From “siand-to” in the evenin’, till “carry
on” at morn;
Thinkin’ all the bloomin’. things you never
thought before;
Thinkin’ of the stunt last night, and feelin’
pretty sore;
Thinkin' you’ll chuck thinkin’ up, before you
turn insane;
Two whizz-bangs, a nine-two-eight,
And then you think again.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Pinch-gut.
Amber and opal, jade and amethyst,
Mazed in a dancing mesh of living gold.
How well she wears her gems, this velvet-
stoled .
Barbaric harbor queen ! Bold brow and wrist
And bosom bound by many a blazing twist ;
The royal scarabs on her vestments scrolled
Flashing through veil and wimple’s shimmer-
ing fold
Like magian shuttles threading moonstone
mist.
And, set in many a coruscating querl,
In some strange spirit of sardonic jest.
A thing of hate and sweat and blood and
tears, . .
(Jrim deep-sea growth of dumb despairing
years, , , ,
The costliest jewel on Port Jackson s breast,
Old Pinch-gut darkles like a great black
pearl.
VICTORIA.
MARIE E. J. PITT.
All or Nothing.
Lean over the fence and kiss? Not I!
If the tide,leaped up to a kiss
The fence were a bar too low —
Or the kiss was a lie !
And a kiss that’s a lie is none
When everything’s said and done.
Lean over the fence and kiss?
Philander and play the fool?
Blood that is blood is hotter than this,
Or life were easy to school!
Lean over the fence and kiss? I? Never!
Scorn to your safe-to-lean-over!
Half-and-half lover!
Give me the kiss that is all,-
Or nothing, for ever.
N.S.W.
MARY GILMORE.
Love.
Love, I have sought thee long. Where is thy
evening?
In what remote recess, what palmy plot.
Sweet, to my eyelids the tear-founts aie
welling, .
Why dost thou banish me, why claim me
not?
Built I a palace for thine own abiding,
Fashioned it fragrantly with mine own
hands —■
Ah ! but a 'spectre I found therein hiding.
Strewing the pathways with colorless sands,
Haunting the heavens with sorrow compel-
ling,
Rapture and laughter in terror dispelling.
Wandered I forth through the valley of
morning,
Down where the dews of delight filled me
through,
Day on the hillocks her hair was adorning.
Binding her tresses with blossoms of blue.
There by the mellowy margins retiring
Seated with Youth, for thy coming I spied,
Tempe were mine in that sunny desiring,
Love, hadst thou lingered a while by my
side; ,
Love, hadst thou gathered me out ot the
morning,
Nought of my loving so splendidly scorning.
Into the chasm of night I am falling,
Love, on my eyes are the shadows of sleep!
When wilt thou answer my passionate call-
ing? „
When through my portal in sympathy creep r
Scented my hair for thy fingers’ caressing,
Blossomed my lips as a rose of the south ;
I am a slave for thy constant possessing,
Bread for thy body and wine for thy mouth.
Love, wilt thou vanish beyond my recalling/
Sip not a moment the sweetness enthralling i
Never, ah never, O love of my dreaming.
Earth gave a lover thy infinite charms—
Eyes with the sunlights of Paradise gleam-
ing.
Pressure of God in thy soft-curvfed arms.
Time, with my soul, as a priestess adoring,
Came to my chamber at close of the day.
Sat there and sang to me, comfort restoring,
Drying my tears with the lilt of her lay,
Singing that Love through the darkness is
beaming. ...
Come to me, Love, for I weary of dreaming.
N.S.W.
ZORA CROSS.
A Geographical Lyric.
This is the map of the world.
llow it tangles and blurs!
Mountains on mountain chains curled —■
And all of it ought to be hers!
Continents set for her feet.
Empires to cushion her head ;
And a jewel-bright lake, burnished blue for
her sake,
To bathe her white limbs should be spread.
A necklace of islands to wear,
With a coraline one
To set in the dark of her hair,
Aflash with a tropical sun.
Greenland to fan her cheek cool,
Etna for cold .winter days;
And each longitude line with its polar incline
To use as a busk for her stays.
Silk of green steppes for her dress;
And. to powder her nose,
Arabia’s spicy caress
Is dusty enough. I suppose.
Oh, geographical love!
Queen in your absolute right,
I can’t give you the lot; it’s too heavy and
hot —•
Take my heart as a part for to-night!
N'.S.'WV
PAT O’MAORI.

Dodge Brothers
MOTOR CAR
As improvements are made in the Dodge Brothers Car nothing
is said to the public about them.
This is in pursuance of a policy inaugurated by Dodge
Brothers at the very outset.
They look upon the progressive improvement of the car as
a matter of course.
The car, however, is basically the same as it was when first
built two years ago.
Yet there is not the slightest doubt but that it is a better car.
Standard Touring 1 Car ... £340
Standard Roadster £335
Dodge Brothers’ Cars will he on view at our pavilion at the
Royal Easter Show, to be held at Moore Park, Sydney, from
April 3 to 11.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £l 6s. 6d. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special' Christmas Number, to anY
address in Australasia, post paid.
Post free to any address abroad, £1 10s. pet-
annum.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
SOOO words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. Tiie Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical' drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MARCH 15, 1917.
THE CONSPIRACY’S APPEAL FOR
VOTES.
All the talk about Federal elections to “win
the war” and to “ensure the representation
of the Commonwealth at the Imperial Con-
ference” and the wild yells about the preserva-
tion of the Empire have no more meaning
than the cackle of kookaburras. The plain
question for the electors is: To whom shall
the administration of the public affairs of
Australia be entrusted—to the party rejected
at the last election, but now, in defiance
of the people, back at the old address, or the
party returned at that election, but now in
th. wilderness? Does Joe Cook Liberalism,
puts a discredited rump of billet-hunters.and
office-mongers, look any better now than it
did in 1914? Does Labor, minus the
Hughes and Pearce job-hunters, appear
any less entitled to the support of Austra-
lian men and women? On the answer to
either of these questiotfs the citizen with a
vote can get to the very crux of the problem
before the nation. By so doing he solves
for himself 7(342 irrelevant issues that will
be flung down to keep him in a state of con-
fusion. The Government, driven to the
country by the failure of its undemocratic
intrigues, is asking that into its hands shall
be placed at this hour of tremendous crisis
the custody and direction of a democratic
people’s future. In effect it is shrieking
that, since plot and wire-pull have not given
it the majority yielded by the ballot-box to
another Government, the public shall rush
up reinforcements and administer the boot
to everyone who objected to Australia being
dominated by a surreptitious fusion among
individuals who, during a riot, had got into
a place where, by bargaining one with the
other, they could exercise political power
without consulting the constituencies. And
Liberalism, which now. talks through the
mouth of Hughes, has the assurance to go
shouting up and down the country praises
of its own “patriotism.” and denunciations
of the “reign of terror” that is abroad in
tlie land because of secret juntas being at
work! Fine exhibitions of honest patriotism
indeed were given by the junta haggling in
the Cabinet room at Melbourne lately and
constructing a platform dependent on
successful rat-catching!
Except upon the dubious ground that
there was an opportunity to give Labor a
wallop, how could a vote for Hughes,
Cook and Pearce be justified by a demo-
cratic Australian? This paper knows of
none, and though it is profoundly conscious
•that during the last year or two Labor has
been gallivanting about with straws in its
hair, the fact that Hughes and Pearce
were to a- very considerable extent respon-
sible for that calamitous exhibition has an
important bearing on the claims these indi-
viduals are now making. They have
muddled everything to which they have
put their hand. Hughes stands to-day as
the recognised champion blatherskite of
the world; Pearce as a dull, commonplace
bluffer whose administration of a great
State Department makes a story of inept,
ignorant, wasteful bungling without parallel
in Australian history. Yet these men,
whom the people of the Commonwealth have
watched encouraging the Federal Parlia-
ment to pursue a life of sloth, while fur-
tively trying to set themselves up in a
ridiculous sort of dictatorship—fumbling
and bungling their obligations day in and
day out, and blithering inconsequential
phrases about nothing in particular—these
men are calling on the electors to pass a reso-
lution of confidence in them and grant them a
passport to go on wagging their tongues and
making hash of public affairs. Their
vanity is boundless, their faith in the gag
irremovable. The record of the Federal
Parliament since last election is a bad one.
Labor knows that well. The Liberals are
quite as well informed on the point. It is
a Parliament that Australia has reasons to
be ashamed of. It has been a shirker and a
waster; for most of the time the pliant,
time-serving instrument of evasion. For the
greater part of its life it has been in the hands
of Hughes and Pearce. Who can suppose
that a Liberal Parliament of to-morrow, con-
trolled by the same tortuous influence,
would differ from the Labor Parliament of
yesterday—that the hole-and-corner methods
of Hughes the Labor man would not be
followed by Hughes the colleague of Cook?
Australia cannot afford to set its seal upon
a Ministry born in such an environment as
this. To do so would be to put a premium
on all that miserable huckstering which
threatens to destroy the foundations of democ-
racy. Still less can it afford to be stam-
peded by noisy schemers, blaring on bugles
about their “loyalty,” into acceptance of any
Government in which such men as Hughes
and Pearce hold place. A Ministry made up
of men like Bigjoitn Forrest, Irvine and
Millen would be infinitely preferable, and
it is one of Australia’s momentary tragedies
that 99 per cent, of the people who custom-
arily vote against Labor know that this is
so —know that in their hearts they mistrust
Hughes now as they mistrusted him before.
And why should they not? What is his
claim on them? Simply this—that he is
shouting about it being the patriotic duty of
Australians to support his “war policy.”
Well, what is his war policy? The Bul-
letin yields place to no one in recognition
of the importance of this war to Australia.
The policy it advocated from the start was
the raising under Conscription of an army of
such strength as the country could afford to
despatch, having regard to the size of the
population, and its ability to foot the bill; a'
concentration on the industrial and financial
problems of peace ; and the abolition of that
reckless wastage of the public estate that
.Australia has endured so long. The policy of
Hughes was to scoff at all this —and talk.
He has been doing nothing but talk ever
since,, except when he was mysteriously
sneaking around trying to move juntas and
do by stealth what he had not the courage to
do openly. All the rest of the “war problem”
he let go hang, and shirk and waste have been
rampant. Such war policy as he acquired
he had damned in advance by making the
public pay for a censorship that kept it in a
state of ignorance about the war, by wagging
an irresponsible jaw in London and setting
the populace by the ears with demented sta-
tistics, and by ordering a “round-up” that
would have been about the last word in folly
if the vote-tarapering escapade did not cap
it. In support of that “war policy” Hughes
called upon Australia to stand behind him.
Having made a mess of it. he wildly asks to
be assisted again in a policy that is mere
wind, and puts forward that request as the
principal reason for being authorised to re-
main where he is. And Tearce, with his
administrative honors thick upon him, and
the newspaper offices bulging with his notifi-
cations and regulations, wants to go on
shuffling through life as before, mulishly' per-
sisting in being a stick-in-the-mud. If either
of them could be called upon by a Court to
show cause why they should not be dis-
missed, no answer that was free from frivol-
ity would be open to them.
Hughes has no policy except to talk, hang
on to his job and stretch his fingers from
his nose at Labor. He can only remain
where he is by serving the Liberal
interest. That can only be well and truly-
done by opposing all that Labor stands for.
And whatever its mistakes may have been —-
and' Heaven knows they have been tragic
enough ! —it is only through the Labor move-
ment in Commonwealth politics that Aus-
tralian ideals and ambitions will ever get a
chance of being realised. Once that is broken
Australia will have lost her moorings, per-
haps beyond recovery. There are articles in
the Labor faith, degraded though it may have
been by the baser influences of the passing
hour, that can only be even temporarily
abandoned at the risk of disaster. The foun-
dation of these is the conception of Australia
as a nation. This is a thing that Australia
cannot monkey with. Never in her history
was it more vital that this should be remem-
bered. Yet the Government now in office and
appealing for a, mandate to stay there cares
about as much for Australian nationalism as
for a shower of rain. Hughes, its head and
front, has gone quite mad on the subject of
an organic Imperialism, and would to-
morrow, if he could, pull the Commonwealth
into entanglements and commitments that
would change the whole face of her. political
life and upend the status of the Australian
citizen. It would be a calamity for Australia
if this man were by the vote of the people
encouraged to go on his impetuous way. lie
has misrepresented the Australian people
badlv enough already. If he is returned to
power, with the opportunity for evil greatly
increased, it will be fearful proof of the ex-
tent to which reactionary influences have
affected the public mind. This paper is under
no delusions about what Labor looks like;
but with all its faults and weaknesses it looks
a better and a cleaner thing for the Common-
wealth to trust just now than the coterie of
rejects and conspirators who have seized the
machinery and want to show their loyalty
bv remaining in charge.
AT THE ASSES’ BRIDGE.
Bi a policy of wrangle-and-bluff the British
Government hoped to avoid the necessity of
doing anything with regard to Irish affairs
until the war ended. This policy has now
come to a sudden smash through the blunt
refusal of Lloyd George to “coerce Ulster.”
The disgust of Redmond and his followers
is not difficult to understand. Home Rule
for Ireland is already British law, and but
for the war an Irish Parliament should now
be meeting in Dublin. Because there were
some .difficulties and minor dangers in the
way, accredited Irish leaders consented to
delay and found that in so consenting they
had yielded ground to their national enemies,
whose influence became greater and greater
in British affairs as the war dragged on,
while their own dwindled. Still, the fact
that the long-expected and liard-fought-for
measure was actually on the statute book
kept them hoping. It seemed scarcely pos-
sible that England, in the midst of a terrific
war for the liberties of small nations, could
be so dead to the logic of things that she
would continue to deny liberty to that small
nation which had suffered under her own
misrule. But the British logical faculty
is a very slender thing at most times, and
the last hope of it snapped when Lloyd
George made his flaccid statement in Par-
liament. Ireland should have Home Rule
at some date unspecified, but have it in a
form which must take away its utility and
significance. Then Redmond and his fol-
lowers, tricked and cheated in return for
their generous patience and moderation, rose
and left the House of Commons. Surely
Germany must have cheered at this spectacle
of the largest and most dangerous-looking
of British statesmen baulking at the old
asses’ bridge!
The alleged difficulty which British states-
men find in going on honorably with the
Home Rule programme, as missed by their
own Parliament, is the attitude of a small
minority of people in the Province of Ulster.
The fact that this attitude had been deliber-
ately fostered by Englishmen and that it
was used by an English political party for
purposes that had no relation to Ireland or
Irish affairs is steadily ignored. The
menace is magnified out of all proportion to
its real importance, and the will of Ireland,
Scotland, Wales and half England, as ex-
pressed through their Parliamentary repre-
sentatives, is set aside because of the noise
made in a few parishes of eastern Ulster.
Here, of course, the rights of minorities and
small nations is dragged in and an attempt,
is made to show that the coercion of Ulster
by the rest of Ireland differs in no degree
from the coercion of Ireland as a’whole by
England. The parallel, however, breaks
down the moment it is closely examined.
The faction in Ulster opposed to Home Rule
is not a nation. It is not even a majority
of the people of a province. In the towns
of Belfast and Londonderry, where the anti-
Ilome Rule sentiment is strongest, there are
enough Nationalists to return members of
Parliament; arid throughout the counties of
Down and Antrim, where on the twelfth of
July the Orangemen get drunkest to the
"pious, glorious and immortal memory” of
William 111., neighboring families of the
same race and owning similar race-names
arc on opposite sides of the controversy. Nor
is that controversy in any real sense reli-
gious. While there are few Roman Catho-
lics opposed to Home Rule there are any
number of Presbyterians in favor of it; and
Presbyterian Scotland, looking soberly
across the narrow strait of Moyle and observ-
ing the antics of its fellow-religionists, has
steadily voted with the majority of Irish-
Catholies and against the faction which pre-
tends to be' in such danger of oppression
if the liberty of Ireland is granted. The
Ulster followers of Edward Carson are in
point of fact merely a political party and
cannot claim to represent a distinct nation-
ality or religion. Further than this, a care-
ful study of race movements has gone to
show that the people inhabiting Ireland are
tolerably uniform in texture throughout.
The basis is everywhere Celtic and the
foreign elements in Ulster are not greater
than those in Leinster, possibly not greater
even than those in the extreme South.' But
even if it could be shown that some of the
people of Antrim, Down, Armagh and Lon-
donderry were foreigners of comparatively
recent arrival, it would be a poor doctrine
that an otherwise united nation must have
a Constitution dictated by a small foreign
minority with a loud voice. The whole ques-
tion narrows itself down to the finest
point. If the CARSON-ite claim is to be
allowed, it means that minorities every-
where have the right to destroy majorities.
It means that in a country like Australia
all legislation must be suspended until there
is unanimity of opinion, and that as long
as a minority is opposed to a measure noth-
ing can be done about it. To merely state
such a case is to show the futility of the
whole anti-Home Rule position. But, apart
from the logical implications of the course
Lloyd George and his Ministry are trying
to follow, there is always in war time the
other question of expediency. The Carson-
ite faction is dangerous —we know this be-
cause it bought German rifles to oppose Eng-
lish law; but does any sane man suppose
that the few armed drum-beaters in Ulster
could be as dangerous as the whole people
of Ireland roused to desperation by lying,
trickery and base ingratitude? The British
Government in bowing supinely to an in-
furiated tomcat is in danger, of rousing a
full-clawed tiger.
Much has been heard lately of the assist-
ance which British overseas opinion can
render to England in this time of stress;
and here is one of those matters in which
the Dominions may very well help. At a
distance we have much better perspective
and can see the Irish problem in its proper
proportions. That is the chief reason why
the opinion of Australia, South Africa,
Maoriland and Canada is so overwhelmingly
in favor of granting plain Home Rule with-
out trick or reservation to the Irish people.
England, from our point of view, has failed
lamentably in the government of Ireland.
England is in danger of committing suicide
through following her old insane methods
with regard to that country. Let the legis-
latures of States and Commonwealth make a
plain pronouncement on the question, not with
a view to increasing England's difficulties, but
with the clear, patriotic object of prevent-
ing a division in the face of the enemy.
Australians have shed their blood to assist
Britain to remain as a bulwark of civilisa-
tion and liberty. If Britain in her dealings
with her nearest dependency is still foolish,
unjust and insincere, she is not the Britain
Australians believed she was. That is the
gist of a great matter ; and that word ought
to be spoken in English ears with blunt
vigor. '
THE PRESS, THE PRIME MINISTER
AND THE GAG.
[The Second Charter of this Article
Will be Printed when the Present Cen-
sorship has keen Abolished.]
In nn attack wliic’.i tlie Prime Minister was
good enough to make on The Bulletin in
the House of Reps, last week he referred to
a “venomous article” about himself which
might have been suppressed by the Censor-
ship, but which, on it being shown to him
by the Censor, he allowed to be published.
The incident to which the Prime Minister
alludes serves very well to show how the
Censorship has operated in Australia. The
“venomous article” was printed in September
last, when Mr. Hughes, after talking so
valiantly in England, had returned to make
the fatal error of appealing to the individual
citizen on the question of Conscription.
The article was an expression of opinion
regarding the claims of Mr. Hughes to
statesmanship. Because The Bulletin
believes Mr. Hughes to be a grave national
danger, the article was not flattering. But
it committed no breach of any instruction
issued by tlie Censorship. It was there-
fore passed by the Censor. Before it was
published, however, an instruction was issued
which covered such an article. Then Mr.
Hughes saw the article. Mr. Hughes did
not like it. It is not too much to say
that he was furious about it. But it
could only have been suppressed after it had
been passed for publication. As a matter of
fact, it was published. But it was only
published because Mr. Hughes was in-
formed that, the Censor having passed it,
there would be no Bulletin that week with-
out it. # #
The Bulletin is not permitted to say
what the instruction given to the press on
that occasion was. It would, if it were
published, afford the public an interesting
view of the character of Mr. Hughes. It
was, however, not the only instruction of the
same nature issued at or about the same
time. Mr. Hughes says lightly that there
was some small protest by the newspapers
that he met their representatives in Mel-
bourne and Sydney and told them that they
were perfectly free to print what political
criticisms they pleased. There was nothing
so light and airy as that about the matter.
The political censorship was only withdrawn
after a very drastic measure had been agreed
to by the leaders of both political parties in
both Houses in X. S. Wales. The Prime
Minister did meet the newspapers—in Syd-
ney. at any rate —and tell them they were
free to publish political criticisms. But
Mr. Hughes allowed free speech and a free
press—moderately free, at any rate —not
because he wanted to, but because, to his
everlasting credit, William Holman, sup-
ported by other party leaders in N. S. W ales,
drove him to it.
* * * *
So the outrageous restrictions were with-
drawn. Then arose the matter which was dis-
cussed in the House of Reps, last- week. The
Bulletin printed a paragraph commenting
upon the administration of the Defence De-
partment in regard to a certain matter very
intimately connected with the rights of the
widow of a soldier killed at the Front. If
it is not the duty of the press to endeavor
to secure justice for the widows of the men
who have fallen in pur fight, then the press
has no duties at all,' and no justification for
existence. The Bulletin was informed
that that effort to assist the soldier’s widow
was an offence, a breach of the War Precau-
tions regulations, and that such paragraphs
should be submitted for censorship. It re-
plied, as the proceedings in Parliament show,
that, by agreement with the Prime Minister,
such matter need not be submitted.
Though the press and public speakers
have not yet been informed specifically as to
the regulations which are to operate, it is
at least promised that they shall be “as free
. to speak about political matters as ever they
had been.” This further undertaking should,
however, be demanded: that no politician
shall interfere with the Censorship during
the campaign. Further, unless more satis-
factory assurances are given, anything what-
ever that may be written in regard to the
administration of the Defence Department
a matter on which the election must turn, to
a great extent —can be suppressed as “pre-
judicial to recruiting,” and anything that a
speaker may say on the same class of sub-
jects is liable to bring a prosecution upon him.
If Pearce is to take shelter in that dugout
against the criticism of his administration
it will be a scandalous misuse of the Censor-
ship. If there were the smallest desire on
the part of the influential press of Australia
to prejudice recruiting there might be some
excuse for using this gag. But, in the case
of The Bulletin, for instance, almost at


the very moment when the Department of
Defence was accusing it of doing things to
prejudice recruiting, the Director-General
of Recruiting was—quite unnecessarily
thanking it for its “valuable assistance’ .
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Australia’s Lost Interest in the War.
There are two reasons why recruiting in
Australia is so slow. The first is that every
pintpot has a bottom; the other that Aus-
tralia has lost interest in the war. We can t
get more than a pint out of a pintpot, so the
tirst reason may not he got rid of; yet there
is still enough left in the pot to make it well
worth while considering whether the second
reason is here to stay. Australia has lost
interest in the war —why? If we know that
we may remove the cause. While the Gal-
lipoli campaign was in progress Australian in-
terest simply boiled. The news of the trouble
which our men struck at and after the Land-
ing brought a rush of recruits that seems
positively incredible in the light of recent
tardy enlistments. The whole explanation
of it was that at that time Australians were
not submerged. They were doing something
or trying to do something on their own
account; and their national individuality
was preserved. The war then was to Aus-
tralians a personal matter. To-day it is
quite impersonal. And simply because those
who are in charge of affairs don’t know the
Australian, there has been no attempt to
make it anything else. Something might
easily have been done in Egypt, where the
Desert work was essentially Australian
work, and the glory achieved by the mounted
troops is Australia’s glory. But there was
no official Australian correspondent to stil-
us as Ashmeap Bartlett stirred us in the
days of Gallipoli; and the magnificent Aus-
tralian exploits in the Sinai Peninsula have
been worth nothing to the recruiters. It is
doubtless more difficult to distinguish the
performances of the Australians in France.
But it is by no means impossible. Yet prac-
tically nothing has been done or attempted.
We have had reams of recruiting literature
from the Back; hardly anything with an
appeal in it from.the Front. The communi-
cations of the official correspondent have
been colorless, and no waverer’s pulse has
been quickened by them. The Bulletin
does not believe that press-agency work
should be necessary in order to raise a
nation’s army. We ought to have national
service, as Britain has. But as, through the
blundering of politicians, we have not got it,
we must adopt just the same methods to get
men into the army that we adopt to get them
to do other things. The British authorities
ought to recognise this, and, while giving
away no military secrets, allow a much
greater latitude in regard to the reporting of
Australian achievements. The press is eager
to help ; it has never been allowed to do one-
tenth of what it could do. No doubt this is
largely the fault of people who have pro-
fessed to speak for Australia. They have
talked so much about Imperialism and unity
that the British authorities naturally believe
that we are well enough fed if the doings of
the British army are put on our breakfast-
plates. Nothing could be further from the
truth. We may be proud in a general,
detached sort of way of British achieve-
ments ; but Young Australia reads of them
With an emotion not to be compared with the
feeling that gripped it when the news of
Gallipoli was coming through. To-day every
Australian recruit is, one way and another,
costing this country £5O to get: and the
number we do get is almost humiliating. It
is time we tried a new plan. The services of
two outstanding men in the war-correspon-
dent business should be secured. They can
pass with a few words what the rest are
doing; Australia wants to know what the
Australians are doing, not. only in France,
but in England, too. And until it does know,
the right atmosphere for the Australian
recruiter to work in will not be created.
The Horrible Story in the Ledger.
After having a look at the Federal cashbox
and the future, Treasurer Bigjohn Forrest
the other day went along to the Reps, and
explained what he saw. It was mostly a
yarn about bigger debt and higher charges
and greater needs. If in the coming year
revenue and expenditure were on the current
year’s basis the Treasury reckons that the
income would be £2,634,000 short. But
expenditure will lie so greatly increased that
another £6,800,000 must be got somewhere.
Thus it will be necessary to get a grip on
£9,434,000 more than is now being handled
in order to pay interest and war pensions
and so on ; which means that a lot of Aus-
tralians are going to be stung on a sore
place. Moreover, it is a sure thing that,
not long after, they will have to be badly
bitten again. But Btg.tohn was content
with giving a general statement of the facts,
remarking that the manner in which so
much money could be raised was “undesir-
able to indicate at the present time.” Where-
upon :
Fenton (Vic.): Will you deal with it in your
election manifesto?
Bigjohn: Certainly not. That remark shows you
have no sense of responsibility. Did you ever hear
of a Minister telling Parliament how he was going
to raise funds before raising them? The hon. gentle-
man will cease to have my regard if he asks
questions like that.
Yet before the election is over John
Forrest will find that this question of how
the Government proposes to make ends meet
will have to. be answered with some atten-
tion to detail. It is one of the most im-
portant questions of the hour, and upon it
public attention should be closely fixed.
Taxation is heavy now. That it must be
heavier than the most dismal pessimist ever
dreamed of is certain. Therefore it is not
good enough to allow political parties to
take refuge behind a cloud of words about
increased production and an influx of popu-
lation and all the rest of it. The Hughes
Government promises a policy of “sound
finance.” Very well, let us have it. If the
people who are to provide these additional
millions are not entitled to know something
about it and have a voice in saying how the
burden is to be distributed, who is?
General Muddle Reports Progress.
The only possible result of publishing the
findings of the Gallipoli Commission at this
moment is to start an acrimonious discus-
sion about matters having nothing to do with
the job on hand. Everybody knew already
that there had been muddle and that all the
gorgeous yarns the public had been fed on
during the earlier fighting were calculated
perjuries; hence the production of corrobora-
tive testimony on the subject was a waste of
time. Still, there it is now for the Dachshunds
to bark over. One of the main questions
was whether CRurchill and Asquith
ordered the naval operations in defiance of
the expressed opinion of their naval a^vis ® l ' s ‘
That they did so appears clear. lhe
admirals were against it. That they dul not
protest at the actual meeting of the Nvar
Council which decided on the attack, after
having already put their views before that
body, matters nothing. The War Council
was responsible for the undertaking: the
admirals for carrying out any contract the
Council decided on. There may have been
important political considerations to which
the Ministers were compelled to give
greater weight than to the opinions of the
Admiralty. That is a side of the matter
on which light has not been shed ; and until
it is, the just distribution of individual blame
remains very difficult. In any case, it occurs
to this paper that there are more weighty
things to think about than what was done
a couple of years ago by two discarded poli-
ticians, a dead soldier and a retired sailor.
Between them all they made a dreadful
mess; but going back to contemplate the
mess is about as helpful as searching for
yesterday morning through the wrong end of
a telescope.

A Parliamentary Election About a Fiji
Uncle.
Tiie strategy of the P.L.L. in the State
election riot now raging in N. S. .Wales is
of the elementary kind invented by the
general-staff serving under the famous
Chinese Emperor Wot 110 XXX. Wot Uo
about 74G2 b.c., as near as this paper can
fix it, was attacked by a rebellious horde num-
bering 147 generals and four privates. They
came from the desert in search of the pre-
served-ginger department. Wot had no
experience of war, but his general staff was
wise with the wisdom of years. “Let us,”
they said, “yell that the marauders have
measles. Then will our friends be alarmed
and throw the mud of the Ho-hang-ho at
them and chase them home.” It all hap-
pened according to schedule, and for thou-
sands of years afterwards the successors of
Wot 110 armed their troops with a yell and
thus saved the Royal ginger. That is just
what is happening now in N. S. Wales,
with this difference —that, instead of yelp-
ing that the Liberalites and the llolman-
ites want to give all of us the measles, the
P.L.L. says that these people are going to
present the State with a compulsory mili-
tary-service law. This is the kind of muni-
tions the P.L.L. is fighting a general election
with ! Never was a contest hejd upon an issue
so meaningless. Does Labor seriously ask
the electors of the State to measure its own
fitness for office by the other party’s alleged
desire to do something it can’t do and
declares it has no intention whatever of
attempting? The State Government has no
more to do with military service, voluntary
or otherwise, than with Holman’s missing
whiskers. The P.L.L. knows this. The kids
in the streets know it. Has the Labor party
no merits of its own that it must rely on
the fictitious demerits of its opponents? Has
it gone daft or does it think that the people
have? It must be one of the two.
♦
The Censorship anil a Minister Who
Does Not Know the Facts.
This paper has no desire to tell Assistant
Minister for Defence Groom that he is
untruthful. But it does want to tell him
that unless he makes himself better
acquainted with the performances of Hughes
and Tearce he will certainly earn for him-
self the reputation enjoyed by the late
lamented Bill Adams, of Waterloo. In the
House of Reps, last week, in an “explana-
tion” of the censorship, he said: —
The whole system was controlled by the Imperial
authorities , being a branch of the Imperial staff.
It was divided into three branches, and, as regards
the press censorship, that was regulated by instruc-
tions issued by the chief censor in London in con-
junction with instructions issued by the naval and
military authorities of the Commonwealth.
The plain intention of this was to convey
the idea that the Censorship operating in
Australia has the sanction of the Imperial
authorities as a measure of protection com-
mon to England .and the Commonwealth. It
does not matter who made the regulations. It
is the things that are done under them that
form the scandal. Of course it may be said that
this is mere assertion. Groom may oven
-choose to say that it is untrue. If lie docs
the obligation will be strong upon him to
give this paper an opportunity to prove its
case. It will undertake to do so to the
satisfaction of every impartial citizen in
the Commonwealth. The Bulletin is pre-
pared to prove a few other things; but the
Censorship won’t let it. That, of course, is
why there is a Censorship—to help win the
war.

The Freetratle Policy of Let-It-Slide.
V person named Keans consumes half a
column of the Melbourne Argus to _ groan
about anyone objecting to Australia’s tre-
mendous importations and to incidentally
explain that, “whatever the importance of
the figures,” “the fact that has struck me
with the greatest force is that every inci-
dent connected directly or indirectly with
the struggle is made 41 peg to hang a Pro-
tectionist argument on.” The fact that
strikes this paper with the greatest force is
that if Australia continues this demented
importation of goods that are paid, for fiy
additions to the public debt, Keans will
soon be so busy snaring sparrows for break-
fast that he will have no time to write to
the Argus, except to point out that taxation
swallows up 19s. Gd. in the fl of his in-
come. The questions involved in the con-
sideration of Freetrade or Protection are
not relevant to the position Australia has
to face. The inescapable fact is that the
people can only dodge ruin by mending the
national ways. What we have now is a form
of Protection made wholly inadequate by the
borrowing of millions a year. This gives the
country all the disadvantages of Freetrade
plus a tariff and a vast charge for debt.
It is not a fiscal or any other sort of
policy. It is just plain supping with the
Devil. To gird at Protectionists for not post-
poning their efforts until after the war _ is
about as intelligent as it would be to advise
the French army to go back to work until
the war is over. What the Protectionists
are talking about is part of the war. The
financial and industrial stability of a nation
is vital to its existence and can no more be
huddled away out of sight until war is over
than warships can be sunk for purposes of
safety. To take the advice of Keans would
be to run the risk of waking up. when the
war is over, to discover that the premises
have fallen down in a heap. And-, anyhow,
can Keans or Beans or anyone else detect
an “incident connected directly or indirectly
with the struggle” which implies that Aus-
tralians have no need to live by their own
labor and enterprise?
Repatriation and Apples.
“Jacko” on the problem of making “the
land” pay:—
Here are the facts about the Bathurst effort at
“repatriation.” Recently the N.S.W. Lands Depart-
ment resumed one of the district's best-known orchard
properties for just on £SOOO, the account being fixed
up something like this: —
105 acres (about) of varied land ( £l4
an acre).. .. •• •• ..£2300
75 acres (about) of fruit trees ( £6l
10s. an acre) .. .. • • • • 4612
Various improvements .. .. • • <,50
Total .. •• •• ..£7007
Of the 105 acres 00 are described by the Land Board
which handled the’ matter as “bush land.” Since
the purchase 10 more acres (by the way, the next-
best orchard site in the whole locality) have been
taken from another owner at £l3 10s. per acre for
soldiers’ home sites. Add the cost of the horses and
other live stock about the place, the plant and the
new material sent along, and the total doesn’t leave
much of £IO,OOO. The settlement is to be divided
into 15 holdings for as many soldiers ami their
families. Each is to have a home costing £4OO,
which tacks on another £6OOO. So that, when the
settlement has actually been established, the figure
will easily have touched £15,000 —£1000 a man.
The show is to be worked on the collective basis,
and for the first six months the men are to get
their nine 'bob a day. Thereafter they will be
expected to rub along on the profits.
When the Land Board sifted the thing it came to
the conclusion that, “so far as the orchard is con-
cerned, a net return may be looked for, with a con-
tinuance of the same diligent energy and working
as at present, of £25 an acre.” Overlooking the
fact that there may and maj' not be this same “dili-
gent energy,” and the possibility that the original
owner himself—a shrewd expert—never got £25
an acre, we find that, even if this maximum of
optimism is realised, it will mean a profit of. only
about £3B weekly to be cut up among the 15
families. The return from the remaining ground—
mostly “bush land”—isn’t likely to affect the pro-
position profoundly. The prospective £25 an acre
is worked out by the Department on the basis of a
return of about 20,000 bushels from the orchard.
The truth is that this year’s yield, with the orchard
in its prime and the season generally good, won’t
go over 5000 bushels. Only 3000 cases have been
ordered by the new settlers. Which brings the
profits to minus something when the £64 fort-
nightly expenses are deducted.
What will happen when the thrip and frost and
hail and moth and other little pleasantnesses come
along only Ashfokd, the Minister for Lands, knows.
Even if, at any time, the soldiers themselves rake
out a crust, what about the repayment of the money
advanced for plant and material, depreciation, and
the interest on the whole £15,000? The Depart-
ment will learn then —as usual, at the public ex-
pense —that under present conditions the fruit-grow-
ing business is too precarious to fiddle with under
the soldier-settlement head. Better to put the cash
jnto wheat land, where the figures might keep
within reason. Then we might get back a few pence
of the millions it is going to cost us.
Our Airy Future.
The rush of Australians to get into the local
flying-schools looks like good business. Of
all the machines of war which the European
struggle has developed, the aeroplane is
easily first; and it gives promise of still
bigger things in the years to come. No
country in the world has more need of the
flying-machine than Australia; and no coun-
try seems better adapted to its use. The
huge extent of comparatively level country
which makes up the bulk of this continent
should offer the very best of air conditions;
and the advantages of the improved aero-
plane as a means of rapid communication
between isolated and distant settlements are
pretty obvious. Where the camel carries the
load at the goading of the Afghan the aero-
plane may carry the mail and the stray
passenger, and thus draw Australian homes
closer together and make the continent more
compact in sympathy and sentiment. But-
it is as a weapon in the Commonwealth’s
defence armory that the aeroplane really
counts. For many years to come the pro-
blem of our defence authorities —if they
grasp the fact that there is any problem at
all —must be to devise a scheme by which a
very small number of soldiers can hold their
own against an immense invading horde in
an almost limitless country. Clearly, the
trench lines of Europe offer no hint of the
way in which Australia could be defended.
Our whole available manhood could not hold
50 miles of trenches of the French or Bel-
gian sort; and the length of our Front might
run into thousands of miles. It is here that
the aeroplane comes in. One man in an
aeroplane seems already to be as useful as
a dozen or two on the ground ; and as fur-
ther improvements are made in the machine
the proportional value of the flier is likely to
be increased. More than this, the nature of
our back-country settlement lends itself to
the formation of aeroplane corps. Even a
lonely back station could have its machine
or two, and the remote township with its
pub, store and blacksmith’s shop might be
able to raise a covey of mechanical birds
armed to tire tail' with weapons of defence.
To train flying soldiers appears more im-
portant to-day than anything . else in the
military way; and, if Australia takes, the
business up in earnest, her insular position
must always give her the advantage against
any possible invader. The enemy’s planes
must rise from the water; we have a solid
place to jump off.

• Tleliogabalus ” about a row up North :
If you love a good old sectarian scrap, hurry up
to Queensland. It all conies out of Jack Fihelly’s
speech to the Brisbane Hibernians last September,
when the Coadjutor-Minister for Justice (as he is
called now) told his audience that England was “the
home of cant, humbug and hypocrisy.” The speech,
which incidentally was highly approved by Lennon
(Minister for Agriculture), was printed in the local
H.O. rag, after John had edited-it. Brisbane Daily
Mail, at the request of an influential advertiser,
reprinted it. Then there was the devil to pay. The
papers seethed with letters denouncing Fiiielly ;
the Mail and the Courier fulminated; Parliament
was moved, and even the Governor was implored to
exercise his latent power and remove John. In the
Assembly John tendered an “apology,” which merely
added fuel to the fire. The Ryan majority exon-
erated him and Lennon ; but the Council, led by
Fowlks, passed a condemnatory resolution, which
was duly presented to the Governor. The country
then took up the matter. At Toowoomba 500
Hibernians cheered Dr. Duma, the R.C. Archbishop
of Brisbane, who defended the Fiiielly outburst.
The trouble embittered the Conscription campaign,
and since then it has emerged from its lair twice a
week regularly. Every, day some council or semi-
public body passes indignant protests and resolu-
tions, which arc duly sent to the Governor or Parlia-
ment or Tom Ryan, or all three. Just when things
had begun to cool off, Dr. Mannix came along to
Brisbane and repeated his statement about the war
being merely a war for trade. This set the Mail
going again; and after Fiiielly's pal McCawley
had been made Judge of the Industrial Court, the
Anglican Church took up an openly hostile attitude
towards the Government and the R.C. hierarchy,
who, it alleged, were hand-in-glove to “stuff” the
public service with “green” protegees. Bishop Le
Fanu, Coadjutor to Archbishop Donaldson, opened
file; Archbish. Duma replied; then Donaldson
retorted; and at latest two archbishops were
reloading their blunderbusses, and the accompanying
row sounds like the finest dog-fight that ever was.
“Murkati” : While P.M.G. Webster is tin-
kering with the holes through which that
£315,000 leaked, he" might with advantage
east an eye on the telegraph accounts section.
Some time ago I took a job as temporary
assistant in a suburban office. During the
process of taking-over from the man I was
displacing I noticed a number of red forms,
stating: “Amount due on collect telegram
| here followed particulars of message] not
received. Please attach stamps covering
amount to this form and return.” I re-
marked on them, and was informed that they
didn’t matter, as “the telegraph branch sel-
dom followed them up.” He spoke no less
than the truth. They didn’t matter. The
messengers, taking advantage of this slack-
ness, frequently collect the money and do the
picture-shows on the proceeds. Nothing was
done about it in the four months I remained
at that office. That is 17 months ago, and
from inquiries I made when I visited the
place the other day I believe the same slip-
shod system is still in force.

Tiie old ghosts of Sydney sat on the hills
last week and grinned when four of the
Maltese immigrants interned on the hulk
Anglian swam ashore and scrambled away
through the bush with police panting in pur-
suit. Two got away, and will, no doubt, take
the picks and shovels out of the mouths of
two honest Australians who don’t give a
d—n how many foreign products come in so
long as the makers don’t arrive in person.
After the aliens’ break, a police launch
fussed up and began guard-duty. It wouldn’t
be so farcical if we had a tariff to match the
Alien Immigration Restriction Act. But,
while we are repelling these few chance
boarders, the products of millions of people
who get about fourpence a day are pouring
in almost without hindrance. As a matter
of fact, it is far better, from the industrial
point of view, to let the alien in than to let
his goods in. When the alien arrives we can
at least impose our industrial laws on him,
and his competition isn’t therefore as deadly
as it is when he works in his own country,
where he can toil unlimited hours and under
any conditions he pleases.

From an old and saddened Protectionist -
Australia continues to talk about its great natural
resources and its intention to profit by the lessons
of the war ; but thus far there is little or no evidence
of new enterprise in local manufacture. The beer
which should be as light and uninebriating as the
lightest of German lagers has yet to be brewed i>(
these latitudes. The good Australian woollen serges,
in plain colors, which ought to have a monopoly of
the steady local market for such cloths, are not
being woven. The best makes of Australian boots
are not being branded in competition with the boots
that come from abroad and carry the makers’ names
as advts. The hat-millers are not straining every
nerve, or any nerve, to turn out hats equal in every
respect, or in almost every respect, to the best of
the imported goods. Seemingly the manufacturers
in all the biggest branches of industry—softgoods
or hardware—are just making all the money they
can instead of speculating their profits in experiments
with a view to the time when the competition from
foreign goods will be keener than it is these times. *
The same want of enterprise is glaringly apparent in
retail trading. Among the purchasing public there
is a persistent demand for the ousting of the Dagors
from their strongholds; but there is no systematic
Australian endeavor to meet the demand. Big busi-
ness chances are offering, but there are no big
bidders.

Bagdad and Other Things.
In the campaign against the Turk the cap-
ture of Bagdad by General Maude’s army is
a cheerful incident, mostly because it is goad
evidence that the muddle associated with
the Mesopotamian expedition has been cleaned
up. The next thing to hear about is whether
the advancing force can maintain its long
line of communication with the sea. anil
whether a junction can be made with tin*
Russians in the north and east. Meantime
the cock-a-whoop yarn that the fall of Bag-
dad “shatters the German dream” may safely
be put aside for verification later on. Just
now we needn’t bother about Prussian
dreams; there is something far more
material to engage all our attention
so long as German armies are in
the field and German submarines are out
in the wet. These two things represent the
problems that have to be dealt with, and
until they are accounted for the significance
of Bagdad may well he left in abeyance. If
the submarine campaign succeeds, the war in
Mesopotamia won’t matter. So also if the
German armies are not broken in France,
Germany will he boss of Europe and the
dreams of other people will evaporate in
spite of Bagdad. As for the submarine,
it is better to listen to the voice of
responsible authority rather than to state-
ments made by the same stupid old crowd
that continues to lead us on to destruction
by burbling to ns that all is well no matter
what the truth may be. Lloyd George and
Carson spoke upon the submarine subject
last week. Both emphasised the gravity of
the peril; both declared that the problem
had not yet been solved. In the face of
this, with Carson’s statement that half a
million tons of shipping had been sunk in
February. The Bulletin has seen three
times during the week the definite assertion,
made by alleged cable messages published in
Australian newspapers, that the submarine
campaign has broken down —been defeated —
proved a farce ! The best that can be said
is that there is room for faith and hope. But
there is need to regard the situation with
profound seriousness; and until it does so
regard it. Australia will not realise its great
responsibilities in connection with the war.
The presence of a raider as far afield as the
Indian Ocean was heard of the other day
after part of the news had been laboriously
dragged out of the Ministry; hut as the
facts relating to the vessel are kept shrouded
in gloom in Australia though apparently
published in London, it is only a fair thing
to assume that the shattering of Australia’s
foolish dreams is no part of a little man’s
method of winning the war.
Miss Beatrice Grimsiiaw whoops up her
Papuan home: —
The too-fortunate agriculturists, who have been so
often abused for not knowing their own luck, know
it quite well in Papua in these days of food scarcity.
Nobody lives as well as the planter now that every-
thing has gone up in price. If he does not kill his
own cattle (and some do) he can. keep himself sup-
plied with wild pig, than which there is no better
meat, wallaby (as different from the coarse Austra-
lian wallaby as lamb is from old goat), and plenty
of eatable birds, all to be got by a shooting boy at
a pound a month. Sweet potatoes, planted by the
easy method of roughly hilling the ground and stick-
ing in bunches of tops, will produce from two to
10 tons an acre, starting in three months from plant-
ing. Maize comes to the eating stage in two and a
half months, and goes on for ever, or until you get
tired of putting in one crop after another. Pawpaw
fruits in eight or nine months from seed ; all tropic
fruits grow rapidly and well, and most temperate
veegtables can he raised. Fowls pick up their own
living in the teeming bush, and increase like flies.
Coffee robusta bears in two and a half years, and
if you have once drunk real coffee you will never
drink anything but tea out of the tropics again,
for all bought coffee is a mockery. Native sago, a
most; delicate and nourishing foojl, making the only
jolly that will set in hot countries withou’t ice, can
be cut and washed by the hundredweight in the near-
est swamp. The rivers teem with fine fish, and there
is a swift and safe way of getting them ; hut as it
is against the law, one need hardly say that it is
never, under any circumstances, practised! They
are beginning to grow rice now, and it does exceed-
ingly well, especially the hill rice, which can be
grown on dry land. Yes, you might be in worse
places than Papua in this hungry stage of the
world’s history.
The pop-shop gets a stronger grip than
the drink habit or the drug mania. Here is
Billson, the leader of Labor in Victoria,
castigating the Peacock Government for
cutting down public works “while money
could he obtained to carry them out.” And
when Bowser’s Economy party voted to
force Peacock to cut down his estimates,
Billson and co. would have none of its pro-
posals, because the policy would mean un-
employment. There is only one way by
which the present—and still more the com-
ing—situation can he met. That is by enor-
mously increasing production. One means
is by a Protective tariff, which will force
Australians to make most of their own
goods. Another is by cutting out the horde
of parasites and puttijig them to work. This
means that certain strenuous toilers, such as
State Governors, many State politicians,
stacks of messengers, attendants, agents and
what not, would have to find other, jobs.
Every unproductive citizen is a drain on
the community, out of whose pockets his
wages or salary must come; and the inter-
est' bill on our borrowings is going to be
so high directly that there will he precious
little left in those pockets for the owner
after the Government’s little lot has been
taken.
From N. S. Wales Worker (March 1) :
Ought wc to submit with patience to be robbed?
When burglars are ih the bouse of the Moderate
Man, does he move slowly? Or does be reach out

No, editor Boote is not at last getting a

To encourage wheat-growing an inter-State
conference is to he recommended to guaran-
tee. on behalf of the Governments, 4s. per
bushel for next season's crop. There may be
special circumstances to excuse a guarantee
just now; but the principle is rotten. What
the farmer needs is, not a guarantee, but to
he saved from sharks. They are all sorts
and sizes. There is the land-grabber who
uses great arable areas for grazing and
pushes the cultivator out on to the Edge,
where rain is uncertain and freights are
deadly. There is the politician who borrows
and bloats, creating an artificial prosperity
and an artificial level of wages, thus also
pushing up the farmer’s cost of living though
it can’t push up the price of the farmer’s
wheat, because that is governed by the
world’s markets. And there is the horde of
agents who fatten on the farmer; though
that is the farmers’ own fault, largely,
because he will not co-operate. Is there no
Department of Agriculture in Australia
sufficiently free from city agents’ influences
to do the best thing it could possibly do for
the farmers and promote co-operation among
them?
Hugh Percy Murray-Aynsely, one of the
host-known figures in Canterbury (M.L.),
has just had a funeral. He left his native
Gloucestershire in 1858, when lie was going
on for 30, and came this way with the
Queen, the boat that was brought out to
open steam trade between Australia and
Maoriland. He did woolbroking and sheep-
growing for a time, and was one of the
main spirits in the N.Z. Shipping Co. and
chairman for many years. Occasionally he
spared time to be acting-superintendent of
the Canterbury province when William Rol-
lestcm was away, and he sat in the Maori-
land’s House of Reps, when the provinces
were put out of business.
