Victoria gets another unpleasant shock by
learning that, in addition to the admitted
deficit on the State railways, that undertak-
ing has achieved a further loss of £820,000
in the last four years, which loss has just
been tardily disclosed. It occurs through
the non-payment by the Commissioners of
an amount equal to 2-1 per cent, on the capi-
tal cokt of rolling stock, and should come to
£250.000 a year, but the last four annual
payments have been only £50.000. £50.000.
£50,000 and £30,000. An added beauty is
given to this swindle on the kid by the fact
that the Commissioners, by means of extra
local rates, collect the 21 per cent, on the
rolling-stock used on new lines —such as the
Beech Forest extension —with mathematical
regularity.

The Gonsalez Opera Co. promises to re-
peat its previous success at Melbourne Prin-
cess’s. On Saturday a big house turned up
for another evening of “Trovatore” and
surfed in the sound-waves, as it were, with
all thfe enthusiasm of young Sydney at a
Manly carnival. A record tumult greeted
Cappelli, who responded to the welcome by
squeezing the last ounce of sweetness out of
his honey-sodden role. Vocally his Manrico
was a shower of pearls, but lie was not too
comfortable on the dramatic plane. In the
first place, a cruel tradition bad destroyed
liis dignity by condemning him to resemble
an advt. for Jaeger underwear. Also, he
had to endure an absurd tin hat, the port-
cullis or frontispiece whereof threatened to
fall and amputate his message at any
moment. Scamuzzi was a more confident
exponent of mediawal swagger as Conte di
Luna.' His scowl at Azucena was a revela-
tion of aristocratic choler, and he used his
fine voice to corroborate his annoyance, lie
also had a great reception, and swallowed
enough ovations to keep his ego content for
a month. Mrs. de Revers, whose attitude
to applause recalls a flapper before a tray of
cream-puff's, never let Eleonora be pushed
into obscurity, if mere shrilling could pre-
vent it. Yet she was more impressive when
her soprano was clambering noisily upstairs
than when it was stumbling over vibratos in
the basement, so to speak. Miss Russ has
parted with much of her tremulous enuncia-
tion and furnished a smoother rendering of
Azucena on Saturday. Cacialli’s veteran
basso also kept Ferrante in the forefront of
the proceedings. The band is an improved
institution, and the chorus more capable.
The opera, however, is as unfurnished as
before, and the anvil continues to look under-
sized for the purpose of an orchestral acces-
sory in the mountain scene of Act 11.

When the Easter Cow is being led into
Sydney, “The Pink Lady” will be going—or
gone—from Her Majesty’s. So it’s time for
the laggards to get a move on. (P.S. —“Her

Dentine’s dive of 140 ft. (or thereabouts)
is one of the surest breath-catchers ever put
on at Sydney’s big amusement park the
White City. Iligh-diving has a wicked
fascination for the onlookers, for few are
perfectly satisfied until the accident hap-
pens. Then, as in the case of poor Peart
they clap-to the mental telescope and say
“There you are! Wliat’d I say!” inci-
dentally, the Wirtlis, whose circus' opens at
the Hippodrome (Sydney) in a few days,
had an offer last week from a man to high-
dive into 2ft. of water. The management
reckoned that it had enough trouble in the
menagerie already.

On Sydney screens this week:—
Lyceum: “The Heiress of Coffee Dan’s,” illustrat-
ing' the dreadful bore of possessing £1,000,000 ; “The
Crab,” a comedy-drama with child-actress Thelma
Salter in the lead.
CrystAll Palace : “The Bride of Hate” —nothing
to do with the Kaiser’s missus, but merely a melo-
drama of the old American eotton-slave days.
Ada Reeve will be along to help Sydney
Tivoli presently, and the house needs assist-
ance since Fred Bluett went to Melbourne.
Fast week the bill acquired the Mayos, a
shapely pair of trapezists. The male partner
swings the breadth of the stage whilst hang-
ing by his talented heels, and is so used to
travelling in that fashion that he can’t criti-
cise the way an audience laughs unless he
can get a view upside-down. Lonzo Cox
(“The World’s One Great Silhouettist”)
works confidently with a cute shadow-pro-
jecting machine, a pair of scissors and a
screen, and seems very pleased with the
results; but he invented a complete new
profile for King George the night this
writer saw him. Frank Markley is an
entertaining banj'oist while he sticks to rag-
time. But his attempt at grand opera might
easily cause murder and civil commotion.
Elsie Peerless and Zona Trador are the other
pillars of the tabernacle.
“A Waltz Dream,” which is a light opera
in three short acts and two long intervals,
deserves more vocal assistance than it gets
from the present co. at Melbourne Majesty’s.
It is full of music, sugary but undistinctive,
and rich in opportunities for the spectacular
frippery incidental to Court life in a petty
German principality. “A Waltz Dream,” in
short, is in the way of being a reminder of
“The Merry Widow,” and the present per-
formance seems very inadequate compared
with the production of seven years ago, when
Roberts and Florence Young took the
musical honors. On the male side of the
cast there are no voices to speak of, and the
chorus of courtiers is but a motley gathering
of citizens in garments that once were new.
Moreover, the comedians, being called upon
to supply self-made drollery, are unable to
execute the order. Workman, as the pre-
posterous parent of the royal bride, lacks
the essentials of extravagant stage humor,
and bis essays in broad caricature are dull
and stodgy. As for Alfred Frith, his style-

* * * *
Marie Eaton, as the Princess whose Con-
sort goes astray on the wedding-night, gives
the stern critic a glad surprise. There is
a note of nuptial tenderness in her acting,
and her sometime harsh, unfeeling warble
is sweetened and modulated almost out of
recognition in her softer moments. Also
she and her lady-in-waiting (Ethel Morri-
son) are a pair of line women. They fill the
drapery bill. Dorothy Brunton is a bright
success as the saucy, sentimental leader of
a Ladies’ Band, who sends forth her soul in
a long, lingering kiss. Dorothy’s part degen-
erates into bathetic melodrama at the finish,
but she' saves it from absurdity. Connie
Ediss exploits her plumpness and her
familiar bleat as the hungry executant on
an empty drum ; Maguire is a mildly intelli-
gent Consort required to sing much music
(written for a tenor) in a small, baritonic
voice that often gets lost in amazement at
its temerity; and William Greene is an
uneventful newcomer in a pleasing uniform.
Field Fisher is a scarecrow waiter of minor
importance and MacCunn is the artist in
charge of the orchestra. “A Waltz Dream”
appears to be paving the way for “Canary
Cottage,” for which the public is requested
to get ready.
Taits’ tribute to the saving grace of
womankind and the softening influence of
peaches and jam continues to be presented
at Melbourne King’s. “Turn to the Right”
is a farcical drama that suggests Penitence
as a business proposition, and indicates that
it is better to crack the safe of the grinding
capitalist than leave him to rob the widow
of her valuable freehold. What worries this
paper, after duly approving of “Turn to the
Right,” is tiie thought that penitence works
out its own damnation, so to speak. In
turning utterly respectable, and thus qualify-
ing themselves to be made deacons, the blokes
from prison are probably sacrificing all their
original virtue. You can’t very well remain
penitent whilst expanding into a bloated
capitalist.
At Melbourne Royal the bewildering pic-
ture of Babylon and the Bloke will cease to
spasm after Friday, so anybody who wants
to read the riddle of Intolerance must hurry
up. On Saturday comes the new comedy
co. in “Fair and Warmer.”
Next Saturday, being St. Patrick’s own,
a grand national concert in aid of R.C.
charities will occur at Melbourne Town Hall.
The star soloist will be Goll and the advt.
specially mentions that he will perform on
a Wertlieim piano.
"VERY GOOD EDDIE,” AT SYDNEY
PALACE.
Waiter Charles Pope tries to collect a tip
from Eddie (Barry Lupino).
Malcolm McEachern deserves honorable
mention in this week’s communique from
Melbourne Tivoli. Malcolm is a basso who
sings all things well, and even makes “The
Bandoliers” sound different from the swag-
gering fiasco of an average musical evening.
The Keeley and Aldous dancing act is dam-
aged by the unkempt appearance of Keeley
and Aldous, and that’s a pity, because the
act itself is good enough. The acrobatic
turn of Niagara and Falls is only notable
for the small gent’s frenzied efforts to dis-
tract attention from his ineffectual lady
partner. Stewart and Lorraine cause the
Death of Nelson on strange instruments of
music. Also, they endeavor to turn a
dinner into a mouth-organ oratorio, and only
achieve a series of noisy discords. Of the
other artists the most conspicuous are Sen-
Mei and trickster De Forrest. Mane Iving-
Scott is back with the old set of wheezes,
and another returned empty is the buxom
Belle Oliver. Now Fred Bluett has re-
appeared with a new grimace and several
mouthfuls of fresh airs.
At St. Kilda (Vic.) on Saturday The
Dandies revived their burlesque oratorio,
which is quaint in spots but generally leaves
a lot of burlesque to be desired. Among the
individual performers comedian Joe Bren-
nan made the greatest impression with two
singularly effective pictures of juvenile
depravity. His neat song, “Shall Us.
Let’s!” caused an epidemic of stinging
palms. Robert Agnew sang saccharinely ot
his “Dream Girl” to a dinky spectre in pale
blue. Ford Waltham also Sassoed in a
characteristic item, and Marshall Lawrence’s
baritone created a draught in a manly roar
called “Three for Jack.”

Iloyt’s are again screening the classic
features of Olga Petrova in Melbourne. The
Metro star is the camera’s principal target

elements, an Apache Dance and the Battle
of the Marne. The Sensation of the film,
however, is a bit of blood transfusion
whereby the busy heroine saves the life of a
dubious male acquaintance. The other long
him is “The Faded Flower” produced by the
new Joan firm. Marguerite Snow is a daisy
in the title role, and a band of well-known
photoplayers render strong support.
The Fisk Jubilee Singers commence a brief
season at the Melbourne Auditorium next
Saturday night. A new generation has
grown, lip since the Fisks were last in Mel-
Oourne, so there will be many in the audi-
ence to whom the work of the company will
be a complete novelty.

Give a German a pig and you give him a
companion.— Old Roumanian proverb.
Perth has just lost by death Mrs. Eliza
Tracey, a lady with a 20-year-old grievance
agin the Government. The wrong was well
advertised. Over the door of her cottage
was a sign to the effect that therein dwelt
“Eliza Tracey, Robbed by the Government,”
and she is credited with having written a
lengthy pamphlet on legal ethics. Almost
any Sunday afternoon her voice could be
heard on the Esplanade, haranguing a strag-
gling crowd on all sorts of subjects, with
her personal grievance as a central recurring
theme. She was a withered little nut of an
Irishwoman, full of enthusiasm and volu-
bility.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Stargazer” : The arrival of “Turn to the
Right," at Melbourne King's, strengthens a
belief I have long cherished that the Ameri-
can soul is profoundly sympathetic towards
all crooks. In nine out of 10 recent
Yank dramas that have attained popularity
either hero or heroine has been a criminal
with a sentimental streak. Even where
hero or heroine has pinched nothing and shot
nobody worth mentioning, one or more sym-
pathetic characters in your national Ameri-
can drama are bound to be gaolbirds, or
law-breakers not yet segregated. All this is
merely an offshoot of the American business
ideal.* Both business and politics in America
are worked on lines that must engender in
participants that fellow feeling for crooks
which sets up a wondrous kindness. So we
get American plays for American business-
men in which Business is represented up
against Law the ogre. Law is the business-
man’s bete noire as much as it is that of the
crook. Here you have the genesis of the
criminal hero, sentimentally conceived, slav-
ered with bathos, for the American drama is
engendered and written to comfort and con-
sole the off-moments of the American busi-
ness-man —not American gaolbirds. Such
conditions render possible the sympathetic
conjunction of the angelic “maw” and the
three criminals in “Turn to the Right. The
beautiful grey “maw” (with halo) represents
the American’s capacity to kid himself that
he really loves moral beauty, and clean-
living, and all that sort of thing, when he is
not too busy robbing just such sweet, gentle,
amiable old ladies as this unearthly “maw.”
That they are too innocent to discover what
he is up to renders them doubly lovable.
“Yarraguudry” : For Australia’s sake don’t
take the desert fighting on the screens seri-
ously. The Romani Battle and ensuing
four-days’ fighting was purely a -Light Horse
and Maoriland mounted division action. Eng-
lish Yeomanry were attached to us, but were
kept its reserves. For reasons which I must
not mention here, Katia still stinks in the
C.O.’s nostrils. Some weeks after Romani
the cinema fiend came along. We and the
Maorilanders were then at Hill TO, some
miles away, and got no invitation to the
party. The Egyptian Labor Corps (all
niggers) were sent to represent the Turks,
and the Yeomanry, in Tommy felt hats,
cavorted round representing our blokes.
Should the films reach Australia you will
understand why the lean (and we are lean
now) Anzacs have suddenly become moon-
faced.
“M.P.A.”: William Conway, a young Aus-
tralian pianist who made a start at the Aus-
tral competitions at Bendigo, lias got firmly
on his legs in vaudeville in Wilson’s wordy
territory. Conway hitched up with Cecil
Parkes, the small-boy violinist who left Aus-
tralia with his pa, and got a start with the
Orpheum people. They have just finished
some nine months on the circuit —a 7000-
mile job. As Conway is quite free from the
swank so prevalent in ’Murkan acts, he
has proved as refreshing as wattle-blossom.
The press has noticed it, and nearly every
report mentions William’s simplicity of
style. lie recently finished with Parkes, and
has now Hector Goldspink as a partner.
Goldspink is a Melbourne boy with a fine
tenor voice as an asset.
“Lannercost”: Eroni Bros., circus pro-
prietors, while visiting the Herbert (Q.)
a week or so ago, bought a 6ft. ’gator from
an Ingham resident. This saurian had
consistently refused all nourishment for over
12 months, and the only visible signs of the
fasting were a sort of shrunken appearance
round the neck and a slight depression under
the pinny. I reckon the show will be rushed
by people anxious to get a few points from
that solver of the high-cost-of-living problem.
“The Oldest Inhabitant”: Sorry to have
to correct Edward Dyson (B. 8/3/’l7).
G. V. Brooke was not in Australia in 1863,
and never held a lease of the Theatre Royal,
Ballarat. Dyson is probably thinking of
William Hoskins.
“Ancient Prompter”: “The Oldest In-
habitant” mentions (B. 22/2/T7) the in-
teresting fact that the original Shiel Barry,
whose success as the Miser in “Cloches de
Corneville” most of us have heard of, was
christened theatrically by the late W. 11-
Ford, who saw the two names in a news-
paper and fitted them together—a happy con-
junction. The nomenclature of the stage
would make a quaint and curious study. I
once asked a couple of pantomime comedians
who were brothers, born Smith, but called
themselves Wicksteed and Landerdale (or
something similar), how they came by their
moniker. “Got it off a cart,” was the reply.
“Cockaigne” : So we’re to get Tree at last!
lie is the most versatile actor I ever heard
of —Caliban, Col. Newcome, Bottom, Fal-
staff, Antony, Fagin, Hamlet, Herod, King
John, Macari, Macbeth, Mephistopheles,
Malvolio, Othello, Private Secretary, Rich-
ard the Second, Rip Van Winkle, Sir Peter
Teazle, Shyloclt, Ulysses, Wolsey. Yet, with
all respect for his very great talent, I must
dub him only an illuminative sketcher or
lecturer on his characters. There is no thrill
in him. One sighs for a flash of lightning,
as from a Kean or a Brooke. Devotees of
Macrendy, Phelps or Irving would not allow
Tree’s to be acting at all. A student’s man,
or a book in breeches, rather than flesh and
blood.
“Jwosp” ; I haven t noticed in. the retei-
ences to Henry Bracy any mention of the
scrap that occurred when his co. was play-
ing “Madame Angot.” Clara Thompson
was the Madame Lange, if I remember
aright, and Alice Lambert the Clairette.
There had been considerable jealousy be-
tween the two, and when the quarrel scene
in the last act of “Angot” came round it
developed into a free-for-all until the cui-
tain stopped the bout and hid a stage clut-
tered with rags, locks and hairpins. It was
the best scrap ever seen from the front stalls
in Australia.
“0.K.”: Ada Reeve is returning to the
Tivoli circuit. And (let’s hope) bringing
some new songs with her. The old
stuff was mostly “pussy,” or maudlin, or
both, and it was only the art and personality
of the winsome woman that made it go. It.
is fairly claimed for Ada that none of her
songs have ever been stolen by commonplace
serio-clironies. If other lips and other arts
had the temerity to tackle “The Day When
Baby First Wore Knickerbockers” —well,
the result would probably be fatal.
In winter time the stars come out and the
devout gazer is expected to get cold feet at
their concerts. The press-agent says that
Evelyn Scotney and husband Howard White
are “contemplating” a return visit to Aus-
tralia under J. and N. Tait direction. They
have, as a matter of fact, “contemplated so
far as to book' their passages from N’York
for June 0. That means evening-dress and
bouquets in windy July.
“Stargazer”: What is more persistent
than the mannerisms of an actor? The
Great Pyramid, perhaps. Here is Gaston
Mervale, back after a few years’ absence,
providing a striking example of the un-
changeable. I select Mervale because he
was always a pro. of conspicuous trickiness,
and he hasn’t lost a single trick. As he
was so he is, and ever shall be, world with-
out end, ah men! He stands in the same
old attitudes, tasting his words with com-
pressed lips, his tongue to his palate, like
a wine-tester, making the same peculiar
grimaces over each phrase, the same
gesticulations to a hair, the same quaint
shake of the body at more emphatic
sentences, as like the Gaston Mervale .of
years ago as the Sphinx of to-day is like
the Sphinx of last Spring. “Live and
learn” is a maxim for everyone but your
actor. I have never known one who after
35 developed as much as a bullet dropped
from its mould, which may contract a beauti-
ful smoothness.
“Trot”: When is someone going to mod-
ernise military opera? It seems about time
the soprano was discovered packing com-
forts with an E in alt or knitting coloratura
socks by the sad sea waves. Is there not
a chance here for local genius to seize one
of the old operas, tear nut the yarn that
nobody ever understood, and adapt the
melody (if any) to Australian conditions,
with the sound of battle in the orchestra
and repatriation as the grand finale? Thus
might someone restore the prestige of the
old-time musicians and turn opera into a
rational business.
THE TOUR THAT WASN’T.
[For Thb Bulletin.]
The tickets for England were taken,
The company booked to appear;
The comedy “Saving our Bacon”
Was staged in the House over here;
The Reps, they saw nothing agen it,
v The ushers they gave it a hand,
But the welcome it got in the Senate
Was quite of a different brand;
Oh, the Senate, the hard, deadly Senate,
For the play or the east wouldn’t stand!
The tickets for England were taken,
The troupe started picking its props,
When faith in the mummers was shaken,
And soon they were looking for “shops.”
For in London (the truth 1 must pen it),
Unless their credentials this side
Were approved and endorsed by the senate,
Their mumming was safe to be “guyed”!
The critics who sat in the Senate
Gave “the bird” and declared the show “snide.”
The tickets to England are forfeit,
The press-agents all have been sacked,
What time a fit crowd (perhaps more fit)
Is engaged that can better attract.
And (who knows?) to far abler men it
May fall to be stars in the show —
That’s the view of the much-abused Senate;
So the whole crowd, each posturing pro.,
Through the act of the hard-headed Senate
To the country—not England—will go.
VICTORIA.

SOCIETY
“Yarragundry:” The Gyppo bootblack s
impression of the difference between the
Tommy and the Anzac: “Tommy come he
say, ‘Oi, choom! Has your mother any
children? Give me a shine and I'll give ee
half a piastre.’ Anzac come along he
say, ‘Come on you (censored) black (several
things), shine these boots!’ And he give
me one piastre.”
“Muriobits” : You’ve read about the Front
where men have been drowned in the mud ?
Well, that’s where the following little mili-
tary fashion-note was tapped out by some
regimental typist and circulated for our
guidance:—
Commanding Officers will be held responsible that
all their officers proceeding on leave are informed of
these instructions: —
Belts must always be worn.
Sticks must invariably be carried.
Gloves to be worn or carried in the hand visibly.
Pipes never to be smoked in public places.
Military great-coats must never be worn over
plain clothes, and when worn in uniform must be
properly buttoned up.
Fancy socks are forbidden.
Shoes, if worn, trousers must be turned down.
Dancing is not allowed in public in uniform.
You can always make a Hit by quoting that
bit about dancing to some poor, weary cow
with five pounds of Somme mud on each
boot.
From a Billjim in an English dry dock :
Some game of the green and highly-scented variety
approved by J. Bull was forwarded to this hospital,
and the matron, an Englishwoman, delighted to have
such a delicacy for her Australian lads, took it in
to show them, as happy as could be. But her
happiness was short-lived. The poor, ignorant Bill-
jims turned it down. Their uneducated Australian
stomachs had no fancy for such lining. The missus
pitied them, and decided that if they had once
tasted game they would never refuse it again. Con-
sequently, next time a consignment was dug up and
sent in she didn’t say anything about it, but just
had it prepared and sent round the wards. Imagine
her feelings when she found all the Jimbills sitting
up in bed and roaring for their gas helmets!
NOT SO SURE OF THEIR DEVOTION.
OFFICER : “By Gad, y’ know, the devotion of
our brave fellows is magnificent.”
SHE: “Um — yes—I suppose so. But a lot
of them are frightful flirts.”
“D.F.” : State and Federal politics haven’t
a monopoly of queer incidents. Not long
ago the council clerk of a down-south muni-
cipality lost his job as the result of a mis-
placement of funds. The auditor reported
that it was a pretty clear case, but as the
money was paid the thing was hushed up.
The next happening was a vacancy on the
council, and the ex-clerk got himself elected
and was present to hear and discuss the
report about the irregularities of the late
council clerk.
“Saint”: Anybody entering tenders for
the job of spending five hours 12ft. under
water, head down, is hereby informed that
Walter Smith, a diver employed by the
Union Steamship Co. at Wellington (M.L.),
has completed the job. He went down to
clear a steel hawser which had fouled the
propeller of the stranded Mararoa, and after
he had been below some time the men above
found they could get no reply to signals, nor
DEAD ON.
“Would you scream if I kissed you?"
“I don't see how I can if you kiss me on the
right place."
could they haul him up. Another diver was
sent for; hut, as it was Sunday, there was
a delay of some hours before he could be
found. When he did descend lie found
Smith so firmly held in a bight of the hawser
that it was a work of considerable difficulty
to clear him. It was certainly Smith’s lucky
day, for the only thing about him that hadn’t
fouled was his air pipe.

Adelaide’s police-barracks have been
moved at last from the site behind the
Museum, which they have occupied since
the earliest days of S. Aus. The British
soldiers, as long as there were any, were
quartered there, too, and so was the gaol,
which has long since been r oved down to
the north-west corner of the city. That is
where the police have now gone, too, some
of the olive plantation 'nearby being sacri-
ficed for their new barracks. The ground
had in very early days been used as a dump-
ing-place for rubbish of various kinds, and
the foundations took a bit of basing in con-
crete, but £16.000 has made a complete and
comfortable-looking job—-including a billiard-
room.

“Desirable Villain”: The estate agent’s
life has its- bright spots. The other day a
stout, prosperous-looking missus came
around to look over our property list. She
chose a neat outfit, priced at £4OO and bring-
ing in 8 per cent, gross, and said
she’d take it. I suggested the
usual deposit. “Oh, d’y’ want a
deposit V” the old thing exclaimed.
“I assure y’ I’ll take it, orright.”
I explained that it was the custom
of all agents to ask for a payment
with which to clinch any sale,
since a ready-cash buyer might
come along in the meantime. “But
—er —when,” I asked, carefully,
“would you be able to close the
purchase?” “Why,” missus said,
“me husband’s just made his will.
He showed it t’ me this morn’, an’
he’s left me £5OO. The doctor
says he can’t possibly larst out
the week.” Well, business is busi-
ness, and as the missus didn’t have
the deposit I generously agreed to “keep it
for ’er” —a verbal agreement, of course. She
paid the £4OO yesterday.

we now have the privilege of travelling
second class on the Egyptian railways, pro-
vided there is sufficient room on the train.
Previously third-class (“Gyppo”) carriages
were reserved for Billjim, as Defence Min-
ister Pearce said. But it was only for Bill
on duty. Bill on leave had nothing reserved
for him; he just had to pig in with the
natives. The new rule changes that, though
it does it haltingly. _
The crayfish makes a pleasant enough dish
when properly dismembered and sorted out.
but he needs attention of this kind before
being allowed to come to table. Once a
Vic. Governor was bidden to a banquet
arranged by a wowser mayor. The feast
was spread by the Mayor’s missus and a
select party of the missus’s pals. It con-
sisted mostly of cakes and sandwiches, but
along the groaning board at intervals of
six feet or so of scarlet
crayfish was placed,
ready to be torn to
pieces by the guests.
There were half-a-
dozen outsize speci-
mens on the Gover-
nor’s table. When
his Ex. arrived he
gave one wild, fright-
ened look around the
chamber of red death
and then made firmly
for his seat, eyes
front, with the air of
a man determined to
do his duty at all
costs. Shortly after-
wards an aide stole
stealthily to the near-
est ’phone and sent a
message to Govern-
ment House, tilling
the butler to keep
dinner on the side of
the stove until further
notice. Excellency
politely nibbled a
sandwich, drank a
glass of sodawater,
made his little speech
and then tore himself
away. It was one of
the briefest banquet
attendances recorded
by any Y’ic. Governor.
But the mob sensibly stayed on and shredded
the very last crayfish before retiring for
the night.

Still another lawyer on the prospects of
the profession in connection with the infant
N. S. Wales Workmen’s Compensation Act:
Speaking with some experience of the workings
of the Employers’ Liability Act 1897, I can safely
say that the lawyers—for the employers at any rate
—have not made a hatful in costs, and that,
although the Act allows many, exceptions, employers,
or rather the underwriters behind them, very rarely
contest claims unless an important principle is in-
volved, because it is always “something to nothing.”
In theory the Act was framed to give the poor man
a fair chance against his better-financed employer,
but in actual practice it has given him the thick
end of the stick, as, win or lose, the employer always
stood to drop his costs. As a result the “spec.”
lawyer has been able to hold out for compensation,
not on what a jury would give his client, but on
the basis of what. it would cost the employer to
contest and win. Although the winner has legal
recourse against the loser, the employer has a very
poor chance of “lifting” At 100 or £2OO from the
working man or woman plaintiff. “Sub-section 2,”
who discussed the prospects the other day in The
BULLETIN, looks to the 1916 Act’s definition of an
accident —“Personal injury by accident arising out
of and in the course of employment”—to provide a
harvest for the lawyer, lie spoils his case by show-
ing that under the present 1910 Act only five cases
in all have been fought during the'rpast five years,
and on this point the wording of the two Acts is
absolutely identical.

A TRIANGLE OF LOVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was an ancient man who told the tale,
A hoary elder bearded like the rope
That weary fingers tease in some cold jail
Where never comes tobacco, beer or hope,
llis oakum whiskers, wagging to and fro
From toothless jaws, proclaimed his faded woe.
“She was the peach of all the world,” he cried,
“As dark and winsome as a summer night.
She could not look unpleasant if she tried ;
And to her fingertips there throbbed delight
In the young gladness of the happy spring
When all the ways were sweet with bloom and wing.
“I met her on a Friday afternoon
Somewhere in George-street near a draper’s shop.
A distant band evolved a sleepy tune
From tubes of brass ; and on the highest top
The G.P.O. complained with bunting thin
That the Brindisi mail at last was in.
“Little I cared for mails or flags or bands
Who saw the dusky glory of her eyes.
The small, slim sweetness of her gloveless hands
All in an instant made my hot soul wise.
I raised my hat and asked her then and there
To come to Paris —Cairo —anywhere.
“We compromised with Manly. On the beach,
While rolling surf caressed the sand with foam,
I learned the young taste of the purest peach
That ever strayed on Cossack boots from home.
The flavor of her lips remains with me
As when we kissed that evening by the sea.
“Thereafter often in the starry night,
Or shaded by some herbage from the moon,
We sought all ways where waters of delight
On happy sand or sandstone love to croon.
All trams that electricity can shove
And every ferry-boat observed our love.
“Of ice-cream we absorbed a thousand quarts,
Of chocolate our joy consumed a ton;
And still the beating of our happy hearts
Proclaimed how great a prize we each had won.
We never wearied telling earth and air
How much we liked each other’s eyes and hair.
“So things were good, as you may well suppose.
I loved her for the dimple on her chin,
I loved her for the tip-tilt of her nose
And for her yielding lips as red as sin;
And she loved me for all the manly grace
I 'carried in my proud and perfect face.
“Her name was Kate. The other girl was Flo.
And Flo, I must admit, was sweet and kind.
1 loved her also and I told her so ;
For love like mine is fortunately blind.
She had not quite the peachy taste of Kate,
Though both looked much the same at half-past eight.
“On Monday and ou Thursday Flo was mine ;
Fridays and Sundays all were Kate’s alone.
But every Saturday when nights were fine
My strong impartiality was shown;
For Kate came on from four till five past seven
And Flo from eight till twenty to eleven.
“Thus, working double shifts, my heart beat well;
And Flo’s delicious kiss could bring me joy,
While Kate, more gracious than all words could tell,
Wore a dark sweetness that might never cloy.
And so unharmed we took our triple way,
Triangular in love from day to day.
“I do not make excuse. Wild war and rude
Had blown a call to manhood far and near.
’Twas surely mine to do whate’er I could.
Male love was scarce; and either girl was dear.
1 found them most expensive, but with pride
Observed that each would make a lovely bride.
“It was a happy evening, bright and warm,
When Fate her sharpest shears in anger drew.
The heedless Saturday was full of charm,
The skies above were exquisitely blue;
And Kate and I in happy homing stept
Down the long street whose dust since morn had slept.
“With seven brazen strokes the G.P.O.
Announced the scheduled parting kiss of Kate.
Our hot lips lingered till the voice of Flo
(The girl whose sweetness was not flue till eight)
Broke with a sudden anger on my ear
That caused my knees and soul to sag with fear.
“My curse upon the daylight-saving scheme
That for a week had maddened all the clocks!
Kate’s gladness ended in a sudden scream
With Flo’s ten fingers in her raven locks ;
And all the street became a flying whirl
Of frenzied petticoat and raging girl.
“I turned. 1 fled. Age caught me in an hour
And lined my face and whitened all my hair
As if a sudden avalanche of flour
Poured on me from the mountains of despair.
1 ran and ran from those wild shears of Fate
Whose closing blades were Flo attached to Kate.
“Three weeks I ran, nor dared to turn my head,
Fearful of vengeance still upon my track,
Until these whiskers, sprouting as 1 fled,
Disguised my features. Now 1 wander back,
Sure that however woe my draught may mix x
No girl will dream I am but twenty-six.”
It was an ancient man who told the tale,
A hoary elder bearded like a rope.
His eye was blank, his wrinkled cheek was pale,
He seemed devoid of teeth and shame and hope;
And yet the lie he told had moved me so
I wondered sadly what Kate did to Flo.
N.s.w.

HAVE YOU REALLY GOT
DYSPEPSIA?
THE MAGNESIA TEST.
Physicians say that there are compara-
tively few cases of true dyspepsia, the
majority of the people who complain of this
justly-dreaded ailment being merely victims
of excessive acidity of the stomach. Now
there is a simple test whereby anyone can
tell whether the pain experienced after eat-
ing is due to excessive acidity or to real
dyspepsia. Obtain from the chemist some
bisurated magnesia and immediately after
your next meal take half a teaspoonful of
this in a little water. If, as is more than
likely, the symptoms which now distress you,
such as heartburn, flatulence, flushed face
and an uncomfortable feeling of fulness, are
due to acidity you will benefit considerably
from the use of bisurated magnesia, because
it instantly neutralises the dangerous acid
and also prevents all possibility of food
fermenting in the stomach. On the other
hand, should no benefit follow the use uV
bisurated magnesia, it may be assumed that
there is some derangement of the stomach
or digestive organs requiring a physician’s
personal attention. But give the bisurated. l
magnesia a good trial first, taking care, of
course, that you get genuine bisurated mag-
nesia, and you will probably find that its
valuable antacid properties will safeguard
you against all unpleasantness and discom-
fort after eating.— Advt.
All the horrors are hot in Flanders or
Armenia; some of them are in the next
street. On December 1 last a little girl of
five, daughter of a Smythesdale (Vic.)
engine-driver, was so badly burned through
her dress catching fire that the post-mortem-
ing doctor said one leg should have been
amputated immediately and that the spine
was exposed and charred. Her parents
weren’t Huns or Turks, so they took her
to a Chinese herbalist. He recommended
the Ballarat hospital at once; but as they
said they preferred yellow medicine to white,
Ah Sin applied honey and liquorice powder,
without dressings. The poor little mite grew
no better on this treatment; but the parents
had great faith in the slant-eyed fraud, so
she continued getting no bette 1, for three
long and horrible months —till February 27,
when, being then a mere bag of bones, some
of them charred, she passed to a world that
can hardly be worse for her. After learning
these hideous facts the Coroner opined that
the child died from “shock, arising from
burns.” The actual cause of death, of course,
was gross ignorance—the gross ignorance of
relatives; the grosser ignorance of a Chinese
person who is permitted to treat dreadful
burns and charred bones with honey and
liquorice powder. The other day a man
who wasn t a qualified pharmacist was fined
heavily for measuring out a few drugs and
compounding them according to a doctor’s
prescription. But Ah Sin, who isn't a quali-
fied pharmacist either, can weigh out his
useless remedies without any doctor’s pre-
scription, and apparently the Law merely
wishes him luck with his next victim.

pitals I was quite on my own and extremely
short in the financial sense. I had
taken a room in a house in South
Yarra, and one day when poking
about at the back of my grate to
see what was choking the damper
1 fished out a chamois-leather bag.
Out of this I emptied about £2OO
worth of assorted jewellery on to
the floor. For over three months
I had been in possession of that
room, and nobody had said a word
about lost property. Now, honesty
is my curse. I set to work and
discovered the name and where-
abouts of the former occupants of
the house, and eventually called
with my bag of valuables. Mrs.
Blank saw me, and
when 1 explained the
poor woman was ter-
ribly distressed. “Dear,
dear me!” she ex-
claimed, “I remember
quite well now putting
the things in the chim-
ney. But this is most
unfortunate. I would
not have had it happen
for any money. I sup-
pose you Would not care
to keep the jewellery?”
I stared in astonishment.
“You see,” she went on,
“my husband kicked his
brother . out of the
house, believing he had
stolen those things, and
life would not be worth
living if we had to have
James back in such cir-
cumstances.;” Even to
oblige a lady I would
not accept the missing
jewels, and left, conscience-stricken, realis-
ing that I had put a poor woman in a rather
tight place.
The P. and O. Co. has decided
to revert to agencies in Australia
and do away with the office of
superintendent. The company
was founded as far back as 1837,
“The Peninsula” being the first
scene of operation ; the extension
to “the Orient” occurred in 1840.
Twelve years later the first vessel
of the co.’s Australian fleet came
to Sydney. She was the Chusan,
of 699 tons gross tonnage and 80
horse-power—a new vessel re-
cently launched, barque-rigged,
and (the seas being full of devils)
carrying three guns. Henry Dow
was her colnmander. Sydney was
so proud of the .advent of a mail
steamer that it burst into a grand
ball, held, of all places in the
world, in the Museum Building,
August 26, 1852; a public holi-
day was observed, and “The
Chusan Waltz” was the piece of
the evening. However, the
Crimean War presently interfered
with the regular mail steamers
and the sailing vessels again "got
a show. Subsequently for many
years Melbourne was the head-
quarters of the co. in Australia.
When, later, Sydney became the
terminus, Henry Moore, o f
Moore’s Wharf, Miller’s Point,
became agent and held the billet
until a branch office was estab-
lished in Sydney under the con-
trol of a superintendent nominated
in London. The first Australian
superintendent was F. R. Ken-
dall, with headquarters in Mel-
bourne (1866) ; he was followed
by Geo. Withers; then came E. Trelawney,
and finally A. Gordon Wesche.

“Arden Hill”: At Newcastle recently
Judge Edmunds commented upon the scanty
and unsuitable house accommodation pro-
vided for N. S. Wales coal-miners, especially
on the South Coast and in the Western dis-
trict. Ilis Honor’s remarks are but the
echo of complaints which have been made
to Ministers for many years, and promptly
forgotten. On the South Coast the trouble
is confined mostly to the northern end of
the district, in the vicinities of Coalcliff,
Scarborough and Coledale. Owing to the
dearth of houses the bulk of the employees
in these pits are compelled to travel to and
from their toil by workmen’s train —some
of them a distance of 15 miles, in draughty
corridor carriages with clothes damp after
the rush from underground. It was under-
stood a few years ago that Crown land
between Scarborough and Coalcliff was to
be set apart for the erection of suitable
working-men’s cottages. A lease held by
one of the mining companies was also to
have been made available. But there was
some quibbling over the construction of an
overhead bridge across the railway, access,
and one tiling and another, and eventually
all the schemes collapsed. It would seem
as though tumble-down dwellings and un-
“CUTTING UP THE BIG ESTATES.”
sightly humpies are inseparable from coal-
mining in N. S. Wales. There is not a
tunnel mouth on the Coast the vicinity of
which is not disfigured -with these wretched
makeshifts. Some of them are lop-sided
with age and have roofs secured with logs
nnd boulders; bachelors’ humpies crouch
like dwarfs by the side of mountain path-
ways ; and galvanised-iron terraces bake the
inmates in summer and freeze them in win-
ter. Some are unprovided with drainage
or water supply, and the sanitary arrange-
ments are most primitive. Not long ago an
Inspector reported that some of the resi-
dents,- lacking sanitary conveniences of any
kind, utilised the bush near by. What are
known as “company’s houses” are usually
the crudest of the lot, and appear to have
been run up or jumbled up without any
thought of the inmates’ comfort. No wonder
the coal-miner is a wild, unreasonable indi-
vidual at times. You can’t reason like a
judge wdien half your life is spent in a pit
and the other half in a hovel.

“Marmoset”: The girls were just starting
to wash-up after tea, when Mrs. Brown
gushed in. As the English mail had arrived
we knew 7 she had come with news of Joe.
(Joe is son of Mrs. Brown and the affianced
of my sister Helen.) There was news all
right—a cable: “Married yesterday, remit
£20.” Without a word Helen flung down the
wiping-up cloth and rushed into her bed-
room. Silence for a second, and then a wail
from my young sister by the dish: “Well,
you might have waited till after the wash-
ing-up was done to tell her!”

market for his friends, let him read the last

Increases in wages owing to Wages Board
awards coming into operation since the
commencement of the year ... ..£129,401
Automatic increases due to earlier awards 72,982
Increased cost of materials .. .. 97,980
That was last year ; and as forecasted by the

heavier still. That and the loss of business
brought about by the selfish coal strike,
together-with the increased cost of fuel care-
fully passed on to the public, w-ill answ-er

and the producing interests of the State in
the manner suggested.
“Homaitikai” : So J. Bull is having roast
seagull, and somebody lias cabled out that
it tastes like mutton-bird. Well, well!
Thank Heaven, the enemy has no longer a
mastery of the air, anyhow. But you can
take it from me that if this strain of poultry
gets into general circulation the shrinkage
in the bingey department is going to flood
the market with secondhand pants. The
mutton-bird and the gull are aoout as much
alike in the nude as a barbwire fence and a
jumbuck. The first is a slothful animal; the
gull an athlete. The mutton-bird accumu-
lates layers of fat on its meat and travels
by easy stages from the last feed to the next
sleep. * The gull works up muscle and carries
a small reserve of fat in its innards. If you
know how to cook a mutton-bird and take
lessons in carving, something to eat can be
annexed without distributing smell for more
than half a mile. But when a gull gets on
the grill it promptly distills into attar
of 27 sharks, and leaves but its bones and
sinews as a souvenir. Not having been par-
ticular what it swallowed, though its prefer-
ence was for something pretty gamey, the
defunct gull naturally uoesn’t mind what its
flavor is in the pantry. If Bigjohn Forrest
ever gets to England and has to be nourished
on seagull while he is there, he ought to try
to run the blockade with a few ham sand-
wiches to start with, anyway.

K. G. Nesbit, president of the Licensing
Court in Adelaide, turned loose recently on a
“shockingly improper” action of someone who
had written him an anonymous letter about a
certain hotel. Not only was it "flagrant con-
tempt of court,” but the writer made the
“usual hypocritical statement, ‘I do not sign
my name for obvious reasons.’ ” The obvious
reason, said the S.M., warming up, was that
the writer was “a cur and a sneak, who was
trying to do an injury behind the back of
the licensee, and who would not come for-
ward honorably and make his charge in open
court.”

THE RETURN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
And I watch the track on the Redsoil Plain
To the east when the day is late,
For one who shall surely come back again,
In summer’s heat or in winter’s rain,
Limping under his swag in pain—-
For a wounded Anzac mate.

MORAL COURAGE.
MAUDIE : “He must be awfully brave to hare
enlisted.”
MAISIE : “Oh, he's not the only one.”
MAUDIE : “No, but fancy daring to wear
puttees with legs like those!”
James Gardiner, the new Westralian
Speaker, made some sort, of a reputation as
Colonial Treasurer of the James Adminis-
tration in 1902 ; but his subsequent political
career added nothing to his reputation. lie
was the chief engineer in the formation of
the Country party, but after a comparatively
brief reign the hayseeds tired him in favor of
Willmott, whose brilliance never gives him
headaches. Personally Gardiner is a good
fellow, a tireless storyteller, and highly emo-
tional. He has one excellent qualification
for the Speakership—a slight defect in hear-
ing.
The Bulletin prize for tact goes to
Bruce Smith this week. Speaking in the
Heps, last week, lie asked if it was a fact
that Maltese were imprisoned on a hulk in
Sydney Harbor, and what legal justification
there was for placing an embargo on them.
-I countryman of the Maltese teas at present
the Governor of one of the States. It is a
rule of the Boy Scout troop to which Bruce
belongs to do at least. one kind deed to a
State Governor every day.
The curiosity amongst the candidates pre-
senting themselves for the N. S. Wales
State elections is Percy Watson Brunton,
who puts in a claim for Darlinghurst against
Levy and his selected Labor opponent. John
Farrell. He is the most talkative and popu-
1a r of Syd-
ney Domain
notables, dresses
in a top hat
and frock coat
an d decorates
himself with
whistles a n d
other trifles.
It is all done
to attract trade
to a peanut
business, which
i s profitable
enough to enable
him to pay fre-
quent fines for-
obstructing the
traffic.
LUCIEN
DECHAINEUX.
Principal of the
Hobart Technical
School, a good
Australian ,
though born in
Liege, Belgium.
A. A. Kirkpatrick, who has been chosen
to lead the U.L.P. in South Aus. against
Crawford Vaughan, is a Londoner by birth,
hut has been in Adelaide nearly all his life.
He was one of the first Labor men to get
into Parliament (1891), and spent most of
his time after that in the Log. Council —
including* a term as Chief Secretary and
Minister of Industry—before going to Lon-
don in 1909 as Agent-General. After his
service there was over, he was unlike the
average Agent-General in (1) returning
home promptly and (2) being as keen in
politics as ever. lie will be 70 next year.
Dead at Wellington (M.L.L John Robert
Cole, “Tiaki” of many good Bulletin Abo.-
Maori paragraphs. In his 40 years he had
followed many occupations, including house-
painting, whaling, interpreting and journal-
ism. He was a recognised authority on
Maori folk-lore, and from his sympathetic
insight into the Maori character he was
much beloved by Iloni, whose interpretation
of ms first name (“Jack”) he signed to all
his paragraphs. A generous and unselfish
spirit found him many friends among the
pakeha.
Pen-portraits of politicians, from Pro-
fessor Scott’s new “Short History of Aus-
tralia” :
Barton.—A constructive statesman of wide grasp
and deep learning, who had determined to make this
(Federation) the main purpose of his political life.
Kingston.—A man of haughty temper, notwith-
standing his strong democratic leanings; at his best
intellectually as a draftsman of Parliamentary bills.
He spoke in _ a series of emphatic spasms heaved
forth in a voice of thunder ; but when he took pen
in hand to prepare an Act of Parliament he had
command of a crisp precision of phrase, and a sure
sense of the value , of words, that could express a
meaning in the shortest and most unmistakable
terms.
Watson.—His retirement was regretted by all
parties, for he had made his mark as a fair fighter,
a clear thinker, and a cool, courteous and able
political leader.
Fisher.—With none of the graces of speech) he
had yet, by constant practice at the expense of his
audience, acquired some readiness in debate. His
vein of Scotch caution was allied to a strong will,
which, of course, his opponents described as sheer
obstinacy. Shrewd if rather slow, keen if a little
insensitive, he never lacked personal dignity, and
when unruffled by the passions of conflict was
courteous to all. A thoroughly loyal friend and a
man of staunch rectitude.
Keid. —His fund of humor was not the least of
his endowments; and it was employed to give liveli-
ness to a rare gift of dignified and impassioned elo-
quence, and to a quick-witted skill in debate, which
would seize upon a chance word as it flew and return
it as a weapon barbed....A leader whom nature
designed in a mood of kindness to' political carica-
turists.
John Gavan Duffy, who died last week,
aged 73, was the eldest son of Charles Gavan
Duffy, of much political fame in Britain and
Australia. John was born in Dublin in
1844, the year that saw his father, then pro-
prietor of The Nation, on trial with O’Con-
nell and others for conspiracy, lie came to
Victoria in 1859, four years after his father’s
arrival in that State. On leaving school he
went upon the land, but changed his mind
and studied law. For 30 years he repre-
sented Dalhousie in the Leg. Ass. and had
numerous Ministerial jobs, the lirst of them
in the Service Government, away back in
1880, when John Madden was Minister of
Justice. The late Tommy Bent had liis
first taste of office at the same time.
Back into Maoriland’s Parliament, John
Findlay, formerly Ward’s Attorney-General
and for a long while before that the private
adviser of Seddon. Findlay takes the
Ilawkes Bay seat held by Robert McNab,
and for the first time gets into the House of
Reps. Before the arrival of Massey he
decorated the Leg. Council. Findlay went
with Ward to the Imperial Defence Confer-
ence of 1911, and while in London ivas mis-
guided enough to accept a knighthood. Stung
by the loud jeers of the populace when he
got back he resigned from the Council and
“submitted himself to the people,” selecting
the apparently safe electorate of Parnell. It
looked a good thing, for poor old Frank
Lawrie had been able, in spite of his *sins, to
get through there for many years—and if
Lawrie why not Findlay? When the gale
was over John deposited what was left of
himself in the next train for home, and a
youthful draper proudly thanked the democ-
racy for dragging him away from the bar-
gain-counter. Findlay is a Iv.C., owns a
nimble mind, a mellifluous tongue and a
tendency to the milder radicalism. Had he
been able to find a punch and evade the
penalty of being a Superior Person who in-
variably approaches the public, in a manner
of speaking, with a clinical thermometer in
one hand and a spoonful of cough-mixture
in the other, he might have gone a long way.
W. LI. Forrest, who becomes N. S. Wales
Stamp Commissioner in succession to R. N.
Johnson, is an English solicitor who has
been sitting at a desk in the Crown Law
Office in Sydney for the past 17 years
instead of chasing costs on his own account.
The Harbor Trust has been the department
whose legal affairs he has 'specially looked
alter. For eight years Forrest was presi-
dent of the Tennis Association in the Ma
State, and for four years of the Australasian
body. In the rest of his spare time he
grows roses at Neutral Bay.
Passed out at Maitland* (N.S.W.), John
Pender, who planned most of the non-Gov-
ernmeut buildings in the northern half of
the Ma State. Born 84 years ago at Tober-
mory—the place Harry Lauder sings about
—-Pender landed in Melbourne in 1855, and
went to MaitlancT two years later, just in
time for the big flood of that year. The
rebuilding boom which the flood caused set
him up as architect and builder, and he stuck
to the game for 60 years.
John Earle, whom magician Hughes con-
verted from a Franklin apple-grower’s rep.
to a full-blown Senator in one night, origin-
ally used his judgment on the end of a pick
at the Bischoff mine
and between times
educated his mates
in Labor doctrines.
In return they sent
him to the State
Assembly as their
rep. in 1900, when
he became leader of
the State Labor
Party. John .headed
Tassy’s first. Labor
Government, which
reigned for seven
days in 1909. He
was again Labor
Premier from 1914
to 1916, but resigned
from the party after
the recent conscrip-
tion fight.
SENATOR' JOHN
EARLE.
The Speck’s prison-guests lost a hearty
battler the other day when Mrs. Helen Bird
died at the age of SI. She used to hold services
in the Hobart Gaol till the Sheriff put his
foot down; and at any time to get into
clink was a sure way to interest her. Her
husband, now the “father” of the Tasmanian
House of Dodery, and an ex- (very much
ex) Congregational parson, used to say
that he believed if he cou’d only get a sen-
tence she would devote a bit of attention to
him.

TACTICS.
OFFICER (to applicant for job of motor-
transport driver) : “And now you meet on
the road a mob of horses, and these horses
are frightened at your motor —what do
you do?”
EX-CHAUFFEUR: “Stop the lorry!"
OFFICER: “Quite so; and having stopped the
lorry, the horses are still frantic with fear.
What do you do?”
EX-CHAUFFEUR: “Stop the engine!”
OFFICER : “Quite so. Then the horses being
still frantic, what do you do?”
EX-CHAUFFEUR: “Well, Mister, I’d get down-
off the lorry, take out the screw-
wrench, take the flamin’ lorry to pieces,
dig a hole in the road, bury the lorry, and
then sit down and—well wait.”
Fate played a grim jest on a Sydney scien-
tific man, L. M. Roberts, B.E„ son of
“Jack” Wliear Roberts, who many years ago
quitted journalism to chase millions in
mining. lie volunteered for active service
and went into camp, where he contracted a
serious illness. Unable then to pass the
medical test he was delegated to home ser-
vice, and joined the Patents Investigation
Board in a professional capacity. Having
thus escaped the dangers of active service he
trod on a nail at home; and tetanus finished
a young life of great promise.
Henry Johnson, who weighed anchor for
the last voyage at Sydney the other day,
was a more or less ancient mariner who had
spent most of his life in colliers. He came
to Australia in one of Huddart Parker’s first
steamers, and ran her between Melbourne
and Geelong; but on getting his master’s
ticket he went into the coal trade, first in
the Duckenfield for J. and A. Brown, and
later for the Wallarah Co. He brought out
several new steamers from the Cold Country
for the latter co., as the shiftless Australian
won’t build ships in his own country. Of
late, he had been master of the Beulah; in
the coal trade between Newcastle and Mel-
bourne.
In Adelaide, where barristers and solici-
tors mix it, even the leading men at the Bar
have numerous partners. Thus Glynn, Iv.C.,
and Angas Parsons, K.C., are members of a
firm, with George McEwin : and now J. M.
Napier also joins the combination. Glynn
is M.H.R. and a Federal Minister; Parsons
m president of two dozen societies and
things; McEwin is a City Councillor.
Napier is nothing much yet except brainy
and lecturer on Constitutional law at the
Uni. Paddy Glynn is the State’s best-known
and broguiest Irishman ; Parsons is Consul
for Japan; McEwin is chief of the Cal-
edonians. If Napier is willing to be known
merely as an Australian, Australia won’t
mind.
Old King Cole, of the Book Arcade —now
a very mellow old monarch with white hair
crowding out over his ears from under his
well-preserved silk hat—still keeps the
family brougham in commission. It is one of
the events of the day in Bourke-street when
the equipage trundles slowly up to the brassy
front of the Arcade and the King alights,
assisted across the banana-peel danger-zone
by a princess in glace silk and a sweet smile.
The enterprise looks a very serious one for
the ancient book-merchant, but it will be a
sad day for him when he is finally con-
demned to the old arm-chair and the back-
garden.
WE’RE ALL HELPING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There has been a record entry of candidates for
the scholarships offered by the Melbourne University
Conservatorium of Music.
Now, some will grow two blades of grass where
one was grown before,
And some, by toiling double tides, may make it
three or four,
And others will accept the job, foreseeing duns and
drought,
Of speeding-up old Strawberry to help Australia out;
Yet who would fear the future, pray, with Emma all
day long
Pounding the grand piano for her country right or
wrong ?
The day of reckoning draws lfigh ? Good Arthur,
full of vim,
Is slaving in the city, with his work cut out for
him,
Producing full, round, splendid notes from some-
where near his boots.
Ilis dad is planting maize and oats and raising
various roots
To feed the pigs and cows that pay for plodding
Arthur’s keep,
While day by day his mellow tones grow yet more
rich and deep.
Against the long, lean years ahead the fiddler plies
his bow;
■His duty will be done, at least, however things may
so;
For well he knows his native land has need of men
like him ;
And so he toils and struggles, with a purpose great
and grim,
Bumping the bumps upon the way that leads to
Further Up
Where loud applause and half a guinea brim Ambi-
tion's cup.
Yes, some will grow two blades of grass where once
there was but one,
And some, at work from early morn until the day is
done,
Will help Australia’s flocks and herds to thrive and
multiply ;
And some will break new acres to be seeded by-and-
bye; >
And Emma —earnest little thing—will do her noble
bit
By sitting at the concert-grand and thumping hard
on it.
VICTORIA.

Profits, observe, are said to be gross. They
can, however, be cut up this way:—
Profit earmarked to cover depreciation .. £B,lOO
Ditto to cover insurance . . . . 5,928
Net profit 13,570
Total £27,598
The amount earmarked for depreciation
seems ample. There is no earthly reason
why the co. shouldn’t make it ample. The
directors presumably don’t want to pay more
than 10 p.c. (excessive dividends are un-
healthy nowadays), and there is little or
nothing to be gained by claiming profits in
excess of the amount required to pay the 10
p.c. On the other hand, there is something
to be lost. Perhaps a truer view of how the
co. is really doing can be got from this com-
parison :
Dec..,’lo. Dec.,’ls. June,’l6. Dec.,’l6.
Steamships and
other non-liquid
assets . . £241,508 £345,844 £345,933 £346,749
Liquid assets. . 51,605 132,418 168,101 159;211
Liabilities . . 24,732 39,415 45,671 33,529
Between December, TO,, and December, T 6,
the capital was increased by £110,000; but
£51,000 of that is capitalised profit, so only
£60,000 of new money has been got in. Add
£8797 increase of liabilities; total £68,797.
Meantime, assets have grown by £212,847.
So we can put it this way. Besides paying
the dividend and providing for all repairs to
the fleet, the co. has made £144,050 in six
years. Probably this is not all true profit in
excess of the dividend., Some of it may be
money which will have to be spent later on
in making renewals. But there must have
been extensive repairs, etc., during the past
six years ; and there is no visible reason why
the next six years should show poorer results
than the past six because of a much larger
bill for repairs and renewals. Certainly, the
directors say that “the increased cost of coal
of 3s. .per ton from January 1 this year and
higher wages to workers generally will seri-
ously affect future profits of our steamers.”
They add, however: “Unless compensatory
relief is obtained.” And in this trade, gov-
erned by an honorable understanding (or
worse), “compensatory relief” is very easily
obtained. What the easy and nasty settle-
ment of the coal strike is going to cost the
producers and manufacturers of this country
Heaven only knows. Already it has put up
the price__of gas; here it is going to put up
freights. * And evidently the freights are
going to be put up on the basis of 3s. per ton
dearer coal though the chances are that this
co.’s coal doesn’t cost anything like 3s. per
ton more.

That doesn’t look healthy—£3s,692, £24,514,
£22,521, £21,203, £17,885. The steady

is the matter. Is it falling-off of revenue,
and if so where, or is it increase of exes.?

A remarkable growth of the gross profits
on brewings, etc., occurred between 1911
and 1915. In the four years prior to 1.911
there was a growth of £19,700 in the summer
season’s gross profit; in the four years after
1911 there was a growth, as the foregoing
comparison shows, of £41,800. This was
during the period of the McGowen-Holman
Government’s wild borrowing; for Jimmy
McGowen came into office, full of good in-
tentions, in 1910. This brewery co. has
thus got fat on the bogus prosperity caused
not by the mere borrowing of £41,000,000
in six years, but by the spending of it with-
out serious regard to the value got for the
money.
i Then came the war; and though the
Federal Government has since been borrow-
ing millions, the consequent boom has been,
curiously enough, of quite another sort. It
is the draper and the jeweller and the gew-
gaw . merchant who has been reaping the
harvest this time. A hundred thousand
women went to the liquor referendum poll
in N. S. Wales and compounded for sins
they were, inclined to by damning those they
had no mind to. There should be no liquor-
ing up in war time; so they voted for six
o'clock closing. And in the ensuing six
months tens of thousands of them spent more
on dress and frivols, including silk stock-
ings, than they had ever done before in any
six months of their lives. The wholesale
fluff merchants of Australia have probably
never done so well as they have ip the past 12
months; and there are little drapery stores
in mean suburbs which have made fortunes.
So we get a peculiar result in this liquor
trade. While very tittle of the proceeds of
the heavy war borrowing has gone to this
trade —no more than enough to counter-
balance the loss through the early closing
of pubs—the cos., have been hit by the higher
wage costs which have been the general
result of this borrowing boom. To realise
£97,961 of gross profit on brewings in the
first half-year of the war cost £66,121 in
wages and salaries and office charges: to
realise only £95,638 last half-year cost
£80,300. Hence the reduction of profits.
This paper doesn’t know what is going to
happen when the war ends. For a moment
there may be a joyous reaction and a little
fill-’em-up-again. But it isn’t likely that
there will be much of that. There will be
far too many wounds to heal, far too many
bills to pay. We don’t realise yet what we
have undertaken to do after the war, not
merely in paying interest and repaying bor-
rowed principal, but also in regard to
deferred nay and pensions. We may be a
devil; but we are going to feel a pretty sick
devil when it is all over, and there was
never a saint who was quite as saintly as
a devil who is sick. Certainly some relief
will come from lower costs. Labor, after
it has been in the wilderness for a few
months, will begin to recover the reason
which was taken from it by the dope with
which Labor Governments have wickedly
served it; and, though wages may keep
high, the service given in return will
correspond.

This co.’s properties, in the south-west back-
blocks of Queensland, wilted under a three
years’ drought which broke up in June last.
Thereafter until now there have been won-
derfully fat rains. Nature has simply been
having an orgy of breeding and reproduc-
tion ; and with the reproduction that was
good and meant money there has been breed-
ing that is bad and has meant loss. The
lambing just after the drought broke was
a dreadful failure; only 4932 were marked.
Then came the blowfly plague ; and between
that pest and starvation in the early months
of the year the co. lost 23,516 sheep. To
further cut down the co.’s profits the shear-
ing was delayed in an almost unprecedently
wet season, and delay in shearing means
expense. So the profit works out at only
£1839, notwithstanding that, owing to the
phenomenal prices for wool, the previous
yeaffs clip realised £7731 'more than had
been taken credit for in that year’s accounts.
Certainly next year’s accounts may disclose
that the past year’s wool brought some
thousands more than the money which it
is now estimated to be worth. As a matter
of fact, no single year’s accounts of this co.
are worth anything, because, while the ex-
penditure can be stated to a penny, the
revenue is always a guess. For the past
year, for instance, the clip sold up to
December 31 totalled £418; the remainder
was guessed to be worth £22,083: and on
that guess the profit is based. No more
unsuitable date than December 31 could be
picked for making up the accounts of this
co. The only thing to do is to take a
general survey of the figures.
And this may also help:—
1907. 1914. 1915. 1916.

In nine years, therefore, liabilities have
been reduced by £42,961, while the co. has
about £16,500 more in cash and liquid assets.
On the other hand, live stock has been re-
duced by more than one-half. But for
practical purposes that can be almost dis-
regarded, because, given average conditions,
the stock can bring itself up to- the old
figures in about two years—a wonderful per-
formance, hardly possible in any other in-
dustry in the world. Ilowetmr, the co. has
something to think about besides the fecun-
dity of Mary’s little lamb. The co.’s holding
is on lease, and the lease runs out in about
18 years. Anything may happen then.
Towards the return of capital the co. hasn’t
very much saved up, as the last comparison
shows ; indeed, whenever the co. pays more
than p.c. in a year the directors ask the
shareholders to consider the excess as some-
thing paid off capital. There are 97,335
shares paid to 60s. and worth somewhere
about 275. 3d. in the market.

Besides its home trade the Cascade has a
very fair business with the mainland. Now,
Tasmania, S. Australia and N. S. Wales are
the States which have led in six o’clock clos-
ing ; and the directors of this co. throw dis-
credit on the yarn that that “reform” hasn’t
affected the trade in those States. “Queens-
land, Victoria and Westralia,” they say,
“have shown increases; while in N. S.'Wales
and S. Australia sales have declined.” As
for the future, there seems to be very little
reason to expect that where they diave been
closed the pub doors will again 'be opened
after six o’clock—at any rate, if the matter
is decided by referendum. And the story that
early-closing does not affect the total con-
sumption of liquor is obviously incredible,
though the consumption of wines and spirits
may be affected more than of beer. The out-
look for any brewing co. is, therefore, not
brilliant. And it is most uncertain.
The co. is heavily loaded with property—a
good deal of it non-revenue producing—and
it is carrying a swag of debt in consequence.
But matters are improving:—

So in five years liabilities have been reduced
by £32,058. True, accumulated profits
haven’t been responsible for all that. Cus-
tomers’ balances, advances and the like have
been reduced by £14,152, releasing that
amount for the reduction of debt. But that
movement has been good, too ; for some of
those old accounts looked very doubtful.
Altogether, then, the co. has been doing some-
thing solidly better than the 6 p.c. it has
been paying ; and there may be fairly sub-
stantial profits to come in the distant future
out of the sale of some of its big Cascade
estate.

Pearson Law, Ltd. (Melbourne), manu-
facturers of shirts, collars and other men’s
wear, was registered at the end of Septem-'
ber, 1911, but no accounts are available for
the first nine mpnths. The co. paid £7400
for goodwill (apparently in shares), and
prehmmary exes, account for the rest of the
deficit with which this table starts:—
<—■ —Dividends. A
Pref. WHttpn
June, Profit. 7 p.c. Ordinary. Off. Deficit.

This co. arrived just in time; for though the
mercery trade has fallen off since 800,000
of the mercer’s best Australian customers
got into khaki, the Australian manufacturer
and especially the Australian manufac-
turer with a name already known —is doing
a proportion of the total business so large
that he would have believed it impossible
five years ago. One result is that the capital
of £35,400_ with which the co. started soon
became quite inadequate. The pressure was
eased for a while by hanging on to the
profits. At the end of the third year, though
the co. had made about £14,000, only £991
had been cut up among the ordinary share-
holders and £3OOO among the prefs. Since
then, though a conservative policy has been
pursued with regard to dividends, the need
for more capital has grown, and during the
past year 8496 pref. £1 shares were issued.
At the same time £5664 of the undivided
profit was transferred to capital, the ordi-
naries getting 11,328 shares paid to 10s. —a
not unreasonable thing, seeing that they
were content to do without dividends in the
earlier years when the co. was making
profits, but wanted money for business-
extension purposes. Of course the only
new money brought into the business is that
from the 8496 pref. shares; and that has
left the co. still owing £22,346, mostly to
the bank. Stock, of which the co. held a
modest £14,053 as at December, 1912, now
runs into £36,603; and, owing mainly no
doubt to the greatly increased turnover, the
book debts have pushed along from £8952
to £18,022. There should be plenty more
room yet for the legitimate expansion
ot: those figures. And good hopes, too. We
want something more than a Customs tariff
to put Australian industries where they
should be, and that is a stronger Australian
feeling; and that stronger Australian feel-
ing is being produced in France and England
and Egypt to-day. Those boys of ours are
coming back better Australians for having
been out of Australia; and that is going to
count for something in the future of Aus-
tralian industries.

operative Society, Ltd. (Adelaide), which
began with a deficit of £592, represented by
preliminary exes.:—

The share capital is now £21,264, divided
among no less than 1008 members. _ As usual
with S. Australian co-operative concerns
they don’t have “shareholders,” and “divi-
dends” are more or less wicked things. They
have “members” and “interest” —as a rule,
though sometimes the practical directors for-
get and call the thing by its proper name.
Anyhow, there is a big scope for the co. S.
Aus. has both growers and consumers, and
they are alike interested in coming into
direct contact. The parasite is the man Aus-
tralia must cut. out; lie is the enemy the
Labor party should have attacked—and
didn’t, in N. S. Wales particularly* This co.
consists of growers and retailers, who find
that their interests are surprisingly alike.
Its business is not to sell raw fruit to the
public (that part of the business had already
been fairly well catered for), but to'handle
the surplus and turn it into jams, etc.
Naturally a concern which gave the grower
and the retailer both the manufacturer’s and
the wholesale merchants’ profits has not been
allowed to get a move on without a fight;
but it seems to be pretty safely set up now,
and many of the co-operators are not only
sensible enough, but also public-spirited
enough, to leave their “interest” in the busi-
ness. It wants all the capital it is likely
to be able to get; for even going along in its
present fashion, which so far isn’t much
better than a baby-crawl, it is £7451 in debt.
Not that that is anything to worry about,
seeing that the co. holds £14,464 of stock and
over £6OOO of good book debts and liquid
assets.
“Coot” : Since the war began the French
Government has adopted the use of postal
cheques in connection with the savings banks.
Any depositor with 50 francs (£2) to his
credit can now draw a cheque. There is far
more reason for such a system in this land
of big distances than there is in France.
When shall we have it?

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd.— a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers. — Advt.

Babcock and Wilcox Water-tube Boiler, so univer-
sally used where the economy in steam-raising is con-
sidered, is due, no doubt, to the splitting up of the
water into 4in. tubes, which permit of rapid circula-
tion and excellent facilities for cleaning, the heavy
lin. plates used in the Shell Boiler being done away
with. — Advt.

The public has heard these figures pro-
nounced to be evidence of “the thrift of the
people.” They may be, but before they are
accepted as that it would be a good thing
to know what part the pay of soldiers has
played in the arithmetic and to see a dissec-
tion of the accounts. The evidence of sav-
ings banks generally is that the greater part
of the growth in figures is due to savings by
the proprietors of substantial credits. A few
people seem to save a lot; a lot of people
save very little. For instance, in N. S.
Wales in eight years the number of deposi-
tors with accounts under £lOO has doubled
and the amount to credit risen from
£3,424,000 to £7,971,000. But the number
of depositors with £3OO accounts and up-
wards, though only one-thirtieth as numer-
ous as the other lot, increased nearly three
times in number, and their credits moved
from £4,000,000 to nearly £11,000.000.
Approximately 50 per cent, of the total
deposits come from 3 per cent, of the deposi-
tors. That the Statistician’s figures apply
to the general savings made by the mass of
the Australian people during the last couple
of years is wildly unbelievable.
Sydney used to own most of the Broken
Hill South and Central. The cos. were
formed in the Ma State capital, and their
offices were there. It did no good with them
at all ; and there was a time when the South
was almost abandoned. It actually was
closed down. The Central did a bit better
for a time ; then the co. was glad to sell out
to a metal process co. which wanted a re-
serve of ore to operate on. The North, always
owned' in Melbourne, was given best by the
old co., and sold for a few hundreds. In the
past seven years these three cos. have paid
dividends as follows :

In addition, the three mines, which were
all staggering on the verge of collapse just
prior to the successful treatment of sulphide
ore, have spent approximately £550, on
plant and other improvements within the
last seven-year period. It ought to make
Australian metallurgists feel a bit more
satisfied with themselves when they remem-
ber that the solution of that problem as
well as of the later problem of
handling the -zinc “tailings,” was carried out
in Australia. Among others a German pro-
fessor of great tonnage was imported to solve
it. He described 25 ways of getting out the
metals, but saw no profit in any of them. If
The Bulletin were asked to name one man
who, more than any other, contributed to the
success of the first problem, it would say T.
.1. -Greenway. In a way, certainly, he was
driven to it. His mines, the South and
Block 14, simply had to tackle the sulphides,
because they had nothing else. But, at any
rate, he was the first man to handle sul-
phides and make a profit out of them.
Thank Heaven, they don’t insult men who do
things like that by making them C.M.G.’s!

From Ardlethan: —
The high price of tin is causing a litle more
interest in the Ardlethan field. Kirk and party
have taken options over the principal alluvial
claims, and are testing with a view to hydraulic
sluicing. The Wild Cherry, now known as the
New Venture, is on a dyke formation with
pines of fair milling values. The Carpathia lately
came on a new lens of good ore, and prospects there
are comparatively cheerful.
* < * *
There is a new wolfram show 28 miles
west of Yolgogrin (N.S.W.) ; 43cwt. of con-
centrates, assaying 6G p.c. tungstic acid,
were obtained from eight tons of ore. That’s
a remarkable yield : compare it with Block
14 Torrington’s 546 tons crushed for 24cwt.
of concentrates. The Torrington Co. has
developed many thousands of tons of ore_ of
a grade that requires from 400 tons to 500
tons of exceptionally hard rock to be mined
and treated to produce one ton of concen-
trate, and 1500 tons of this rock have actu-
ally been crushed and treated as an experi-
ment. Manager Nicholas says the district is
capable of turning out 250 tons of wolfram
concentrates a year, but it would involve a
huge battery equipment. And the require-
ments of munition-makers seem to have been
much exaggerated. No doubt that is why
the price offered for concentrates in Aus-
tralia is very moderate.
H. W. Gepp, now general manager of the
Electrolytic Zinc Co. of Australia and in
charge of the Risdon (Tas.) works, with a
squad of U.S.A. experts to help linn swears
that, if the German submarines will let ms
machinery come along, the first unit or tlie
commercial plant will be at work and turn-
ing out 10 tons of pure zinc a day by August
1. The walls of the electric-cells building
have started to grow, the excavation of a site
for the leaching and purifying plant is nearly
finished, and a hustle is to be put into the
building of the wharf and haulage line and
other odds and ends. The glass cell plant is
already turning out zinc from Broken tlili
ores, and while the 10-ton unit is being fixed
up a plant to produce 2501 b. of zinc a day
(the same size as the experimental plant
with which the Anaconda process was tried
on Broken Hill ores in the U.S.A.) is to be
used for testing purposes. As it is exactly a
year since IV. L. Baillieu first ran his e
over the Ilisdon site to see if there was any
chance of its suiting, the co. hasn’t lost much
time. The latest instance of the kindly in-
terest the Hobart people take in the enter-
prise was when the Hobart Marine Board
proposed to clap on a charge of Gd. per ton
on all ore landed. The co. claims that as it
it building its own wharves without asking
the Board for a penny, and as the Hobart
people will get port dues on the ships, any-
way, this charge is'too steep altogether. It is
likely that there will be a compromise, but
ns the war has knocked down the total ship-
ping entering and clearing from the port of
Hobart by about 7U0,000 tons, the Marine
Board wants something new from some-
where.

Tassy’s hydro-electric schemes are hung
up owing to the English Government refus-
ing permission to export machinery. An in-
quiry from the IJ.S.A. found the makers
opening their mouths too wide. The short-
age of gear is so serious that the Ilobait
Gas Co.’s old steam plant will have to be put
into commission in Hobart this winter.
