A send-off to a big performer: —
The scythe has brought down Alfred Tolhurst.
The lure of the Broken Hill boom took “Tolly” out
of a bank clerk’s job in Maoriland in 1888, and
with Bill Quick to guide him he dashed into specu-
lative life in Melbourne. Two such dandies as Quick
and “Tolly” soon got into the swim with the
Tooraketty crowd. Constitutionally the partners
were as wide apart as the Poles, for Tolhurst was
as dashing as Quick was sober-minded. So it was
ordered that Fred Keats should link up with Tolhurst
vice Quick. The new man had fine fellows like
William Jamieson, W. It. Wilson and other leadeis
of “the Queen-street crowd,” as close chums. So coins
flowed into the offlce-pot in a steady current and
were spent as freely. Tolhurst did not distinguish
himself specially over the early W.A. floats, but
Keats and he were the '(**>• promoting brokers through
the whole of the North Queensland copper boom,
starting with Chillagoe and Mt. Garnet and ending
with Leichhardt. After William Knox had got the
Baillieus into Hampdens, Keats and Tolhurst worked
with that brotherhood. A profitable combination it
was. “Tolly” reckoned that he missed his biggest
chance by selling the Duchess held by Keats and
himself to the Hampden; but he could have raked
in a bigger pile if he had clung on to what he made
out of the Mt. Elliott float: The most disastrous of his
big promotions were Chillagoe and Mt. Garnet. His
last streak of luck was -to listen to Fred Tricks when
the original Edna May syndicate was being formed
to send Hartrick to measure up the claim. That
little investment meant that Tolhurst was all there
when most of the early Westonia melon-cutting went
on. When “Tolly’s” luck was booming his chest
couldn’t be measured. The boys had to walk round
him. But they did so with a kindly eye because
they knew the good nature behind it all.

Melbourne has put some money into the
recently-resurrected Childe Harold, near
Laverton (W.A.), and is already figuring on
early dividends. Years ago a man had a look
at this show in company with -the late
George Hall. On Hall’s assurance that
there was a lot of gold in the stone that
could not be seen with the spy-glass he
bought some shares. He has them yet.
From Westonia: —
Edna May Consolidated has opened up an excel-
lent body of ore at the 210 ft. level. The reef is
over 20ft. wide, and the value about loz.; but so
far it has not been driven on to any extent. There
is another reef at 105 ft., with a width of seven feet
and worth 14dwt. per ton, which has been exposed for
a length of 70ft. The outlook for the mine is
better than it has ever been before. However,
finances are low, and a treatment plant is badly
needed.
Edna May Deeps have jumped from ss. to Bs. 3d.
without any material alteration in the mine. As
the bears have a call of ss. per share to help them
along in the future, the chances are the honors will
be with them. There can be only one end to Deeps:
acquisition by Edna May Co.
Edna May’s No. 5 level is opening up in such a
way that it is pretty certain the bears will get
pinched before the end of the year.
Word-painting cannot hide the fact that
Marv Mac, Laverton (W.A.), has done
badly. If 40,000 tons crushed had averaged
355., with slimes included, instead of 255.,
A. 0. Sachse wouldn't be out after more
capital, and there wouldn’t have been any
necessity to stop a crosscut when- it was
only 40ft. from the lode. To say that “mul-
lock intrusions” are a common occurrence
in big lodes is a bit risky; but Sac-hse is
daring. Otherwise, how could he say that
the fact of the bottom crosscut being in
country carrying 15s. of gold “proves it to
be almost a certainty that good payable
values will be met on intersecting the^lode”?
“Broker” : It is not only what Westralia
thinks about Nullagines, but what Bill Rob-
inson will chirrup from his exalted perch in
London when he hears of the show’s latest
troubles. It isn’t widely known that Dick
Hamilton looked in at Nullagine and nodded
approval. But it was Colin Fraser’s report
that started the show, lie gave Grant’s Flat
348,500 cub. yards of an average of 3s. 2d.
gold, or a total value of £55,179 (proven),
and estimated two other paddocks to contain
250,000 cub. yards carrying £30,000 of gold.
Costs on a dredging duty of 0500 cub. yards
weekly were to be 13d. per cub. yard, and
the total working profit was set down at
£52,761. There certainly is room for careful
inquiry as to why there has been so much
difference between estimates and results in a
proposition where estimates ought to be
nearer the mark than in any other class of
mining venture.

Edna Consolidated shines brightest in the
Westonia firmament at present, but the pre-
sence of greenstone off the 200 ft. crosscut
may foretell an -eclipse. What cannot be
ignored is the flat angle at which that slab of
country rock underlies through the claim
from south to north. This means a big push
of the reef towards the Central boundary ; so
if anyone is to profit by the shoot now being
opened up, it looks as though it must be
manager Giblin’s claim. Perth _ and Ival-
goorlie are raspy over the jump in Consoli-
dateds, but the market is so over-sold and
there are such chances in the show that the'
way is open for a squeeze should some news
of the old Barrambie type. shuffle into
Peacock’s office. #
From the chairman’s speech at the Mt.
Elliott meeting in London :
The proposed basis of operations is the treatment
of 500 tons of ore a day of an assay value of 5 per
cent./ to 6 per cent., producing- roughly, therefore,
about 700 tons of copper a month. Working under
present conditions, when the costs are abnormally
high, it is calculated that the cost of mining,
raising, railing to the smelter and smelting the ore,
and also the cost of shipping the blister copper and
all refining charges, will total about £6O per ton
of copper. It would, therefore, appear that when
in full swing our gross profits should be about
£35,000 a month. 1 say gross profits, because
allowance has also to be made for administration
charges, taxes and depreciation. On that basis we
have a long spell of profitable operations ahead of
us. Just how long depends on the mines themselves
and on the railway extensions we get.
And, please, on the maintenance of prices.
That is the most important x in the problem.
Gympie—a very different Gympie from
what it used to be —produced 30590 z. last
month from 8182 tons of stone. The Scot-
tish Gympie crushed 7900 tons for 22960 z.;
so the others got only 7630 z. between them.
The best returns came from No. 1 North
Columbia, which cleaned up GOOoz. from 74
tons (over Boz. per ton) and declared a
dividend of 68. Previous to that No. 1 had
splashed up a fourpenny from its patch.
Two of Gympie’s best old-time mines, the
No. 2 South Great Eastern and the South
Glanmire and Monkland, are liquidating.
There is plenty of machinery and plant, so
there should be a consolation trifle.
* * * *
Maoriland’s old mine, the Waihi, produced
bullion worth £327,506 last year from
194,223 tons of ore. The latest four-weekly
return is £30,606 from 17,315 tons. This
is good enough, so Waihi is handing out its
quarterly Is. The Grand Junction is
crushing 400 tons in 24 hours and its
extraction is 345. per ton; but the shares
droop because the capital is large and ore
reserves rather slight. It is much the same
with the Waihi. Despite the fact that the
returns are so regular the mines are very
patchy and patchy reserves are very difficult
to value.
Recent rains have set Round Hill (Tas.)
working the mill full time. The co. is ship-
ping a few tons of hand-picked ore from
No. 1 tunnel. An assay gave lead 35.5 p.c.;
silver 2160 z.; gold 14dwt. So _the parcel
should realise somewhere about £5O a ton.
A Hobart crowd, called the Daylight Syn-
dicate, have been for the last two or three
months prospecting a reef close to Mathinna,
about four miles from Fingal (Tas.). They
have sunk 50ft. on stone 2ft. at its narrow-
est. Ten tons sent to Bendigo returned 21oz.
7dwt. of free gold, with 3cwt. of concen-
trates. By cyaniding, the tailings yielded
another sdwt. A further sample of 20 tons
is being sent to the Golden Gate battery.
Cap’n Miles’s Deebook No. 2 dredge, work-
ing at Itenong, Malaya, cut up 330,476 cub.
yards in the last six months. As the ground
went 0.9131 b. of tin per cub. yard, the co.
earned £13,515. Half-pound ground is now
taking the gilt off the gingerbread, but later
Miles, junior, who runs the plant, will be
wallowing in 15oz. ground. Then things are
to hum in royal style! No. 1 dredge is out
of commission: it came to grief some time
ago. Young Miles has been putting in his
spare time retrieving it.
Kampong Kamunting recovered 8 ._6Bd.

two were going to be poorer, by the look of
things. Costs, fortunately, keep down to
about so there was a working profit
of £4839 (G.lld. per yard) for the month.
The little Mount Lindsay tin show (Tas.)
is cheerful about prospects. The manager
took charge last October, and _ since then
driving on the lode has given 251 tons ore,
which returned seven tons concentrates
assaying 70 p.c. tin. The drive is below
old tribute workings and has luckily run
into a rich shoot on the footwall and at the
bottom of the drive. On the strength of
this good make the manager is sending in
another drive nearly at the battery level.
The directors have a little milling plant,
purchased from the tributors.
* * * *
Chairman Fairthorne has been almost a
lifetime on the Board of Mt. Bischoff, and
his cheerful remarks about tackling the lodes
that possibly exist in the big area held by
the co. were quite in the old pioneer spirit.
For the latter half of 1916 tin averaged
£175 Os. 5d., Or £l2 10s. less than during
the previous term, and as the output was
233 tons of concentrates, containing approxi-
mately 140 tons of tin, the lower price made
a difference of £1750, or nearly 3s. per share.
At present tin is worth over £2OO per ton.
Bischoff has been unable to secure carpen-
ters to finish its new 20-head battery
quickly, but the delay will not be for long.
The building of this fine plant, with all the
latest improvements for handling slimes,
shows that Bischoff isn’t short of faith in
its mountain.
* * * *
North Mount Farrell (Tas.) is installing
a 375-li.p. suction gas engine, with which it
expects to save about £2OOO a year in fuel.
The inine is looking well, and the late rains
have replenished the water supply.

Sydney, March 12, 1917.
My Dear Arini. —
The’last word about the Stricklands is
that they will face the sea-pirates together,
and make for England in April. His Ex.
got the final courtesy of leave of absence;
hut he doesn’t return. They talk of set-
tling—for a time, anyhow—at Sizergk, the
ancient home in Westmoreland. It is
alleged to be haunted by a more reliable old
ghost than any other baronial roof in the
North Countree. Whether it is anything
like William Holman I don’t know. In his
dreams his Ex. is pretty sure to see it in
that shape.
The tips about a sub-regal successor no
longer include the Galways; they’re not
even starters. Cullen, C.J., will carry on
until an infusion of fresh blood comes from
the other side. State Governors can be
picked up fairly easily just now, I’m told.
Men with estates in England are looking for
something to relieve the strain of the heavy
taxation. But there is another side to that.
We have our war taxation, too; wherefore
the new man will, when he arrives, be made
to understand that he is a luxury we really
can’t afford.
That bright and capable girl, Miss Mary
Strickland, had to go into Lewisham
Hospital for a few days. The finger that
gave some trouble about three months ago
had to come off. Eldest daughter’s goldi-
locks have spun a web around Australian
hearts, and her little ailment brings a ton
of sympathy.
Sydney welcomed home more bandaged
Kangaroos on Sunday morning. It was a
best blue-and-gold day, with a brisk wind
tossing the white caps on tlfe Harbor. The
Anzac buffet was the rendezvous. With
.Miss Evans in charge, the Y.A.D.’s
greeted the female friend or relative of any
returned soldier as cordially as the rush per-
mitted. No longer does the fierce intimation,
“Ladies not admitted,” frown over the turn-
stile entrance. Queenslanders went on to
Bose Hall, the big brown house on Darling.-
hurst Heights, where matron Fox keeps the
V.A.D.’s on the move. Mrs. Chris Bennett,
the N. S. Wales mother of the voluntaries,
was amongst the visitors, with Commandant
Lee and his wife to extend the glad hand to
Bananaland men. Bose Hall generally gets
hold, of a good band for these Sunday
reunions. As the cars rolled up the red-gravel
path a Fire Brigade band let the boys
have “Home, Sweet Home,” to step out to.
Downstairs, in the innards of the establish-
ment, the V.A.D.’s jingled the crockery and
staggered out to the lawn, where the big fig-
trees shed their slippery fruit, to fill the
Kangaroo with good things.
The recent sale of the Esquimau puppies
which came back on the Aurora reminds
one of the woolly canines on Stewart Island
nine or 10 years ago. I fancy they had been
snapped up as a spec, from the Southern
(Toss. expedition. By selling the puppies at
£5. apiece, the simple settler thought he was
going to make something for the tin savings
bank on the kitchen mantelpiece. But he
forgot the habits of the Arctic dog, first
cousin to the wolf, in idle captivity. Half
Moon Bay echoed with the howls of these
wild beasts o’ moonlight nights. By day.
wherever two or three were gathered
together, they fought'to the last gasp, while
the non-belligerents sat on their haunches to
see the final round. No —you can buy Arctic
dogs, but you can’t teach them even back-
yard habits.
Antill of Jarvisfield, now Brigadier-Gen-
eral, writes from London :
Had to give in for a little, while, influenza and
bronchitis, but feel Al, and after a fortnight at
Bournemouth hope to be back in my place in the
mud again.

C.M.G. before leaving London for the other
side of the Channel.
. Mrs. George Sly, wife of the Judge, is
.amongst the homing pigeons, after about
three years’ absence. Two nice-looking
daughters come with mamma from the other
side.
Chief Justice Cullen took the chair at a
meeting convened to form a committee to
work up a Shackleton lecture in aid of the
widow of Captain Mclntosh, of the recent
expedition. But argue as you may as to
the advantages that man will reap, some
time or other, from the struggles of explorers
among the ice mountains, Sydney remains
lukewarm. So the cane chairs predomi-
nated on Friday, and the company consisted
chiefly of the conveners. General Finn, on
the platform, had at his elbow the lathy
figure of Captain Davis, of the Aurora. This
intrepid sailor is as shy as a bandicoot. But
he jerked out a manly tribute to the grit
and endurance of the late Captain Mclntosh
in a deep sea voice that seemed to resound
with the noise of many waters.
Charles Waldron, the tall actor who
married pretty Miss King, of this hamlet,
arrived with wife and youngsters in patriar-
chal style. A mountain of baggage followed
the family out to Auntie Brunton's. The big
house that stands on the rim of Double Bay
will house the party.
I tried 2s. 2d. worth of Gonsalez opera-
singers at the second of their Town Hall
concerts. The 2d. was the amusement tax.’
which is, of course, the public’s funeral.
Side-lines in this back area-of our muni-
cipal barn are the infant-in-arms, who yells
at inopportunte moments, generally when
the favorite tenor is letting it go on a top
C, and the scrunch of paper bags. Peanuts
and boiled lollies are occasionally inter-
changed between the shilling and two-bob
seats. Very nearly all the applause comes
from these plebeian parts. Cappelli, Balboni
and Miss Gonsalez are the favorites. Miss
liuss still hangs on to her tremolo. Garbed
in duck-egg blue, she wore a rosette rather
smaller than a soup-plate over one ear.
Soprano de Revers —she who can an she
will shake the welkin —appeared with her
head apparently tied up in shells and sea-
weed. I say apparently. It looked mer-
maidy from the 2s. 2d. area. Cacialli was,
as ever, a topnotcher. __ But the average
Italian opera-singer doesn’t do more than
his third best in swallow-tails, supported by
a bleak background of empty platform seats.
Lieut. Fred Busby, only son of a Wool-
lahra Point family, has died in France. Hi's
mother, Mrs. Busby, was Augusta Merewether
in her single days. An aunt of the young
lieutenant is Mrs. John Donkin, of Lake
Cowal, Wyalong; her son is also in khaki.
Another of the clan, Darcy Donkin, lucky
owner of Meteor Downs station in Queens-
land, started olf recently for active service
in the aircraft line. He was an expert at
polo and most other expensive sports.
Thomas Graythwaite Dibbs and his help-
mate shook the glad hand of many a rela-
tive and long-time friend at Point Piper on
Sunday. There was plenty of iced food and
much congratulation because of the diamond
jubilee of the fine old couple’s wedding-day.
Lights will twinkle in the gardens of Dun-
vegan, Mosman, on. the 24th, when the
Mae!cod girls will make a special effort to
help the 17th Battalion Comforts Fund.
And on the 27th Mrs. D. Sheridan will give
a party at the sth Brigade Artillery depot
in Carringto'n-street in aid of that little lot’s
fund.
I fere is the .backbone of the Red Cross sauce
department—Mrs. Joscelyne. She learned the
art of making everything, except the weather
and the mutton, in a bush home years ago.
When the boys in Egypt or France sing a
song of thanksgiving for any of the 54 tons
of condiments that come their way from
Ash-street, they incidentally praise the ver-
satile Australian woman. Amongst Her
other activities Mrs. Joscelyne organised
the North Sydney
chemists’ wives. In
consequence, the
soldiers’ dependants
up that way get
their drugs free,
and their North
Sydney centre is
amazingly popular
with the little
things that Tommy
left behind him.
With a brief pull-
off recently for ill-
health, this slender
bundle of energy
has worked all day
and every say as
lion, chef of the
Ash-street pickle
premises. Military
hospitals here get their share of -the output.
The rest goes to garnish bully beef at the
Front.
Mentioned in dispatches for valuable ser-
vices, Sister J. Baxter, a daughter of the
Baxters of Glebe Point. She trained at our
Coast Hospital and for the last two years
has been with our armv in Egypt " and
France. Recent work on the frontier inva-
lided her to England, and now her nursing
hours are spent at Southall Hospital.
Society w-ise and otherwise rushed St.
Stephen’s to see the beauty bride, Dora
-Alexander, hand her liberty over to Leslie
Walford. w'ho for the second time on earth
promised the usual things. Jeanette Gordon
shed further glory over the ceremony in her
fuchsia-toned gown of varying shades. She
looked handsome enough to have been
carried off .as a bridal posy. Two nights
prior to his second binding the bridegroom
was feasted at Paris House by 50 admirers.
But, of course, they didn’t see the trousseau.
It was just scrumptious!
Sydney Hospital, which has just had its
first" 1917 directors’ meeting, has Matthew
Harris as president. On the domestic side
the ’Orspital has descended from its former
lofty attitude towards the useful V.A.D. It
has recently asked to have the loan in the
busy mornings of some of these hard-working-
young char-ladies. The lasses take a fort-
night at a stretch. One dainty damsel tells
me she doesn’t char all the time: she has
been promoted to feed the more helpless
patients. The butterfly brigade, which cast
a flimsy chiffon shadow' over the first V.A.D.
movement, soon fainted away before the orgy
of wmshing-up w'hich is the voluntary’s lot
here below. So the hospital that begs the
Young Person to come over and help now
generally gets a mighty energetic char-girl—
gratis.
After five years’
absence from his
native land, violin-
ist Lionel Lawson
stepped off a mail-
boat the other day.
He came from Eng-
land to see his
father, and then—
off to the war! If
possible he will go
from an Australian
aviation school. If
that is too long a
wait, he will go
anyhow the autho-
rities will take
him. “I’d rather go
if only to wash
dishes,” he says,
“than stay at home.” This well-set-up,
high-spirited boy knows Germany. He had
had two years’ study in the Berlin Conserva-
toire when the wild animal broke loose
in 1914, and he knows what it is likely to
do before it is driven back into its cage.
The little .photo shows Lionel Lawson
now just short of his nineteenth birthday.
Chatting of music and other things (prin-
cipally other things) he says: “As I saw
the German professor in Berlin, he was of
a type very different from the aggressive
military caste there. lie was generally a
fairly genial chap with his head at all sorts
of odd moments over a. pot of something or
other. Meals seemed to be always going on.
The average German oats in a very messy
way, and sops his bread or biscuits into
any liquid that’s handy. The German
woman is crushed—but. she doesn’t know
it. So in the home there’s peace of the
kind we’d get here if they won the war!
Even when I was there freedom was un-
known in the Kaiser’s kingdom. In this
sense —if you’re only there six days they
want to know all about you, and they can
put their hand on you at a moment’s notice
if they want you. They wanted me badly
when the war came; but we—my mother
and sister were with me —had escaped
to England.” While there, Lionel, besides
helping at concerts for the wounded, had a
few successful affairs on his own account.
“It’s all nonsense to condemn the amuse-
ments that go on in London. iilhey’re
mostly to cheer the men from the trenches,
and the English peonle as a whole are now
working magnificently -for the war. In
fact, it’s the war all the time. If some of
the slackers could see the ‘wounded coming
back from France, or the funerals of women
and babies killed in Zep. raids, as I have
done, they wouldn’t be long out of khaki.”

'Our old friend Southwell, who had about
a year to live when Sydney hurled a hand-
some cheque at his pocket in 1914, is looking
very well and fit. This news comes from a
recently-returned Australian, who saw a
good deal of him in London. Paling’s for-
mer concert-manager does a lot of honorary
management for patriotic shows in tin l Fog,
and butters a crust for himself with journal-
istic work. So there’s no word of his return.
A few weeks ago I gave you the bones of
a romantic story concerning Michel Domer-
ehikoff, a Russian naval officer who was in
Sydney a few years ago. He is still piling
up adventures that will take the stiffening
out of merely sensational fiction. The gun-
boat he commanded was recently torpedoed,
and he and "some of the crew were eventu-
ally saved after an icy immersion. At latest
advices Michel was sampling much hospi-
tality from the Y.M.C.A. near the scene of
his dip. He married a Sydney girl, Miss
Fussel, as you may remember. She is get-
ting her share of thrills, and travelled alone
half round Asia to meet her husband in
Russia. While Michel, who has acquired a
barrow-load of decorations, was hanging on
to a raft, after the submarine encounter, a
little “Dom” was born —I think in Odessa.
It was a very small party that met at the
Carlton the other afternoon, after a quiet
wedding in St. Stephen’s. The bride was
Muriel Davidson, who accepted a plain gold
ring from Captain Tyrie. A.M.C., formerly
of Westralia. The Wagga girl was frocked
m a tailor-built cream suit.
At a recent gathering of Thrift dames one
member of the party began to ask what the
Thing really was, and what it wasn’t. “For
instance,” said the doubting Thomasina,
“you say that you can't sell good home-made
cake under Is. a lb., present price of ingredi-
ents. Then you prepare to make and sell it.
in Thrift week, at that figure. If I can buy
cake, shop or factory made, that serves my
purpose, at, say, Gd. a lb., then am I a
thrifty person to spend double the money on
yours?” At this crisis the conversation
veered round to less explosive arguments.
The ghost of the Soudan expedition—the
white donkey—is once more in print. I
saw him (or is it a lady?) amongst the
Duntroon dandelions a while ago; and the
Canberra area is still the home of the plump
pensioner. I fancy the late Bob Nathan, a
member of the futile expedition, handed the
donkey over to the Campbells, then living
in the big homestead which is now included
iii the military-college grounds.

centre last week, and was handed £75 to dis-

the Jutland fight. The cash was acquired
at a recent concert given by the Naval Com-
rades’ Association..

If local society had more time for frills
it might try the chopstick as a diversion at
the evening meal. While the Taisei Maru
was in port, our Best People made a few
hurried efforts to consume soup and other
slithery sustenance with the aid of the
inadequate sticks. Only a few well-informed
citizens, who had learnt the trick in Japan,
seized both weapons in the right hand. This
leaves the other digit free to scoop up the
food and hurl it on to the ivory tools. You
can’t do it in your best gloves—it might give
the soup a flavor. A chopstick demonstra-
tion would be a lively item at a comforts
fund show. Anyone would part with a shil-
ling to see, say, a Public Service Board epi-
cure juggling with the prongs.
Alfred Hill is now definitely in charge of
the Apollo Club, which used to be the Liede.r-
tafel. In the long ago he shook very good
music out of the old Lied. Now many of
the Apollos are warbling—or cursing—in
the muddied fields of France ; but there are
a few old stagers left to supply the Club
with its deep Aniens. .
Before we begin to breakfast by gaslight
on dull mornings, the clock is to be adjusted.
The poultry-yard, which refuses to go to roost
by the daylight-saving time, and the equally
insubordinate cow will not have their domes-
tic affairs upset after March 25.
There’s a chance of your old coat and
skirt—if it was made by a good tailor —
looking fairly in the picture by June or July.
If it is not too tube-like in the skirt it will
be an inconspicuous garment. The very
wide drapery is on the wane. The male
cutter never liked it, and it’s Scissors that
rules the fashion-plates.
Where do all the hairpins go? At one
women’s club, otherwise well catered for,
members may be seen making frantic efforts
to scratch skewers out of all kinds of crevices.
Pins are equally priceless in moments of dis-
tress. The famine in toilet-tools at most
women’s clubs suggests the dreadful thought
that night-watchmen use the crinkly weapons
to clean their pipes. But at the Queen’s
there’s always plenty. Your good bridge-player
couldn’t stand the mental fag of hiding the
nuisance in her mouth while she put her
marcel waves into a fresh bun. But the
bone-bare appearance, of the ordinary femi-
nine club’s retreat is one of the burning
questions of the hour.
Artist Tindall’s eldest son has been wed in
Launceston to a local girl. Grace Cookman.
The bride had a presentation from the choir
of St. John’s. Young Tindall is doing black-
and-white work in Melbourne.
Kitty Kennedy, a niece of Father Shaw, of
the Australian “wireless” experiments, has
married Leslie Quigley, a son of the John
Quigleys, of Widgerie Waa station, Trangie.
The knot was tied at the Sacred Heart.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, March 12, 1917.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Australian Cup brought out the mil-
liners’ butterflies. The first flight—the ones
who came to lunch-parties—were smothered
in furs because of the suspiciously misty
morning; but the later arrivals were in
summer gear. Mrs. Clive Leonard was a
vision of white ninon. Mrs. Marlow (Syd-
ney) looked pale after her illness in a fawn
I rock. Airs. Hocking (Westralia) was in
navy with a Tuscan hat and equal to any
sort of weather. Airs. Hawker (Adelaide)
splashed big black spots on her white fur-
trimmed ninon. Airs. Charlie Waters pro-
claimed herself a Sydney woman by dis-
trusting our Melbourne autumn in dark
navy tailorings with a big pink velvet collar.
Mrs. J. A. Collins (Auckland) was in dove
grey from hat to shoe-tips. The two Rouse
girls (Sydney) walked the lawn in pretty
girlish frocks —Marjorie in pink and white
ninons cut in the Empire style. Airs. L. K.
S. Alackinnon had bright blue laurel leaves
in her hat. Aliss Alackinnon floated in voile
that flared to a yard-wide hem and sup-
ported a rustic hat composed of wild-flower
wreaths. Mrs. Philip Russell, in white
cloth tailorings, received the heaps of con-
gratulations showered on her after her hus-
band’s win. Alr.s. Doolette was in black
taffeta, and Airs. Eric Connolly wore a lovely
little frock of black taffeta embroidered iu
gold.
The last hurrah of the V.R.C. Autumn
Aleeting was shouted and the last curse mut-
tered at Flemington on Saturday. Sassiety
went home with its gold chain-bag very loose
and floppy, for the handy half-crowns all
vanished with the defeat of numerous hot
favorites by “a nose” or “half a nose.” Why
don’t they breed favorites with extra long
beaks? 1 The very sick Leslie Stuart had a
tonic presented to him by his horse Fire-
works, which won the Autumn Steeplechase
from Flash Bob —the only other animal to
finish on its legs.
Airs. Bertie Knight, visiting from Singa-
pore, put fine silk tailorings into the pie-
uire. She used to be one of the Ben
Osbornes, of N. S. Wales. Airs. Ranclaud
wore fluffy brown furs over her light silk
frock. Airs. Dick Hawker v'oro navy tailor-
ings of a smart cut. Edith Hill, from Syd-
ney, watched the horses in the paddock from
out of rainy-day togs while standing in a
wonderful pair of patent-leather boots. Airs.
Yicol, who saw Rael Locin nearly win the
last race, was in cloth called “midnight
blue.” Airs. Isaacs, the Judge’s wife, wore
startling black and white, and daughter
Nancy _ white and gold. Airs. Dudley
Brahani from Perth found a duck of a Dutch
blue hat and wore it with her grey togs.
Airs. Reggie Clarke was in black, and Airs.
Sep Aliiler in blue. Airs. Byron Aloore
sailed about under spread wings of white
lace in a black hat.
A larger bunch than usual of Sydney
sports are over for the Autumn gallops.
Theo Marks (architect), Harry Chisholm
(horse-flesh), Jimmy Nott Osborne (wool),
John Spencer Brunton (flour), Ben Osborne
(sheep), AlcArthur Onslow (politics and
Polycrates), and John Brown (coal) are
amongst those punters who have come to
take a fall out of the bookies. Amongst the
dressers I notice Mrs. Ken Richards and
her youthful mother, Airs. Alaiden; Mrs.
Ben Osborne, with her pal Airs. Wilkinson;
Edith Hill, the statuesque heiress; and
little May Alilne, who shows children how
to point the toe.
Squatting widow Clifton, of Darling
Downs, is in Alelbourne for the races. Aliss
Johnston, a one-time Bananaland journalist,
is flatting with her a,t the Lincoln house, in
Spring-street. Lieut. Ralph Clifton has just
sent mother details of his marriage. The
soldier got eight days’ leave, and hurried off
to the parson with his Australian girl. He
married Ida Boyd, daughter of Alajor Boyd,
of Brisbane. Miss Boyd wrote for the
Courier for many years, and now that her
husband has gone back to France she is
making copy for English papers from
Weymouth.
Airs. Glenloth Peters is in town, but is
wearing quiet, dark garb and not looking
towards Flemington, where her race frocks
are usually a sensation. Uncle “Ottawa”
Campbell’s death and the passing of the two
fine Campbell sons in France (all within a
few months) have sent her into social retire-
ment.
«
Lawyer Leslie Stuart is seriously ill, and
the autumnal saddling-paddock at Fleming-
. ton has missed him. Airs. Stuart has
turned herself into nurse-in-chief and left
her race frocks unpacked in their tissue-
paper wrappings. The Stuarts are house-
keeping at the gorgeous home of the Fred
Fairbairns, which they took over when the
Fairbarns raced to England.
The Italians came back on Saturday. The
Princess’s was packed with Sassiety and
enthusiasm, for tfie handsome Cappelli was
starring in “II Trovatore.” The best music-
eaters sat it out like a deaf and dumb asy-
lum, and at the finale Cappelli was the only
star who received flowers. It was almost a
tragedy to Russ and de Revers, who had
wailed and warbled with him. Not even a
voice jujube was handed to the poor things.
Cappelli hugged his flower-garden and jabbed
a white rose under tbe protesting patrician
nose of Scamuzzi. He then threw posies to
friends in the stalls and beamed and showed
Ids white teeth in endless smiles. De Revers
made her hit in black velvet and very white
muscular arms. She looked as if she could
have used force to gaoler Scamuzzi when
that handsome baritone was arguing the
point with her through pages and pages of
“score.”
Scamuzzi strode about in silver armor
and a white linen cloak. The meaning of
that flapping, loose white cloak-of-opera is
now clear to me since the Canadians’ famous
nightdress raid; it is for disguise in the
snow. The pretty little Russ was dis-
guised under the brown stain and gipsy dress
of the mother. Still she managed to get in
some brilliant eye-play in the direction of
her front-stall adorers while she lay resting
on the uncomfortable prison couch.
The season was to have started with a
reception-matinge on Saturday; but some
hitch in the transport of the scenery inter-
fered with the arrangements. The gaol walls,
etc., were thrown into place only just in
time for the evening performance. This
theatrical jerrybuilding led to some queer
by-play in the prison scene. Cappelli had to
rush to the heavy bars of the window and
shake them in a frenzy. He nearly pulled
the whole penitentiary about his ears. Some
prompt stage-hands saved the edifice from
collapse. But the extreme tremolo of de
Reverb voice shook it all the tune.
Victoria’s Ancient Mariners propose to
plough the charity seas of Melbourne for
six days, beginning on Monday, the 19th.
On the 20th they will give their own particu-
lar form of entertainment at the Town
Hall, at which Governor and Lady Stanley
will be present and the chanteys will be
selected with the strictest regard for polite
society. The Mariners gave a similar show
at the Athenaeum Hall last year, when the
proceeds enabled the management to send
pipes and tobacco to over 1000 mine-sweepers
on the North Sea. The top price for tickets
(ask at the bookstalls) is only 2s. Gd., and
anyone who doesn’t want a ticket can give
a bit for the boys who are up against the
submarine menace. On Button Day (March
23) the ladies’ committee will run a kiosk
outside the Town Hall and a cafe chantant
inside.
In frugal France all patriotic endeavor
must be conducted with the strictest
economy and on the most up-to-date busi-
ness lines by order of the Government,
which takes no one and nothing on trust.
Even the most handsomely-coroneted dames
.who run soldiers’ comforts bureaus have to
account for every centime spent, and the
secretary has to keep the books written up
and the cash balanced, for police auditors
may drop in at any moment, and the slight-
est inaccuracy results in the unfortunate
official being hauled into court. Rather a
contrast to the methods obtaining in Mel-
bourne ! One committee administering pub-
lic funds had been buying tea from retail
firms until a new member brazenly suggested
the vulgar plan of getting the stuff wholesale
—a money-grubbing idea which was met with
a tired, contemptuous smile of opposition by
the original fumblers. But when the exas-
perated new member came back with a
quotation from a wholesale grocery and
showed the opposition that a saving of three
shillings in the dozen pounds could be made,
they showed signs, of intelligence—even of
gratitude. A lot of that sort of waste has
been going on.
Mrs. Richardson, of Lockinge. Kew, has
had a bushranging party for the Red Cross.
It lasted for an afternoon and an evening.
Olive Godwin, Kirby. Irma Caron, Norah
Delaney and Strella Wilson manned a first-
class concert programme, and billiardist
Lindrum played exhibition games in the
evening.
Mrs. A. E. V. Richardson took her fine
voice and a string orchestra along to Mat-
lock House Hospital on Saturday, and gave
the soldier patients a treat. Mrs. Richard-
son used to charm Adelaide as Lilian Lucas.
Who would be a councillor when he can
be a captain? The cabled news of Charles
Atkins’s promotion to the three-star rank
made all Hennessy’s men stick out their
waistcoats last week and talk about the
coming downfall of the Hun. Atkins is
with the South Lancashires because Pearce’s
Department wouldn’t find him anything to
do with the Australians. While he is on
the job of cleaning up the Kaiser he has
extended leave to neglect the metropolitan
efrains and dustboxes.
When the Gaud Mayor gave a party to
returned soldiers he took the Theatre Royal
and the big “Intolerance” picture for the
evening. The Patriotic Leaguers who toil
at packing goods in the basement of the
Town Hall were also asked. The Hennessy
dame in black, sparkling with jet and dia-
monds, sat in the front circle with her hus-
band. General Sellheim in highly-decorated
khaki and Mrs. Sellheim in black, with
hair to match, and sparkling eyes, lightened
the gloom alongside. Commander and Mrs.
Tickell sat close handy. Mrs. Norman
Brookes was with the Harry JSummertons,
and Ivy Deakin-Brookes sat with her hus-
band. A neat bunch of returned nurses
chattered in the stalls, the white nun-coifs,
scarlet cloth capes and grey frocks making
a great splash of color. These sisters of
Billjim were motored to the theatre in open
cars at the head of the marching regiment of
soldier-guests.
Mrs. Goodall, of Middle Brighton, has
been in a state of pot-and-kettle ecstasy ever
since 10 o’clock on Saturday night, when
Mrs. Eric Connolly ran round to tell her she
had won the superb kitchen—Mrs. Con-
nolly’s own contribution to the Brighton fes-
tival. It was raffled by. her to the cheery
tune of £478.
War works all sorts of changes. An Anzac
colonel, who threw up a legal job to get into
khaki, lately strayed from the Roman lines
into the Anglican camp. The 'version hap-
pened in Egypt, and the Bishop of Jerusalem
swore in the new recruit.
Melbourne has another skirted recruiting-
speaker. Her name is Nellie Martyn, and
her farther runs the Steel Co. of Australia at
Brunswick. During the daytime Miss
Martyn takes a hand in managing dad’s busi-
ness, and climbs the platform or the soap-
box at nights. Her only previous speaking
experience was with the A.W.N.L.
A small Sassiety flutterer with large soulful
eyes is crying them out because she has been
refused a passport to England. “I do so
want to go and do my pit” is her plaint—
after two years’ frivolling here. What does
she want to do, anyhow—build battleships,
or weary wounded soldiers with chatter?
Died of wounds in France, Lieut. E.
Keith Carlile, son of the Knight of South
Yarra. The young man had only recently
arrived at the Front after spells of service
at Rabaul and various training camps. Many
a Billjim will miss him badly; for he was
one of the kindly sort, who never disguised
a deep concern for his subordinates’ comfort.
Keith has left a brother in the mud of the
Somme and a sister patching casualties near
by.
Robert Bruce (descended in a direct line
from the spider-watcher) is going to Eng-
land via America to do his bit. Mrs. Bruce
(who was one of the two handsome Jackson
sisters) is accompanying him as far as New
York.
General Kirkpatrick’s wife is no longer
plain “Mrs.,” a title having fallen on our
late Inspector-General. In a recept letter
to a Melbourne friend she described the
delights of launching pretty daughter
Georgie on Simla society, and added that
the General was “straining at the leash” to
get off to the war. But the Viceroy and
the Commander-in-Chief -were holding the
other end of the string and assuring the
General’s wife that “they could not possibly
do without him.”
Rumor insists that “Better-Not” Walling-
ton is engaged to be married to a duchess.
But cautious Australians won’t cheer until
the news is officially confirmed. It would
almost choke Wally to say “I will,” without
any sort of qualification or reservation.
While on her way to London, via Maori-
land, Mab Gutteridge was awakened in her
hotel bedroom by the dulcet voice of a burg-
lar whispering : “Don’t scream and I won’t
hurt you !” Mab opened her eyes and looked
into the muzzle of a revolver; but the next
instant she had struck it from the raider’s
hand, and a disarmed burglar was plunging
towards the window with a very angry lady
hanging on to his coat. He did his frantic
best to get away, but Mab is the big sister
of fighting doctors, and she managed to
entertain him till a policeman came up.
The Law got him just as he slipped over the
window-ledge.
The University boys in khaki gave a
return dance party on Saturday to some
girl hostesses who partied them at East St.
Kilda a month or two ago. The hosts took
the Gregory Hall, ol' All Saints (it has a
perfect floor for dancing), and saw to it
that there were plenty of partners. Miss
Embling was in a frilly white frock all
tipped and edged with silver lace. The sweet
little Roth well girl appeared in pink tulle
and ripped her frock from hem to waist
before the party was an hour old ; but she
got pinned up with dozens of small
“safeties,” had her tears dried and enjoyed
the rest of the party. Mrs. Embling, in a
pale jade green gown and tawny hair, came
as a chaperone. Mrs. Joske was another
overseer in black lace. Mrs. Roth well stayed
ar home on the sick list because of an acci-
dent. with a bursting stove while she was
playing locum tenens for a departed cook.
Percy Leason, one of the best of Victoria’s
younger water-colorists, and the illustrator
of “Here is Faery,” has taken his brushes to
Sydney, there to reside permanently. In
the intervals of work he is trying to size up
the banquet of studio “stuff” provided by
Nature. But he is luckier than etcher John
Shirlow, who could only find a holiday week
in which to look round the inexhaustible
harbor. Then he had to pack his bag and
come home lamenting. Melbourne is a fine
city, but .Man made it alone. Providence
collaborated with Man in the mixing of the
northern rival.
Maggie Knight, now with a J.O.W. co. in
Brisbane, has just heard that her young son
Bombardier Bruce Plenderson has been
severely wounded in France, and is in
the County of Middlesex Hospital, London.
The young man put in two years in the
field before being bowled over, and he had
to charge the recruiting-officer three times
before he could pass. But strict attention
to physical culture got him into khaki at
19. He used to be a clerk in the Royal Bank
here.
Borne away on the idea that every stage
amateur carries a leading-lady’s contract
in her powder-sack, Allie Robson and the two
Handley girls have left to try their luck in
the U.S.A. The trio, who have been appear-
ing frequently with Gregan McMahon, had
an idea of also visiting the Cold Country,
but officials negatived the proposal with
enthusiasm. Ethel Leon, who had originally
intended to join the party, finally decided
that Australia was good enough for her.
Minnie Waugh-Orr has come back from
her long Maoriland honeymoon tour, and is
at work once more in her voice studio. She
sang in Wellington while she was away, but
said “Not this time, thank you,” to an
Auckland appeal. Her iron merchant was
then heading for Melbourne, and she wanted
to accompany his sea-song.
No sound in the social world -but the irri-
tating click-click of knitting-needles and
mutterings about cotton. An occasional
petticoated tea is organised, but things gener-
ally are so dull that a few desperate Misses
are seeking excitement on the Yarra Bank.
One leading lady in soap-box oratory is the
eldest daughter of Weigall, Iv.C. She lately
harangued able-bodied Sunday promenaders,
and their best girls from the roof of an apple
case (“O Eve!”), and had to answer so
many interrupters that she became hoarse—
the next worst tiling to being struck dumb.
Of course, the listeners enjoyed themselves
thoroughly. I don’t know how many recruits
she got.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

AGENT: “I can sell you several household patents that will save your wife and daugh-
ters a lot of time and energy."
COCKY: “Wot I'd like t'r know is 'ow are they gain' t'r make use of th' time an' energy

“M.P.A.”: I’ve seen big loads drawn by
horses, bullocks, camels and traction engines, but
for. the long, strong pull give me elephants every
time. If “Lowan” (B. 22/2/’I7) had seen a few
pull-outs in boggy ground by a team of elephants
attached to 1 t tons of heavy gear he would have
kicked the bucket ere this. I saw Wirths’ elephants
' Alice, Lizzie, G’ardy, Jumbo, Little Alice, Molly and
the recently-departed Toby, the champion puller of
the team—simply run away with a big jinker, loaded
with 17 tons of wet canvas and other gear, and the
wheels never revolved. The whole concern just tore
up the bog like a plough. As a puller Toby stood
alone in Australasia. She could shift the aforesaid
jinker from a dead start on a level road. Watching
war movies, showing- squads of men shifting heavy
guns, I could never understand why elephants are
not used. In shifting the circus in all weathers
the big fellows are always up to the toil, and score
where bullocks or horses would fail, because they
can push as well as pull. One good Jumbo is as
good as eight horses any day. In the winter their
hay allowance is always increased. They pull with
a heavy leather breast-plate and chains, and the pull
is worked on different lines to the teaming of horses.
With (say) a team of seven elephants in a drag
out of boggy ground three would be harnessed team
fashion, two attached to the front wheels, and two
big weighty chaps “dulling” on the back wheels.
This method was employed in drawing out a traction
engine that had got bogged in the heavy shingle
of the Tuki Tuki River in Maoriland, I’d like
“Lowan” to see what Wirths’ elephants would do
to 12 tons of wool. If the waggon wouldn’t stand
up to the tug they’d simply lift the bales off and
land ’em at the railway siding. Instructions would
be entirely unnecessary. “Lowan” and “Spraggs”
may have their nags every time, but elephants for
me. If Geo. and I’hil Wirth had to depend on horses
to shift their show it would never get half the one*
night stands done. *
* * * *
“McNorth” : Black brother isn’t surprised
at’ the wonders of civilisation to any great extent.
-He is too philosophical. 1 once asked: a far-outback
nig. if he didn’t think matches were wonderful things
to make fire with. lie replied indifferently: “Oh,
matchiss he nothing; he fashion belong white man.”
He blamed (lie things he didn’t understand on to the
white man as a matter of course, and let it go at
that. A Papuan who had accompanied his boss on
a trip south was asked what he considered the most
wonderful thing in Sydney. The fuzzy-headed child
of nature, unimpressed by the motors, trams, picture-
shows, ferries and all the rest of it, answered that it
was. marvellous how all the people of the city lived,
as hot one of them seemed to have a garden. He
reckoned, also, the people were more or less dilly
because they put on their ciothes in , the daytime
when it Was hot and took them off at night when it
was cold. A New Guinea man, being sane, reverses
the process.
* * * *
“Apis”: There is a lamp-post in a busy
Prahran (Vic.) street against which the local Aus-
tralian, however casual, does not lean. It is an
iron beehive. There are many gardens, to say noth-
ing of the municipal destructor, in the neighborhood,
and a working party of bees have made the lamp-
post their impregnable home. They have their exits
and their entrances by a small hole 12in. from the
base of the post, around which the autochthonous (6 to
4 that one) small boy gnashes his sweet tooth and
rages in vain.
* * * *
Bindyi : After he had been half an hour
complaining about the price of wheat I asked him
how the missus was. “Oh, orl right,” he said.
Happened to meet him next day. “I told you wrong
yesterday,” he explained. “The missus is not orl
light; she s pretty crook. I asked her lars’ night.”
“Leo Lear” : One of our most interesting
birds is the spine-tailed swift, whose nearest relation
is the humming-bird. Swifts are not absolutely
Australian, as they spend our winter in Northern
Asia and nest there. Then
before the great freeze settles
down on the lonely land at
the top, they turn south ir
countless hordes, skimming
over China, the islands and
seas to summer in Australia,
Tasmania and Maoriland. This
migration is centuries old,
and must date from the Jime
this continent stretched much
further east than it does at
present. Thus the birds learnt
the existence of Maoriland.
In Bananaland the swifts
usually arrive at the begin-
ning of November, though
I’ve seen them' a full month
earlier. In Southern Victoria
it is often March before any'
are sighted—and then look-
out for a change in the
weather t 1 don’t want to •
advance their claims as rain-
prophets, but I’ve never seen
the birds here unless an atmo-
spheric change was ’ about.
Almost invariably they appear
with a falling barometer. On
a hot January day on the
Queensland coast I’ve sat in
the shade and looked up
through flight on flight of
swifts, the lowest skimming
the ground with a loud swish,
the top ranks just dots on the
white cumulus clouds. Then
in a minute they were gone,
in what direction I didn’t
know-—they just disappeared.
The birds are wonderful fliers
—swift, as their name indi-
cates, and tireless. They are
reputed never to settle, though
they sometimes do cling to
cliffs. Has any Abo. seen a
spine- tail settle? And if so,,
where and when? If they do
happen to come to earth their
wings are so long and legs so
short that rising would be ex-
tremely difficult. Though one
of our fastest movers, their
bones are not hollow like those of most birds, so
bang goes the theory that hollow bones are a neces-
sity for buoyancy in flight.
* * * »
“Leo Lear”: “Reynard” (B. l/2/’l7)
asks information re nesting localities of white ibis.
I his bird is certainly not as common as the straw-
necked variety, but I’ve seen flocks running into
hundreds on low, swampy land. They work across
the land for insects, etc., in a face like a mob of
merino sheep on the browse. One noted camp of
• white ibis was in a large swamp on Darlot’s Creek
(S.W. Vic.), within a mile of my house." From
all appearances they had nested here for centuries,
and in the season could be seen in. all stages from
eggs to first flighters. However, in 1910 the swamp
was drained and the ibis departed ; but last spring
about 30 returned and started another rookery in
the reeds on the creek". I can see them flying over
.their nests from my kitchen window.
“Bedourie”: “Barcop’s” tale about the
cattle-drowning incident in the Georgina River, about
February, 1904, is all wrong. I was on the spot, so
I ought to know. The mob of 1500 had come all the
way from Wologorang (N.T.) in charge of drover
Rankin, and were supposed to be regular water-dogs.
When they arrived at Bedourie, on the Georgina, the
river was in high flood and the drover was advised to
put 50 or 00 over first, to give confidence to the
remainder. Not he! He said that they had been
reared on the coast where the country was princi-
pally lagoons and inlets from the sea, and he pushed
on. The next thing was that the beasts started to
ring in shallow water, and it was with great difficulty
that , they were persuaded to enter the deep water.
When they were at last jambed into the current, 30
or 40 abreast, with a depth of 20ft., they had no
chance to ring. The consequence was the ones behind
were trampling those in front and drowning them.
When Rankin got a count on the other side he was
exactly 310 short. For fully half an hour after the
last animal had landed safely, carcases in lots of
10, 12 and 15 would rise to the surface all entangled
together and go floating down the stream. Tom
Tracey in 1890 lost 60 head of bullocks at Cooper's
Creek, crossing Windorab, in exactly the same
manner, and all old drovers will remember this.
Four experienced men were helping him—Johnny
Craigie, Jim Lawson, Dave Suttie and Pat Condon.
Which seems to hint that experience isn’t always
the marble to back.
* * * *
“Poddy” : Any Abo. tell me why none of
our native birds seem to like the common or garden
slug? All the hills which lie between the Northern
and Southern Gippsland lines, when cleared and
sown down with cocksfoot, clover, rye, etc., become
infested with slugs in the early autumn and winter.
The ground is covered with them in the early morn-
ing, yet none of the native birds seem to tackle them
at all. ' I was digging spuds the other day in com-
pany with the usual kookaburra, rohin and grey
thrush, and though they readily ate any grub or
worm that turned up they- point-blank refused slug,
white, black or brindle. Whaffor?
Fiank Snodgrass” : The alarming reports
of the ravages of the blowfly on sheep and lambs
inland should cause any practical man to seek for a
remedy. As the various dips are not being qu jt e
ANY PORT IN A STORM.
TICKET-COLLECTOR ( sternly, to stowaway,
who has produced a defunct ticket):
“Here! This is no good — there's no
date on it!”
STOWAWAY ( desperately ): “What yer
cornin' at! Can’t yer see that yous
railway blokes has been punchin’ it all
the way from Bourke to Sydney, and
YE'VE CUT THE BLEEDIN' DATE OUT!”
satisfactory and traps not at all able to cope
with the danger, I have no hesitation in suggesting
the following mixture. Of course, pastoralists have
their own hobbies and can “make their own
pomade,” as it were, but it must have these in-
gredients
1 kerosene-tin of fat or lard.
1 quart of turpentine.
1 quart of Stockholm-tar.
11b. of beeswax.
Now add from 21b. to 41b. of insecticide or insecti-
bane. Render them all down, stir well and apply as
any practical man knows how to do. In small flocks
or specially valuable studs this would be very
efficacious and valuable. It kills the fly on contact
as well as fly-deposit. The mixture is not expensive,
and the ingredients are ready at hand on a sheep
station—except perhaps the insecticides, and those
the nearest general store will have in stock.

“A-wa” : The boss on the Solomon Island
plantation was a typical Western Queenslander, six-
foot-two, and he had a sick “boy” on his hands.
When the big man came into the hut he said:
“Well, Charley, you better little bit to-day?” “No
more, master. No matter you give me medicine, he
no good ; me going to die finish.” Said the boss in
a voice of thunder, with his fist half an inch off the
boy’s nose: “Look, here; you die finish, me KILL
you!” With a yell of terror the patient nearly
hit the roof as he fled to work. That’s three months
ago, arid he hasn't knocked off yet for meals.
“Chink” : “E.S.S.” makes one of his few
mistakes when he introduces the reed-warbler, the
bush-lark and the blue wren as Australia’s only bird-
singers of the night. There’s another, the black-and-
white fantail, whose melody makes a very merry
lyric. And the best of all our warblers is over-
looked —the magpie. I’ve heard Madge repeatedly
carolling beautifully at all hours between sunset and
sunrise in summer. Incidentally, why should a bush-
man quote the opinions of Gregory Mathews on
Australian bird-songs? Mathews is merely a cabinet
naturalist, and, incidentally, hangs out in Maoriland.

THE WAY TO MAKRAWAH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From Smithton to Marrawah the plains go up and
down,
With spear-grass and button-grass a wave of shining
brown ;
And white clouds and grey clouds go drifting o’er
the lea,
hor eager is the wooing wind that conies across the
sea ;
hnchanted are the green hills (hat beckon from afar,
The wide lands, the long lands, .
The uplands, the strong lands, s*;'•
The lands that stretch from Smithton to Verdant
Marrawah. ~f.‘-
From Smithton to Marrawah the air is like a draught
Of red wine and white wine, on Mount Olympus
quaff’d ;
On sand-hill and gully-ridge the scarlet pig-face
weaves
A purple pattern in and out among the autumn
leaves;
And fragrant is the clover where the browsing cattle
are,
The sweet lands, the near lands,
The low-lands, the dear lands,
The lands that stretch from Smithton to verdant
Marrawah.
From Smithton to Marrawah the shadows fall awry,.
From half-light to twilight the changing moments
fly;
The tea-tree and currant-bush are nodding in the
breeze,
And wondrous is the yellow moon that peeps between
the trees;
While soft sounds the lullaby of waves upon the bar,
Of the grey lands, the coast lands,
The dream lands, the ghost lands,
The lands that steal from Smithton away to Marra-
wah.

‘F.N.T. : N. S. Wales Attorney-General
Hall, who so much admires the oak, elm, larch and
other imported trees when they are incorporated
with Australian scenery, left out one of the prettiest
from his list (B, 22/2/’l7). This beauty should not
bo ignored. Indeed, it can’t be ignored by those
who live in its neighborhood. At Malvern, where
some of the streets are planted with planes, one
dweller, who hardly knew he had a chest until he
went to live among these delightful immigrants, is
now a chronic hay-fever and asthma patient, and
has had to try a vaccine-inoculation in the hope of
getting relief. Ilis little boy is going the same
way. the doctor knows what it is—a poison-laden,
infinitesimal fibre from the plane which causes pro-
found irritation when inhaled with the Malvern air.
The elm, too, so belauded by the Attorney-General
and lots of other thoughtless souls, has a fatal, fine,
old, well-known English habit of suddenly dropping
a laigo, bony branch, without a solitary warning
ciack, and it isn’t healthy for any person who hap-
pens to be underneath.
“Eryander”: The world’s biggest rainfall
(B. 22/2/’l/) is in Assam, where Charapunji, in the
hhasi Hills, tops the score with 805 in. in one year
(1861), 530 in. in six months', 366 in. in a month
(July, ’6l), 150 in. in five days, 30in. in 24 hours,
and 9in. in 90min. The average fall for 25 years
is 494 in. Charapunji’s 24-hours’ figure was, how-
eyer, beaten by the heaviest falls ever recorded in
Europe—3lin. in 24 hours, at Joyeuse (France) and
33in. in 26 hours at Gibraltar.

THE LAW ( indignantly): “An’ fer whoy did ye hit th’ malm?”
MIKE THE BANANAMAN (also indignantly) : “’It ’im—me? Why. I jist ’ad a dozen
left when e ups an’ sez: ‘How much?’ ‘A sprat th’ lot,’ sez I. ‘Well’ sez ’e
lookin’ at me ’orse. ‘I might git that fer th’ ’arness,’ sez ’e. Then ’e jist begin
examinin' me watchchain, calc’latin ’ like, when—when ’e slipped I tell yer!”
Yatti”: A snake called the copperhead
here (North Coast) reproduces its kind from eggs.
I saw a batch of 10 or 12 eggs turned up in some
loose soil, all joined together in two rows, after the
style of honeycomb. Opened one and a seemingly
fully developed 9-inch copperhead crawled out. The
other eggs were left in the very hot shn all day
and they shrivelled up, the young crawlers bein«-
dead when we looked at them next clay. We never
saw the mother snake.

“Musketoon” : The wet winter of 1916 has
made a great change in some of the lakes of N S
Wales. Twelve months ago Lake CowaJ near
Forbes, consisted of less than a spoonful of moisture.
During the previous, season wheat and hay crons
flourished in the bed. Now there is a sheet of
water miles square, teeming with waterfowl. Recently
a party of duck-murderers discovered a flock of over
a hundred pelicans disporting themselves on the
water. It is over 30 years since these birds had
been seen thereabouts.

"Iris” : One of the favorite drinks of the
poisonous black spider—the one with a red ribbon
down his spine—is bee-blood. This cunning devil
loves the dark. Mostly he is to be found in gloomy
recesses, lurking under stuff that hasn’t been shifted
in a dog’s age, and the bee-hive is just the sort of
shelter he likes. All he has to do is to choose the
right corner and wait for the bee to bring his dinner
to him-—until such time as the bee-keeper notices
that something is amiss and turns the hive inside
out for inspection. Then black-and-red brother rolls
himself up into a tight ball, drops to the ground
and pretends to be dead and gone. Generally he is,
within two or three seconds, which must be a bitter
blow to his self-confidence.

The St. John’s wort weed now covers
according to the official figures, 184,000 acres in
Victoria, of which 156,000 are Crown lands, while
12 years ago only 10,000 acres were affected. If it
goes on spreading at the same rate it will cover the
whole State in a century or so. Various people are
getting excited over the prospect, but the State Min-

Condamine : This is in case someone is
as innocent or foolish as I was. IJiad a fruit tree,
loaded, and the ants were destroying a good deal
of fruit. As a remedy I tied a kerosene rag around
the butt. It was effective. The ants changed their
address, but perhaps that was because the fruit also
changed its address. The tree died above the rag
and sent out suckers below it.
Ivhaki : Did you know that in chewing
sassafras bark or leaves you are supplying your body
with one of the best tonics and appetisers going?
Also, that a few fragments of the bark thrown into
the cask of home-made beer will enhance both flavor
and “bite” of the contents?
‘■Wart”: Our departed brother Binghi of
Tasmania had a legend to explain the origin of fire.
One day when the blacks had been sleeping at the
bottom of a hill they saw two strangers on the
summit. On going towards them the strangers
threw into their midst something like a star, which
set light to the grass and caused a panic. When
the inhabitants recovered from their fright thev
decided to save a sample, which was carefully wrapped
up and given to the
gins to take cal-e of.
Some little time
afterwards two gins
were sitting by the
seashore bewailing
their sad fate. They
were getting old and
their husbands had
taken up with
younger women. How-
ever, even heart-
-broken wives must
eat, so they decided
to dive for a cray-
fish. , While they
were in the water
a large stingray im-
paled them on his
spear. But the two
strangers who had
thrown the fire came
and killed the sting-
ray, and brought the
gins ashore. Unfor-
tunately they' were
dead. Observing this
the visitors lit a fire,
placed one gin on
each side of it, and
got some of the
largo blue ants
locally known as
bluebottles. These
they placed on the
breasts of the de-
ceased, who on
being stung imme-
diately came to life.
The strangers, who
were celestial be-
ings, then caused a
fog until everything
was as- black as
night, and under
cover of the darkness took the ex-eorpses with them
into the sky, where they still dwell, Castor and
1 ollux being pointed out as their star forms. You
could get a light from them any time if you knew
the now long-lost eorroboree.

“Cross-cut;”: The dense North Coast
(N.S.W.) forests breed thousands of carpet-snakes
every year. T’other day, in putting a big teak log
tin ough the mill at Dunoon, we discovered a nest
of 52 unbroken and over a score of mutilated carpet’s
eggs They were the size of the first joint of the
thumb, and were linked together by a thread which
seemingly kept up the vitality; but whether they
were the effort of one carpet lady or the labors of
neighbors we couldn’t find out. What is the biggest
nest ever found? Another recent find was a shed
skin of a black snake, which though crinkled up
measured 6ft. 2in. Allowing for shrinkage it must
have covered a reptile of over 7ft. Timber-getters
and cockies don’t bother the carpet brother; but
the bandy-bandy black with yellow belly and the
brown get the sapling’ every time.
‘Tetifi”: Fred Turner, F.L.S., of Sydney,
says saltbush is the best vegetable to grow as a
firebreak in the bush. Having observed in fire-swept
country that the succulent leaves of this good Aus-
tralian were only slightly scorched, while other
trees were chiefly ashes and charcoal, Turner plumps
for the “old-man,” the grey and the Wcstralian tall
saltbush to protect houses and crops. Some merits
of the saltbushes are that they are easily grown,' can
be lopped as, fodder during drought without destroy-
ing their fire-resisting- qualities, and the young stems
and leaves make a good substitute for the cultivated
spinach.
* * *
1.11. : The snake’s mode of entering life
is various. Near Ballan (Vic.) I found a black
•snake containing from 20 to 30 soft eggs connected
in single file by a gristly cord. A few weeks later
killed two of the reptiles containing- respectively
20 and 33 perfect, snakes about a quarter of an inch
tlack and seven inches long. I inferred that they
were hatched within and had not previously seen
light. They only lived a few minutes after exposure
About the same time and place I found perfect young
lizards in soft shells which had .been separately
buried in ft mound of earth and were to be hatched
apparently without maternal agency.
“E.S.S.”: The golden-breasted whistler,
also called the white-throated thickhead (Pachycc-
phnla pectoral's), is an exception to the general rule
that bright plumage indicates weak power of song.
I hough gaily-dressed he is one of the most musical of
scrub lodgers. The under-surface of his togs is rich
yellow, the breast is crossed with a band of black ;
throat white, back olive green and head black. His
cousin, the rufous-breasted whistler, is only a little less
gaily apparelled and is an equally famous songster.
The music is heard to best advantage when the
whistlers perform in company. They have many
ringing, mellifluous notes, running flute-like, strong
and clear, and when the chorus is in full swing a
flood of harmonious sound seems to roll round and
round the enclosing hills. These birds have an extra
claim on the affections of Australians, as they are
whales for insects and caterpillars.

years show:—Tiger-snake: bites, 37; deaths, 15.
Death-adder: bites, 12; deaths, seven. Brown snake:
bites, 31; deaths, six. ■ Black snake: bites, 68;
deaths, two. The death-adder is our only viper. Its
venom causes the characteristic blood-thickening,
whereas the poison of our other snakes—all colu-
brine—produces the opposite effect. Viperine poison
acts powerfully through the stomach,, but colubrine
may be swallowed with impunity if there are no
broken surfaces. The antivenines prepared from
snake-poisons are effective only against the special
venom of their origin, and the poison of each species
is inert for only that species.
THEN HIS TURN CAME.
SHE: “ One a.m. is a nice time to get home!”
HE: "Well, I had to stag in town to go into
a business matter with Billy Jones.”
SHE: "Try someone else! Y’see, Billy’s
been here with me all the evening!”
TUPORO’S HIGH-GALLOPING HORSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Te Tuporo bought a horse from Bill
(And Bill was a dealer shrewd and cute) ;
That horse would buck all clay up-hill
And was known to the boys as a (crimson) beaut.
But Tuporo knew how to ride a horse;
He stuck to that “buck” like its own bay skin,
And with Maori methods of vast resource
He mastered the nedcly and broke him in.
Old Bill was down at the pub one day
When Tuporo entered to “hava te trink.”
Bill laughed, “Well, how's th’ old buckin’ bay?
He’s the very good horse to go, I think!”
Te Tuporo grinned as he quaffed his beer.
“He kot te hard mout’, but I toan’ min’.
An’ sometime te peggar a rittle bit queer—
He te planner to gallop too high pehin'."
MAORI LAND.
TIAKI.

Business, Bobbery, etc
“Wart” : One of the secrets of the great
guns which did such good work for Germany
at the beginning of the war was that they
contained 3 to 4 per cent, of molybdenum.
Molybdenum steel allowed the Huns to cut
down the weight while increasing the
strength. It is estimated that the increased
cost of 25 per cent, increased the useful life
of the gun 2000 per cent. How much of
the molybdenum came from Australia, I
wonder. I have a handful of molybdenite
specimens left with me 10 years ago by a
gentleman with a German name who offered
to find a market for all I could get hold
of. The same Hun bought from me several
’weights of osmiridium, which is now prob-
ably helping to tip the trembler blades
and screws of Germany’s aeroplane and
Zeppelin engines. Billy Hughes and
the other people who fish for the
votes of the ignorant curse the Germans
for getting the metal trade. Rubbish!
The proper people to throw bricks at are
the British for their deadly dullness. I
sold to the German, for instance, because
he came to me with offers. No Britisher
ever offered me anything.

“F.N.T.”: Spuds are being planted in
English parks, according to “Viator” (B.
15/2/’l7), because they “won’t travel in a
state of suspended deadness,” while “the
refrigerator won’t persuade Murphy to
keep.” Murp. ivill travel—not in the re-
frigerator, but in an earth “clamp” excluding
the light and air. Where does “Viator”
think he got the spuds that were served
with the roast beef last time he crossed the
Bay of Biscay, bound for Port o’ London?
But it pays better to ship other fruits, such
as wheat and wool.

“P.X.” attends a funeral:—
After keeping its sign up for close on 50 years
the Beehive, at Bendigo, throws in its license. Where
the Beehive left off and the Bendigo Stock Exchange
began, thousands of people who have been there
would have been puzzled to say. What stirring
times the old place passed through ! What booms it
wet with gold-top, and what gin has been swallowed
with the bitters! Once nearly everything that
happened at Bendigo happened at the Beehive. “At
the Beehive”—it was the heading tinder which E. A.
Banks, the kindliest old “Mosquito” that ever
buzzed, wrote his weekly column of gossip and
philosophy. In the vestibule you could find George
Lansell and Dyason and occasionally Dr. Atkinson
and Mueller and the plucky Boyds and all the Fat
men of Bendigo who mostly would have been fatter
if they hadn’t spent their thousands in poking down
a mile or more in their prodigious faith in the ever-
lastingness of the field. And, behind, rubbing shoul-
ders with the owners as he came, in and out, Peter
Phillips, the one-armed secretary of the “Hay Hem
Hay,” the tenderest-hearted old man who ever
growled habitually, the bear in a remarkable menag-
erie—topped by landlord Harry Burridge’s talking
canary. Bendigo mining hardly knows what a strike
is; and it might not be too much to say.that that
was because owners and miners met at The Beehive,
and yarned in the vestibule and found that they were
the same sort of men under the skin.

“X.” : I wanted a little ready cash the
other day, so I wrote to one of the well-
known firms of moneylenders from whom I
had received circulars with copies of testi-
monials furnished by grateful clients* I
wanted £2O for two months, and this is what
I got. They posted me an application form
asking, as they were quite justified in doing,
every - possible question in regard to my
financial state, where I bought my furniture,
whether I had paid my rent, and so on, not
forgetting whether I was likely to be under-
taking military or naval service. If, after
studying my replies, they approved of my
application, they would be pleased to for-
ward papers for me to sign, when they
would be agreeable to advance me £l6 10s",
charging me £3 10s. interest. For this I was
to pay £lO at the end of one month and £lO
at the end of the second month. I would
thus hold £l6 10s. for one month and £6 10s.
for another month, which averages £ll 10s.
per month. The interest proposed, £3 10s.,
in turn averages £1 15s. per month, or £2l
per annum. Working on these averages the
annual interest on the' sum of £ll 10s. is
£2l, which works out at about 182 per cent,
per annum, giving the usurers the benefit of
the fraction. I told my creditors to wait.
It is easier to owe.
“Silas Snell”: This is something in the
nature of a law report. It was a County
Court case. Two jackasses on a 140-acre
estate in Gippsland had pounced simultane-
ously on a worm. The worm was a true
Gippsland specimen, not as large as a conger-
eel, but big enough to provide all the worm
two reasonable laughing jacks would be
likely to need. Each bird fastened on
to an end, and began to swallow greedily.
They met half-way, and then commenced a
tug-Of-war, with the result that each bird
slid off his section of the worm, and went
end-over with a flutter of wings and some
mutterings of disgust. Again the birds dived
on the worm, gobbling to break all records.
Again they met half-way, each having swal-
lowed his allotted span. Once more there
was an energetic pull on the part of both
claimants, each desirous of dispossessing the
other of his half-interest in the delectable
insect, and once more the birds slithered off
their slippery prize, and turned empty away,
in a manner of speaking. Five times the
operation was repeated, and the matter was
still in dispute. But by now a small con-
gregation of birds, including a most inquisi-
tive magpie, had formed round the centre of
argument, and were watching the contest
with interest and some little arguments on
the points of law and certain precedents
bearing on the case. The kooks had met
again, ligatured by the worm. They pulled
for the sixth endeavor, when suddenly the mag-
pie, who had assumed a prominent judicial
position, slipped forward, and delivered judg-
ment. lie snapped the worm sharply between
the contending beaks, and severed it at a
blow. The tugging kooks went keel-over
again, but each in possession of his just and
legal proportion of the property in dispute.
The kooks came up smiling, there were
mutual congratulations, and the court dis-
persed, leaving everybody satisfied. As a
police-magistrate Solomon could have given
no points on legal administration to that
business-like ’pie.
“Marboc”: Pretty cocky knows another
trick besides the one of partly filling wheat
bags with soil. Down at a N. S. Wales
border town Bill Wheatsheaf had drawn his
load on to the weighbridge, and when the
weigher went into his office Bill slipped
round to the far side of the load and grabbed
the lugs of a bag. The weigher wondered
why the indicator kept bobbing up and down
till, looking through the window, he saw
Bill’s legs dangling. When Wheatsheaf
came rotind for his ticket the weigher handed
it to him without a word. Later on he
went to the buyer’s agent and told him the
whole trick, adding, as an afterthought,
‘What weight do you think Bill is?” “Oh,
about 12 stone, 1 should say,” said the
agent. “Twelve stone! That’s 1681 b., isn’t
it? Well, I’ve allowed a fair margin. I
docked him 3cwt.”

“Bluebeard” : Paddy’s Market, in Bourke-
street, Melbourne, has lost by retirement
William Joseph Tiller. William Joseph was
born in Sackville-street, Dublin, learnt the
picture-framing trade at Islington, and
arrived in Melbourne on Cup day, ’79, to
begin his life-work of supplying the suburbs
with mirrors framed in plush and oleographs
of kings and queens in coronation robes. The
old man keeps on his factory, as the air of
Heaven would be unbreathable if it were not
tinged with the scent of glue and gold paint.

‘Trallwm” : The gentle slum-maker. An
advt. came into us the other day for the sale
of one of the most glowingly-described
worker’s home-properties ever offered by auc-
tioneer. Its wonderful advantages filled a
double-demy sale-bill. Then at the bottom,
in shrinking bashful 10-point roman, was
this sermon in type: ON LAND 15ft. by
42ft. Three good rooms, all Conveniences,
land 15 by 42 !
“F.T.”: To lots of people a rose by a
foreign name would smell a lot sweeter.
Nobody in Australia would look at a rabbit-
skin “fur”; but call it “lapin fur” and—
why not? Anyhow, a line is being tried with
the French moniker, and business is re-
ported “splendid.”
“Yarra Ben” : They are making good use
of their mayor at Wonthaggi (Vic.). The
borough council recently appointed the
wearer of the rabbit-skins a debt-collector,
authorising him to interview a defaulting
ratepayer who owed £7O. His Nibs didn't
get the cash, but he got a pretty good pro-
posal from the debtor to pay at the rate of
30s. a week. Another instance of “Money
makes the mayor to go.”
Adelaide is only just realising the possi-
bilities of the parts about Victoria Square.
It is the very centre of the city. But the
early settlement was necessarily on the city’s
northern boundary, for the sake of the
Torrens water. Also, the road from the
Port came in that way, and presently a Port
railway meant a station, which drew in all
the other subsequent lines. So business all
settled down near the northern boundary.
The reaction is coming now. Chas. Moore,
the Ulsterman (just dead, unfortunately),
declared war on the Bundle-street drapers
by building a palatial place running from the
Supreme Court to the Central Market, and
others are doing, their best on a smaller
scale. A cut just made behind the G.P.0., by
the removal of a timber-yard, lets daylight in
on a nest of comfortable vine-clad cottages
there of great age ; and that means a large
expanse of land within a few yards of King
William-street, only waiting a far-sighted
builder.
* * * *
Mutton took to wings in Maoriland the
other day and made the highest flight on
southern record. At Fordell, Wanganui, a
line of 750 Romney “flock” ewes brought the
staggering price of 645. 6d. per head.
“Panhandle Pete”: We had been fed for
months on tasteless English jams, and Mace-
donia was littered with tins we had not had
the courage to finish. Then all of a sudden
we started to get Australian stuff, and the
blanky of the English Tommy at being “able
to taste the blanky. apricots in the blanky
jam” was the sweetest music I had ever
heard.
Informative item from the Akaroa Mail :
(M.L.) -
Barry’s Bay factory, which had been receiving
1900 gallons of milk per day before the heavy rain,
is now receiving a daily supply of 2300 gallons. The
daily supply at Okain’s factory has also increased by
some 300 gallons.
No wonder the heavens weep !
