“Mimosa”: A certain Barcoo shanty is
well known for the number of raffles and
sweeps it gets up. The members of the
household have amazingly good luck in these
gambles—so good that the old hands won’t
bite. A big sweep was recently got up on a
Southern race, and a jackeroo was' caught
in a weak moment and persuaded to take a
ticket. “Yes; put my name down, and I’ll
give you the money later.” A month or so
elapsed, and the trustful investor happened
to meet one of the family in town. “Hullo!
How’d I get on in that sweep?” “Oh, didn’t
you know? Mother won the first prize.
Ain’t she lucky? Me brother Alf got the
second prize and my cousin Gwen the. third.
Ain’t they lucky?” “They’re lucky, all
right!” muttered the mug. But the voluble
young lady, continuing, said: “Oh, Mr.
Tally, you haven’t paid for your ticket yet,
if you remember.” “That so?” grinned
Tally. “Ain’t I lucky?”
The war has altered a host of things, and
the peace may alter more. For instance,
many of the aviators will decline to settle
down to be earth-worms. A lot of them
will want to follow Basil Watson’s example
and carry the mails, and when you are
able to post a letter in Sydney in
the morning and have it delivered in
Melbourne the same afternoon, or send
a line from Brisbane on Tuesday which
will catch the mail-boat leaving Adel-
aide on Wednesday, the public will insist
on having its letters carried that way. By
that time the wireless and the telephone
will both be in much greater use; and half
the loan money sunk in, the post and tele-
graph department will be represented by
“scrap.” But the debt will be as good as
new; because the politician insists on
assuming that every public work is ever-
lasting and no sinking fund is therefore
needed.
“John Dory’s” kettle of fish : —•
N. S. Wales State trawlers crawled out of Wool-
loomooloo Bay (Sydney) one day lately after being
laid up for some weeks while a lovely row raged fore
and .aft. There are nine positions for engineers on
the three craft, and the argument was as to whether
the nine should be supplied by the Marine Engineers’
Ass. or by the Engineers’ Ass. Just prior to this
uproar the boats were rusting for months while a
dog fight raged as to who was to appoint the masters.
Meanwhile all hands gracefully drew full pay, just
as they did while they wrangled about the engineers.
Likewise, during the campaign the superintendent
and the supervisor and the marine superintendent
and the secretary, 14 clerks, 17 wharf-hands, 25
cleaners, 35 carters, 45 shop-hands and 55 sundries
all lifted full pay. The cooks are now expected to
come out to decide whether the three slushies shall
be supplied by the Federated Cooks and Stewards’
Union or the Amalgamated Cooks and Pantrymen,
or the Marine Cooks, or by Cook’s Statue. And when
we get a State ferry—mother of Neptune!
“Ham”: Is this anything of a record?
This good Billjim (although he was born
in England) served eight years in the navy
and is now with the A.I.F. His mother,
young brother and two sisters are working
on munitions and his two elder brothers
ar flinging lead about in France. The only
one of the family not doing a bit is the
father. His only excuse is that he was
drowned at sea 20 years ago.
“Joe B.”: Consider Private Frederick
Clark, of St. Ivilda (Vic.), aged (of course)
45, returned soldier, married seven times,
with a tally of 25 children, 11 of whom are
serving at the Front. This Father of Young
Australia is now enjoying the breezes of
Hobson’s Bay, and getting back some of his
lost strength before, as he hopes, facing the
Hun again.
G. P. Doolette, mining man in London,
has sent a picture to Adelaide Art Gallery
as a practical remembrance of the city where
he once had a tailor’s shop. It is a study
of two big St. Bernards on a hearth in a
stone-floored, red-rugged hall. The French-
man George Fauvel did it, and it earned
him a medal in the Paris Salon.
The late Rocky Whelan, who was hanged
at Hobart in 1855, and left half a dozen
caves in various places for the benefit of
tourists, lost his life over a pair of boots.
Rocky, who graduated at Norfolk Island,
developed a habit, after he got loose in the
Speck, of sneaking up behind unsuspecting
travellers, shooting them in the back, and
then robbing them. He made away with
half a dozen in this pleasant manner, but
none of them could supply him with
boots to fit his feet, which were out-
sizes. So he had to go into Hobart to get
a pair ; and as he was passing up Elizabeth-
street a policeman who had been at Norfolk
Island and had known Rocky there, spotted
him. He called another, and they shad-
owed Whelan to a bootshop. Rocky was
an exceptionally powerful man, but the con-
stables waited till he was pulling on a boot
and then, taking a mean advantage, rushed
him. .

“Billy B.” : Where is that man who was
bellowing the other week in The Bulletin
for the suppression of what he called “for-
tune-tellers” ? Let him advance and I will
read to him Zadkiel’s . Almanac and
Ephemeris. On thfe day that I write this
(Feb. 28) I turn to the same date in Zad..
1910, and these are the warnings I receive:
The Kaiser will experience unexpected opposition
to his projects, and insidious enemies will be plot-
ting against him. It would be well for him to avoid
travelling abroad and to run no avoidable risks of
any kind.
Very well; Wilhelm travelled abroad via Bel-
gium, and if anybody desires to swap risks
with him he can have my share of them. But
we’ll pass that and come to the month in
which the war broke out —August, 1914. In
that month, 1910, Zad. got this off (page
33 of his Almanac)
Mars on entering Virgo is in conjunction with
Mercury and the Moon on the 6th day of this
month, and both [characters unreproducable—one
looks like a sheep brand and the other is a new
species of fantod] are in parallel declination with
Regulus. This is ominous for Turkey, in which
country a martial, if not a discordant, spirit will
soon be roused and one difficult to allay. In fact,
the outlook will shortly be serious for Austria and
Greece as well as Turkey, and great efforts on the
part of the peacemakers will be needed if bloodshed
is to be avoided. Paris will be greatly excited.
Now, then, scoffer!
HAWKERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dust, dust, dust and a dog—-
Oh! the sheepdog won’t be last,
Where the long, long shadow of the okl bay horse
With the shadow of his mate is cast.
A brick-brown woman with her brick-brown kids,
And a man with his head half-mast,
The feedbags hung and the bedding slung;
And the blackened bucket made fast
Where the tailboard clings to the tucker and things—
So the hawker’s van goes past.
N.s.iv.
HENRY LAWSON.

POLITICAL
POINTS
During the last two or three years the
Australian banks’ current account money
has increased, and there are now many mil-
lions which are lying at call, practically
there for safe - custody. The men who own
the money are waiting till the clouds roll
by to invest it. The banks don’t, as a rule,
use this money to any extent: it isn't safe
for them to do so. As the Commonwealth
will have to ask for many more millions yet,
an effort ought to be made to devise a
scheme for using some of these unemployed
deposits. There are thousands of institu-
tions and people who know that they will
be able to accumulate a certain amount dur-
ing the next two or three years—this
applies particularly to life-insurance offices
-—and some scheme should be possible
whereby this “call money” at the banks
could be used to finance these institutions
and people at a low rate of interest. Pos-
sibly a special war deposit might be insti-
tuted, the proceeds to be used exclusively to
finance investors in the war loan who want
accommodation for any term up to three
years.
PARLIAMENT ARY MUSICAL CHAIRS.
The most interesting feature is the uncertainty
surrounding the intentions of the Prime Minister.
The seat which he now holds —West Sydney—is
denied him. Another constituency has, therefore, to
be found for Mr. Hughes.—S. M. HERALD.
N. S. Wales Governor Strickland has not
been “recalled” ; he has been given leave of
absence till his term expires. That is the
“nice” way of getting out of a nasty posi-
tion. The whole incident is illuminating:
with his Ex.’s practical deposition goes the
last remaining pretence that Australian
Governors are anything more than a Consti-
tutional myth costing a lot of money that
we can’t spare. When the N. S. Wales
Assembly declined to pass a vote of confi-
dence in the Holman Government. Strickland
thought it was up to him, as Governor, to
get some' fresh advisers ; but when Holman
marched Wade up to Government House
and Wade said that his crowd was behind
Holman, his Ex. saw that lie had been a
very hasty Governor. Yet this at least can
be said: he. believed that he was protecting
the people from the wrong of having a Gov-
ernment which could not command, a vote of
confidence from a majority of the Assembly.
The termination of the incident shows that
it is for the Assembly to protect the people ;
not for the Governor to do it. And that
being so, why have a Governor at all?
When lawyer Irvine, hooked for England,
was making the Government’s grand effort
in the House of Reps, to choke Tudor’s
motion for a Royal Commission of High
OUT OF THE MOUTH OF BABES.
“But you don't really use the bayonet, do
you? You only have it to frighten the
horrid Huns!”
Court Judges to investigate the Ready and
Watson scandals, he put it to the House
like this :
The proposal for a Royal Commission' struck at
the very basis -of constitutional government, and no
Government could vote for the appointment. It was
a matter with which Parliament itself was solely
concerned. Charges of corruption could not be deter-
mined by any tribunal outside Parliament.
But no sooner, had Irvine persuaded the
House that “the charges could not be deter-
mined by any tribunal outside Parliament”
than the Prime Minister started to take
action in the Courts against Watson. Now,
honestly, what does Irvine think of it all?
The N. S. Wales municipal elections were
due in January, but Bumble, following Hol-
man’s lead, got his job of mismanaging the
Drain extended till June, “so that the rate-
payers would go to the poll
free from the excitement caused
by the Conscription refer-
endum” —though Conscription
had no more to do with the
Drain than it has with the
ordinary job of earning bread
and butter; and nobody pro-
posed that that should be
Suspended. Now the State
election this month and the
Federal unpleasantness in May
are seemingly going to keep the
Conscription irrelevancy work-
ing over-time, so that in June
the municipal. elector will be
still unable to give his calmest
consideration to the subject of
the removal of the garbage. In
the circumstances the only
thing to be done seems to be
to declare a day when the
entire population shall take chloroform.
Then in perfect peace a poll may be held,
and Aid. Brokenpot may be re-elected for
the 93rd consecutive time.
From Hughes’s election speech in the
House of Reps.:—
These gentlemen on the other side care nothing
for the war or the Umpire.
Mr. Finlayson: Shame!
Mr. Hughes: Except for its downfall!
That is Mr. Hughes’s opinion of the Labor
party. Now what is the country’s opinion
oFtlie patriot who, during two years of war,
was content to be a member of that party,
who left it in power while he went to Eng-
land, and who, only a month ago, offered it a
place in a Government which he was pre-
pared to lead ?
Nobody has put the Australian attitude
towards the Dardanelles Commission bettei,
after all, than old George Reid :
Australia did not want the Commission. While
bowing her head before her awful sacrifices, Australia
conceived it her duty to refrain from bitterness aid
bickering. [By the publication of the report] we
have reduced our invaluable prestige, given our
enemy a. multitude of texts, and washed our national
soiled-linen publicly to the Germans’ delight.
It was all so obvious, too —not perhaps this
man’s little bit of responsibility or the
other’s; but the foundation trouble. Britain
fumbled along with a standing army of a few
thousand men, and then suddenly found she
had to raise, train and equip millions and
put them into the field : and there was con-
fusion, ail the worse because nobody knew
what anybody else could do. Tlio one man
who had done anything had mostly done it
among black men, in remote places, where
he had learned to trust to himself, and in
the dreadful confusion the rattled nation
threw itself almost pathetically on him,
though his training to meet, the new; condi-
tions had been just about as bad as it could
be. Then the untrained, headstrong poli-
tician also butted in, and disaster followcd-
the occasional disaster inseparable from the
policy of muddling through.
Quaint telegram in a Sydney paper last
week :
Charleville (Q.), Thursday.- -A drover named
Herb Lewis is now close to the N.S.V. border with
8000 wethers from Gowrie station, and a permit
from Hughes to cross. The owners insist that
Lewis must cross at all hazards.
The hazards of shifting jumbucks about
have, of course, always been terrible. But
the hazards to Australia of living under a
system which makes the supply of raw
material for the gridiron dependent upon
war between persons named Hughes and
Ryan look like being worth serious attention
by all'hards. Bananaland, under a lot of
nioat-grablr rs and importers of foreign
beetle-catchers, may get top place as the
Nark State yet.
The Federal election has been fixed for
May 5. The Argus and the S. M. Herald
and other people who have vilified Hughes
for 20 years, and now Suddenly embrace
him, call it a war election. It is nothing
of the kind. It is an election to decide
whether the conduct of the war, so far as
Australia is concerned, shall be in the
hands of the men who have been responsible
for the bungling and mismanagement and
waste and intrigue in the past. If Australia
believes that the men who were responsible
for putting universal service to the vote;
who were responsible for wasting the time
of tons thousands of men in camp, just
at the outset of the harvest, thus making it
practically impossible to carry Conscription ;
who were responsible for restrictions on the
public press and plhtform so intolerable that
the leaders of both political parties in N. S.
Wales joined to demand their withdrawal;
who were responsible for the proposal to
tamper with the Conscription ballot; who
then clung to offifce though they had the
smallest party in the House; who engineered
the discreditable Ready business, and. on
being charged with attempted bribery and cor-
ruption in connection with the Watson mat-
ter, fought against the proposal to let High
Court judges inquire into it; who were -such
patriots that they haggled for weeks with
the Cook party over the division of the
sooil —if the people think these are the
right men to run the war or any other busi-
ness as Australia would like it run, then
they will vote for the present administrators.
THE LATEST PARIS FASHION.
AUSTRALIA JANE: “Tell me, what teas the

Bertie Johnston, M.L.A., of Westralia.
must have put up a record for the shortest
and most shuddersome Speakership: he
looked over the mace for just one week.
The Labor Opposition reckoned, with rea-
son, that Bertie had played a trick on
them; wherefore from the moment he took
liis seat he was bombarded with more or less
insulting adverse motions, to which he replied
with slabs of May and Standing Orders.
The penultimate day of Bertie’s reign could
HIS NEW EXPERIENCE.
“You arc absolutely the first girl I have ever
kissed.”
“Do you expect me to believe that?”
“Well, you are Ilia first who has ever doubted
it.”
hardly have been equalled by the old Sydney
Parliament at its worst. A Stadium crowd
when the referee puts his hand on the de-
feated outsider’s poll would be a Quakers
meeting compared with the Westralian
Legislative Ass. that day. The Speaker
heard himself referred to as (among other
things)—well, a roseate prevaricator. lie
“named” his own Deputy-Speaker Hoi
man, who was surrounded by a bodyguard
when the Sergeant-at-Arms and a constable
attempted to enforce the Speaker’s orders.
Eventually Johnston bolted from the Cham-
ber and next day resigned.
Tasmania supplies a fine example of the
beauty of our system of having two sets of
people attending to the same job. The
Federal Arbitration Court has iixed the
wages of builders’ laborers at 12s. a day for
a week of 44 hours, while the State Wages
Board gives carpenters and other skilled
building tradesmen 12s. (or 11s. in some
cases) a day for a week of 48 hours. As
it is cheaper to employ skilled tradesmen
than laborers, the natural result is that on
big jobs laborers are getting mighty scarce;
even if they are only doing laboring work
the employees are called tradesmen, and are
employed as such, in order that they may
come under the State Wages Board and be
got cheaper. It is a bleak look-out for the
carpenters and other skilled men who have
to learn their trade and find their own tools
and then get less pay than laborers who do
not have to bother about those things.
Officialdom dilutes the “rude javelins of
fact” about the Federal fusion with a
clumsy hand. On the first page of Com-
monwealth Hansard is tabulated the per-
sonnel of the first “Hughes Administration."
This is followed by details of another
“Hughes Administration” (i.e., the nucleus
of the fusion). Then come particulars of
what is labelled the “Commonwealth War
Administration.” In a party newspaper this
alias would pass as part of the game. In
Hansard the employment of such a term is
inexcusable: and it is the Speaker’s job to
see to it.
“Red Tape Junior” : John Storey, who is
leading Labor at the N. S. Wales elections,
tickles the public servants to get them to
vote for his crowd. He proposes to: —
(1) Sack the Public Service Board.
(2) Retire the old buffers at 60.
(3) Restore all increases over £2OO.
(4) Pay all hands at least £156 p.a., or £3 a
week.
(5) Grant all increments.
(6) Make knock-off time 4.30.
(7) Allow no retrenchment.
That’s what I call statesmanship! Johnno
will get all our mob except the old buffers;
that is, he will unless Holman bids up £4
a week with a four-o’clock whistle.
FORGIVENESS: A VISION OF JEANNE D’ARC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Up through the Gates of Gold there rose a cry,
A cry of France in blood and agony,
A cry that shook the Throne;
And Joan the Warrior-Angel, lingering near
The Presence High, by grace, with shining spear,
Trembled in all her glory for a space,
And cried till Ileav’n seemed ringing with the ■
sound: .
“O Lord, it is my sunlit land again, ■
My land of laughter, tears and blood and pain,
That calls me hence once more!
Shall I not go as in the days of yore
And head her legions in the fair Champagne,
And hurl her warriors With their showers of steel
On yonder sullen foe?”
The Presence heard her through immortal calm.
Then spake the Voice: “Look down
And see if there thou do not clear descry
A Woman girt as thou in days’ of old,
Leading her hosts from neighb’ring Northern Isles
To save this land of thine ?”
And, as the Presence bade so did the Maid,
And saw, in wide amaze,
A trident-bearing fair and stately Queen,
Who swept from Anglia’s gleaming ports of stone
And, hailed by France as France’s very own,
Joined hosts and drave th’ invader on his Rhine,
And helped to break his ninefold woven line
And crush his bands of steel!
Then Joan, her eyes aflood with angel tears,
Cried out: “0 England! All mine ancient wrongs,
My wounds, my prison bonds, the fiery breath
Of faggots that were round me at my death
I do forgive thee now!
Go forward blessed in thy proud advance,
And take, to bind thee to thy Sister France,
This kiss upon thy brow!”
QUEENSLAND,
P. AIREY.

THE DAY OF THE EXPERT. To-day is the day of the expert—the man who knows every detail of his work
—who is thorough, efficient and up to date. The engineer who desires to reach the top of the tree in his profession must
be conversant with every engineering method which makes for speed, complete craftsmanship, and for economy.
THE ENGINEER WHO DOES NOT KNOW of the Oxy-Acetylene process is not abreast of the times; he is
at big disadvantage, and leaves himself open to the charge of incompetence.
Throughout the world to-day the Oxy-Acetylene process is the latest scientific addition to every engineering
workshop, which, fitted with this process, has an advantage over the opposition impossible to estimate. It is simple,
yet wonderfully effective; its price is trifling, and the plant is portable. Whether the broken parts require to be cut
or welded at the bench or by the roadside is of no consequence.
Any work of Iron or Steel welding or cutting can be carried out with Oxy-Acetylene. It is revolutionising
y the Engineering trade. Broken parts that ortce were useless can now be successfully welded and made new
again. This, too, in the matter of minutes. Cost is a matter of pence as compared with the expenditure
of pounds in the old method of buying new parts and making new drawings and castings.
All Engineers should at once write us for free catalogues and illustrations showing the remark-
able variety of work that can be done with Oxy-Acetylene. Following are a few of the broken
and worn machinery parts our process w ill make new again:—
Cracked or broken Aluminium Crank or Gear Cases, Differential Boxes, /Steering Wheels, Pipes,
Cylinders, Pistons, Cranks, Clutches, Cardan Shafts, Band Brakes, Worn Keyways in Shafts.
Pieces welded to broken Gears and New Teeth Cut, etc.
Broken Machinery of any kind can be welded, including Cast Iron, Wrought Iron, Mild
Steel, Brass, Copper, Gun Metal, etc., which cannot be repaired by any other process.
The latest authoritative work on the subject, “A Practical Manual of
Autogenous Welding,” published by Charles Griffin & Co., London, states:
‘‘Within the last few years welding by oxy-hydrogen and oxy-acetylene blow-
pipes has been applied in thousands of workshops.” Those workshops which are
rapidly adding the process to their equipment include manufacturers, con-
tractors, machine shops, garages, blacksmiths, boilermakers, repair shops,
foundries, steel mills and railroad shops. ,

SPORTING
Punters whu back top-weights for the
Australian Cup are shutting their eyes to the
records. Only twice in 55 years has the winner
carried more than Bst. 71b. Forty of the winners
have carried less than Bst., but only seven of them
have been, under 7st. As a rule, the bottom weights
are hardly class enough to stay the 2J. miles in good
company. This year was one of the exceptions, for
there was no doubt as to imported Harriet Graham
(6st. 121 b.) and Court Jester (Gst. 91b.) being nags
of quality. But they were both under •suspicion of
being insufficiently fit and seasoned for the passage.
KricConnolly was not cocksure of Court Jester’s
condition, and litle Dempsey, who had choice of
mounts, gave the public a good tip by riding the 10
to 1 chance in preference to the favorite. In a race
which clipped nearly 2sec. off the record the 4-year-
old mare and 3-year-old colt had the finish to them-
selves, and the lady wore the young man down. The
race was a rather bad advt. for the old Australian
blood. Four of the first five horses, were imported,
and the other, a sou of Comedy King, who was foaled
in England.
All reasonable punters must have backed
Patrobas for the King’s Plate last Saturday. For a
while the sturdy son of Wallace was quoted at 3 to 1
in a field of six, and eventually he went out at 9 to
4._ There was no visible reason for supposing that
Patrobas was likely to lose this two-mile race. The
brilliant Aides and Colbert had happened to beat
him in a fast-run 12-furlong go on the Thursday,
whereas Patrobas had narrowly defeated Aides over
10 furlongs on the previous Saturday. Also Wolaroi
and Lavendo had run a brilliant mile, after cutting
up badly in the Leger and Newmarket respectively.
But the fact remained that of all these uncertain
performers Patrobas was the least uncertain, and the
only one that had proved himself fully equal to a
two-mile race. Had the pace been hotter Patrobas
would surely have won the King’s Plate quite easily.
As it was he took the lead at the distance and
outstayed Wolaroi. By the way, the running of
William the Silent in this event suggested that the
unlucky little chestnut is recovering his lost form.
But he wasn’t. He went lame instead.
* * * *
Woorak was beginning' to go off and
Wolaroi to improve towards the end of the V.R.C.
meeting. It looked that way, anyhow. In the C. M.
Lloyd Stakes Wolaroi showed considerably better
form than he exhibited in the Leger. His win over
Lavendo was a really good performance. Woorak,
who had been backed down to 10 to 9 on, was badly
beaten off. The little fellow had been having too
much. Wolaroi started in the Leger at 5 to 4 on
in a field of five, and for the first time in his life
was ridden behind. On being asked to move out of
that position the colt seemed to reckon it was a fair
thing to remain where he was, but at the end he
scrambled in third. William Brown’s pair, Colbert
and Thana, who had been racing at Moorefield a
week before, captured first and second money. They
were backed at 10’s —or, rather, Thana was for a
little, and when Colbert arrived in the lead, follow-
ers of the stable had difficulty to prevent themselves
falling down dead. These nags vyill be after some of
the w.f.a. races at Flemington, ahd anyone who takes
the St. Leger running as a guide may win a million.
But the chances are that he won’t.
Still, Colbert may be a better colt than a lot of
people think. His second to Aides in the Gover-
nor’s Plate, when both .beat Patrobas, was worth
remembering. The champion did not do as well as
he ought to have done in this race, and the wail of
people who laid him at 2 to 1 when they saw what
was occurring had the note of abject misery. But
they had: a chance to get the missing cash back on
the last day at 9 to 4 in the King’s Plate. This
time Patrobas ran clean over Wolaroi and Lavendo
at the finish of a race in which nothing was out to
break records. They were 3min. 33sec. on the two-
mile journey.
■** * *
Imported nags again! Polycrates and
Harriet Graham captured a big double at Fleming-
ton, and Green Cap, Highfield and Aides captured ■
other events. This success of the imported moke
is getting wearisome, and is certainly not met by
the irrelevant allegation that Australian-bred horses
are winning more races than the imported article.
The disconcerting fact is that the English-bred horse,
of moderate class where he comes from, is showing a
greater stamina than the Australian neddy; for
though exceptions may be quoted, there is no getting
away from the cumulative evidence afforded by the
racing of the past two seasons, nor from the horribly-
small number of first-class horses turned out in Aus-
tralia from the multitude of experiments in breed-
ing., One result is to lead to a greater demand for
the .English horse. That may be a good thing. But
if another is to create a change in the Australian
method of treating yearlings and to an overhaul of
two-year-old racing, this page has an idea that
matters might be evened-up so as to dip the scale in
the other direction.
Two plunging punters are said to have
lost more than ~£20,000 between them on the Thurs-
day of. the V.R.C. Meeting. One of the bad invest-
ments was £9OOO to £4OOO on Patrobas.
* * * *
There was at least one lucky punter over
the .Newmarket and' Australian Cup double. He is a
young Sydney barrister, who doesn’t bet. heavily or
often, but having a notion that a couple of im-
ported nags might win these events, backed the
right pair—£20O'O to £B. Service of a cheque
was accepted without fee.
Colonel ,T. W. McArthur Onslow put up
an unusual double on March 3. His horse Poly-
crates won the rich Newmarket. Handicap at Flem-
ington and he himself won, unopposed, the Bondi
seat in the N. S. Wales Leg. Assembly.
liiewton Stewart, winner of the Hopeful
Stakes at Flemington last week, looks just the sort
of youngster to develop into a real good one. He
is a big, upstanding son of Wallace, with the forma-
tion of a stayer. The colt’s response to the vigorous
riding of Joe Killorn suggested gameness and
stamina, and if all goes well with the growing youth
lie should make owner J. N. McArthur happy next
season. Probably Newton Stewart will be a
tougher proposition in long-distance races than his
brilliant stable companion Thrice, who appears to be
boss two-year-old in the business at the present
moment.
“0.K.” : These three horses that ran and
won at the V.R.C. Meeting may be expected to
distinguish themselves in Sydney next month, if
their legs hold good meanwhile —Harriet Graham,
Court Jester and Whitefield.
Weights for the Doncaster Handicap and
Sydney Cup have again set a lot of people wonder-
ing whether handicapping is a science, an art or
just a game of hide-and-seek. Anyhow, it is as hard
to make out the reason for some of the weights as
it is to find the winners. In the Cup the handi-
capper puts Chantemerle within lib. of Patrobas
(9st. 61b.). AVho could tell the reason for this?
For the Australian Cup Patrobas (9st. 41b.) was ■
weighted to give Chantemerle 61b. The imported
horse was scratched for that event. Patrobas since
then lias beaten the best at w.f.a. ; Chantemerle has
done nothing. The assessment for Randwick as
between these two horses looks even morg absurd
than to reckon Quinologist a 51b. better horse than
Green Cap and Secret Service 41b. better than the
Australian Cup winner, Harriet Graham. Is Court
Jester in his right place when he is put a stone
worse than Rushford and Bombita? And what, in
the name of Heaven, is there to justify the assump-
tion that Redfern, over two miles, is within 21b. of
Carlita and better than Wolaroi, Lingle, Sasanof
and Prince Bardolph? Woorak tops the Doncaster
with lOst. 21b. Polycrates is next with 9st. 101 b.
Then comes Chantemerle aa-ain with 9st. 51b. —ahead
of Cetinge, Lavendo, Whitefield, Wolaroi, High-
field, Balarang and 191 b. above Wedding Day. It
may be all right, but it looks to this page as if the
handicappers are getting scared of the imported
moke, and reckon one sort of form the same as any
other.
Bobadil, now 22 years old, was §old the
other day at Melbourne for 330 guineas, and goes
to Bananaland. On the same day his son Blague
changed hands for 350 guineas. When he was
younger Bobadil begat many winners for the Wil-
sons, and if he never does it again he will have more
than a reasonable excuse.
Gone at last to where the good nags go,
Newhaven, who started a hot favorite for the Mel-
bourne Cup in.’96 and won all the way, to the great
regret of Bloodshot, the moke closest to the winner’s
tail at the finish. After a trip to England, New-
haven was purchased by Harry Osborne, of Curran-
dooley, but failed to do what was expected of him
as a parent. About a year ago the old horse, deco-
rated with a coat as shaggy as Cocky’s whiskers,
was put into the saleyards at Orange (N.S.W.) and
exchanged for 25 guineas. The buyer gave him a
trial at his Jerula property, between Forbes and
Cowra. The funeral took place there. Aged 23.
There was actually an 1 of a difference—
also a £ of a margin. At Brisbane the other day
the bags were ready to lay Bolnoo for a small amount
at 15 to 1. The animal rolled in and each 10s. on
the tote (there were 30 tickets) yielded £42, or 83
to 1. The second favorite in the race was Boonoo.
It is alleged that a good many tickets were bought on
Bob-on by people who thought they were backing
Boonoo.

The W.A. Turf Club has bought out the
two unregistered concerns at Bicton and Goodwood.
Races will be run at the fonnerly-uuhallowed spots,
but less frequently than in the past and under
orthodox regulations. The amounts that changed
hands over the transactions have not been stated, but
it was less than a million, for the Bicton-Goodwood
meetings latterly have been poor things.
The Gawler (S.A.) Racing Club has
adopted the new_ Exchange Totalisator, which will
be duly installed at the course by the end of May.
If the Gabriel patent fulfils in practice all the pro-
mise of the working model it will be a bit of a faper
for the A.J.C. The patent machine adopted by that
club will cost about £lO,OOO to set up, whereas
the Gawler installation is estimated to cost less than
£lOOO.
* * * *
Patrobas,, by winning the King’s Plate on
the last day of the V.R.C. Autumn Meeting, raised the
total of his earnings to £16,157. On present w.f.a.
form he should bulge the purse further before the
season ends. About half his rake-in was through
the Melbourne Cup win.
Trainers still cling .to the notion thnt a
dose of alcohol can inspire certain horses to resolute
endeavor in a race. The theory has been proved
fallacious with human foot-racers. It was tested
at Harvard (U.S.) University, with the result that
all the doped athletes thought they had excelled
themselves, whilst the watch showed that not one
of them had equalled his best record. Whisky is a
mocker as regards the human animal. AVhy should
it be an aid to sustained exertion in horses?
Most of the Sydney Chosen were wishing
on Saturday that they had been betting at Fleming-
ton instead of Canterbury Park. Mo’s run of luck
has been none too good for a long time, and on
Saturday the crowd started hitting things up again
with renewed strength. Mo’s main protection from
disaster has been to immediately cramp the odds
down hard as soon as he detects the public starting
a rush. Saturday’s prices on winners: sto 4,4 to 1,
3t01,2t01, 5 to 2 and sto 4 on. A punter
playing up a sovereign on each race would have won
£l6 Is. What are. the chances of anyone specking
six successive winners anywhere? Meanwhile Ike is
hoping hard that he is not called up for service
before he gets to Randwick. There are always
chances of a kill there.
* * * *
The race for Canterbury Park (Sydney)
Handicap on Saturday was farcical. There were
only . three starters? and backers went baldheaded
for Rue. Victoria, who had as opponents Kelso’s old
nag Chid and Blackacre, who had captured the
event on this course some weeks before. The
race was run at a snail’s pace, and that fact
demoralised the favorite, who finished last, some
lengths behind, while the other two fought out a
grim struggle in the straight. They only occupied
2min. 25sec. to get over the mile and a quarter,
without stopping for refreshments.
* * * *
Bazetta, the mare which just squeezed
home in the Canterbury Flying Handicap last Satur-
day, hails from the north of N. S. Wales and seems
something out of the ordinary. She won the last
Armidale Cup (seven furlongs) from a good field
and has been successful in many events on her native
heath. This is the lady’s first visit to the city.
She may pick up a few more prizes -before returning
to the bush. In the same stable is the smart colt
Admiral Beatty, which as a two-year-old captured
an event at Moorefield. The Admiral has again
taken up his residence in Sydney.
There was no one rushing about with
excessively large wads at the yearling- and other
sales in Melbourne last week, but the sportsman
ready to take a chance was there, all the same. At
the sale of the Tye horses the imported mare Ellen
Graeme induced one of the Falkiners to produce
600 guineas, and for the imported filly by the White
Knight—Sal 850 guineas came from the same
quarter. If these animals are what they may be, the
investment is a' good one. If they are what some
animals turn out not to be —well, something else
might have been a better bargain.
A horse by Malster from Lady Trenton
looks well enough bred for anything. AVhen put up
at Melbourne five years ago a yearling with this
breeding was reckoned worth going nap on, and
1600 was handed over for him. Then the youngster
was led away and christened Burrinjuck, and the
rest was mostly a story of what would have happened
if something else had taken place. But last week
Burrinjuck turned up at Sandown Park to give
MacShortodds a lift by winning instead of losing.
He started at 25’s in a field of 10, and as nobody
wanted anything to do with him he arrived first
with news of joy for Mo. The nag that only wins
when he isn’t expected to is one the books ought to
provide with a pension.
Two fine efforts were made in Sydney
junior cricket on Saturday—Storey’s 197 out of
285 for Dailey against St. David’s; and left-hander
Alec Mcßeath’s 16 wickets for 62 runs for Camper-
down against Botany. Mcßeath was playing in pre-
miership cricket matches in the early ’9o’s, but his
awful fielding always kept him back.
O. Asher’s bag of nine wickets for 68
against North Sydney on Saturday lifted Sydney up
top in State premiership cricket. Sydney and Pad-
dington have. each won seven out of nine matches
(the others -were drawn), but the last outright win
gave Sydney the higher percentage. Another effort
worth a cheer was Balmain captain B. J. Folkard’s
11 wickets for 59 against Gordon. This lifted his
collection of wickets for the season to just under the
half-hundred.
* * * »
Prahran bowler Blackie had the job of
the season against Mcdbourne (the strongest batting
club in Vic.) on Saturday, and came out with four
wickets for 91 runs in a total of 258. Warwick
Armstrong (76) and Vernon Hansford (45) did much
to take the sting out of the attack.
The secretary of the League of N. 8.
Wales Wheelmen struck a commendable idea the
other day. He put it to his crowd that he was
going to enlist, and urged all Leaguemen who could
to get into action with him. About 200 pedalled
up and said they were willing, and the first bunch
of 50 hustled up to the barracks last week as if
Bill Martin were on their back wheel.

“Wart” : A. F. Sharland, mentioned in a
recent Sporting Note as the oldest competitor at
Hobart Regatta, was 60 years young on the day of
the regatta. His last performance was at the Tamar
Regatta in ’B7. When returning to the Speck, after
annexing most of the amateur events on the
Parramatta Sharland lost his sculling boat and the
trophies in the wreck of the Tasman on the Ilippo-
lyte Reef. Sharland used to train on the South lisle
River at Belle Vue, Epping, one of the finest fresh-
water courses in Australia. The late Wm. Gibson
ran a regatta there for several years, but it was
held only once after his death. One of these
days it will be revived and be one of the biggest re-
gattas south of the line, but that can only be after
the big estates, which are the curse of the Tasmanian
Midlands, have been broken up. The country round
about carries a smaller population than when Shar-
land trained there 40 years ago.-
Charles C. Acre was born in 1848 at
Sydney, and went aloft in 1917 from Maoriland.
For ' over a quarter of a century he was the main-
stay of M.L.’s oldest water carnival —the Auckland
Anniversary Regatta. Dacre saw this fixture first
when the main attraction was the Maori war-canoe
race. From the hapus of Waitemata and Ilauraki
in those days came brown brother of the old type—-
50 of him on each gun’l’—and as he rowed with
short paddles, loud yells and protruding tongue and
without any millinery, the barbaric proceedings were
something to remember. When decay of the native
ended canoe-racing, Henare’s place on the pro-
gramme was taken by the fastest vessels of their
size ever launched south of the line. Noble-looking
schooners of about 100 tons raced over a 70-mile
course. They in turn passed out, and in the early
’9o’s yacht-racing began to boom. Ever since then,
yachts built by the Logans and Baileys and motor
craft have been battling in the vicinity of Motuiti.
At the prize-giving in connection with the latest
fixture, Henry Parker, the chairman, told how he
and others once rowed a whaleboat’ 30 miles up the
coast to a regatta, and were only beaten by some
seconds. This ancient is the oldest surviving regatta
official. From the hollowed-out tree filled with
demented Henare to .the launch propelled by petrol
spells a Great Change.
* * * *
“Glial”: Doubtful commentary on what
Adelaide claims to be a world's record swim by S.
Mayor (one mile 300 yards in 26min. 9see) includes
the question whether the tide was not more respon-
sible than the swimmer. The tide of the Torrens!
The creek hasn’t the stamina to push its way to the
sea. Evidently a lot of knowledge was distributed
by that faked picture postcard, much in vogue in
Adelaide at the time of George Rex’s visit, depict-
ing the Ophir berthed in the Torrens alongside Elder
Park.
Herb McCoy and Llew Edwards met at
Sydney Stadium on Saturday. It was the sixth
collision. Two of the previous disturbances were
drawn (these were at catch-weights). From the
three on the lightweight scale Edwards had emerged
with the bacon. Weights on Saturday—McCoy,
9st. 101 b. ; Edwards, 9st. 81b. There were no beg-
pardons and very little clinching in the hard, fast
battle which followed. McCoy fought up to his very
best form, both in attack and defence. He started
off to win quick, but the Englishman met the
onslaught with cool dexterity and punch for punch,
and for most of the time thereafter made the fight.
Towards the end McCoy pulled himself together for
a great effort, and for a couple of rounds had a
margin in his favor. But he paid severely for it.
He was tiring away afterwards, and Edwards, whose
condition is alwayra perfect, kept handing over hard,
aggressive wallops as vimfully as ever. In the 19th
round he had the Victorian at his mercy, and in
the next proceeded to finish the argument. As
McCoy broke ground from a hard drive to the ribs
Edwards, with a right swing timed to a fraction,
landed his glove straight under the chin. McCoy
took the count and a bit over. He had fought with
a fine courage and skill, but Edwards is the speedier
and more enduring of the pair—and the brainier.
* * * *
Australia’s old friend Mick King turned
up in New York last month and explained that he
had journeyed there on purpose to get a match with
Darcy. Well, it might come off, but nobody who
remembers what a pity it was for Mick the night
they shoved him into the ring with Darcy at Rush-
cutter’s Bay would pay two cents for a front seat.
“Trot”: Does any decent Australian
sportsman honestly think that Darcy is getting less
than he deserved in N’York? None of my acquaint-
ance, anyhow. Let anyone imagine the U.S.A. being
tangled up in the war and Australia one day hearing
that a prominent boxer had skipped by the light of
the moon into the innards of a tramp steamer with
the design of surreptitiously reaching Sydney as a
place where he could fight for his own pocket instead
of his country, How many Australians would there
be who reckoned the visitor was getting a crooked
deal if the Government fired him out? Darcy looks
no better in the eyes of an American than ot an
Australian, and if he had had sense enough to make
inquiries to start with, could have found this out in
advance.
Gerald A. Taylor is causing Victorian
timekeepers to rub their eyes. In winning the
Shepparton Gift recently Taylor covered 128yds. in
12 l-ssec. —6yds. inside “evens.” Last week he
ran 75iyds. in 7 9-16 sec.—almost as near to “evens
as mathematics will go. And he is suspected ot
being able to travel still faster.
* * * *
According to the latest motor-car statistics
from the U.S.A., (label’s own country will soon be a
gigantic smell of petrol and a cloud of dust. Up to
the end of last June 4,932,455 cars —or one for every
20 Yankees —had been registered, which is nearly a
50 per cent, increase on the number in use 1L
months previously. Peaccmonger Ford is the most
prolific contributor to the motor traffic. His tain
for 1916 was 5P8,000 vehicles, and he expected to
increase his output by another quarter of a million
bv the end of the present year.

“P.F.” : About the Tasmanian bream (B.
22/2/’l7). I fancy that “T.’s” idea that they do
not go out to sea in winter if they can, but that
they get so flabby if the sea-water can’t get into
their river that they are not worth catching, is a
mistake. Anyway, it does not hold good of some
rivers on the Tasmanian East Coast. Sometimes
there will be plentv of bream in the river one sum-
mer ; then when it breaks out to the sea in the
autumn they disappear, and if it closes up during
the winter and there is no flood to break the bai
later there will be no bream in the river next sum-
mer. If they don't go to sea what becomes of them! 1
Besides, 1 have known them to be caught in nets in
the bays along the coast in the winter months and
you will not get them there in the summer. They
certainly breed in the brackish rivers; at any rate
during the summer I have seen plenty of little fellows
from an inch upwards swimming there.

“Wattle” : Get on to the blackfish which
is indigenous to some of the Monaro streams. Man}
people who have sampled both reckon they are far
better eating than rainbow trout, which are also to
be hooked in these streams. With the verdict I
agree, and sorry I am that blackfish are. not so
plentiful as formerly. One angler in whom is no
guile told me that the rainbow trout undoubtedly
prey on blackey. Another imported curse 1
Anthony Hall, just dead at Broken Hill
after a tragically short illness, was one of two
Adelaide lawyer brothers who went afield to practise.
“Bob,” after being in partnership with C. C. King-
ston, removed to Westralia, and prospered there.
“Tot” was just ready to hang out his brass plate
when Broken Hill got busy. He went there in
1888, and grew up with the place. A keen sport
and a successful athlete, he was patron or president
of most things in that line at “The Hill,” and was
equally ready to referee a fight or take the judge s
box at a race meeting. Also lie was a sound and
successful lawyer, and 'the local unions had a great
opinion of his work in arbitration cases. He lost
his elder son at Gallipoli, and his own turn has
come at a mere 52.
From V. J. Newton, of the Gippsland Co-
operative Co.: —
The correspondent who biographed me in recent
Bulletin personal notes didn’t add up properly. I
started life as a pupil-teacher, not as a monitor,
and I didn’t draw £1 a week. It was 16s. Bd.
The bush school is all right, but I was never
manager of the local butter factory, and I did not
start the Gippsland Co-operative Co. The original
organising work was done by Messrs. J. McKenzie
and A. Wyett, of Yarrain, and A. W. Wilson, of
Mirboo North. The last-named has practically, since
the inception of the company, been in charge, and it
is owing to his excellent administration that the
company owes 'the position it occupies to-day. I
swung into the movement officially about a year after
the co. was formed. The' co. has had a London rep.
these 11 years. I went there first in 1912, but have
been back in Victoria since March, 1916, doing my
little bit to gather the producers into the co-operative
fold.

THIS instep-strap shoe has been specially
designed to look trim and dainty
beneath those delightfully feminine
frills and flounced hems that have
returned to vogue again. It is a particularly
pretty shoe, with a fine air of individuality
ahout it. The front part is patent leather,
and the back and strap are black flowered
brocade, set off by an attractive slide and
ornament of dull silver. The picture gives
sonie idea of the happiness of this combina-
tion, but the shoe itself is far more charm-
ing than the picture. Made in the dainty,
short-fronted shape and with that smart
Cuban heel which makes walking so comfort-
able. it really is a “find” in the way of
shoes. And ladies will see other and equally
delightful shoes in the latest Bostoek ship-
ments, especially if they view them at once.

There are two co-operative cos. in the butter-
distributing business in N. S. Wales, and
this is tlic greater of them. It lias a very
big North Coast connection—it handles the
output of the Byron Bay Colossus, among
others —and has reached a turnover of two
millions a year. Last six months, to the end
of September, the actual figures were
£994,919; and for the current half-year they
will be much 'larger. Yet outstanding
accounts as at September 30 were only
£95,194 —a little more than a fortnight’s
turnover. Not the smallest service this co.
has rendered the butter-makers of the State
has been a rigid insistence on cash payments
in the retail trade. In addition to the butter
business the co. has nowadays two other sec-
tions—one handling grain, etc.; the other
fruit and vegetables. In both there is a
tremendous field for this experienced co. to
work to the advantage of both producer and
Consumer. Steady progress is being made in
both ; and valuable new work is being done
as the opportunity presents itself. For ex-
ample, in this very favorable season the crop
of lucerne in the Morpeth district, where the
co. has good hay storage, is very large; and
the price, owing to the abundance of feed, is
only a couple of pounds or thereabouts. The co.
is storing this hay, making liberal advances
on it, and presently it expects to do a good
turn to itself, to buyers and to producers.
The eo.’s profits from all sources are held
until after the March balance, when they are
cut up among co-operators and shareholders.
The latter are well content with a reasonable
6 p.c., knowing that what they don't get in
dividend they get either as direct payment
for their supplies or by way of bonus. One
way or another they get everything that a
thoroughly organised distributing agency can
get for their produce less only the actual cost
of distribution to the retailers. That, includ-
ing bad debts, cartage and repairs and all
other charges, ran to only 81 p.c. of the
turnover. That is co-operation.

A nice, comfortable little concern, making
nobody fat, but giving a good many people
the means to save besides showing them
what to save means. Pay £39 by monthly
instalments in 10 years and the society guar-
antees a return of £5O: and in addition to
the £5O there is invariably a bonus payable
out of surplus profits. For the past couple
of years this bonus has been an annual ss.
per £5O share; for five years before that it
averaged 3s. 6d. per annum. There is, it is
true, no independent guarantee that these
results will be available when the shares
mature; but there is a solid internal guaran-
tee in the shape of the reserves. In addi-
tion to the share capital in the business there
are some deposits. The next little table
shows how they have moved and how they
are employed :
1905. 1913. 1914. 1915. 1916.
Capital . . £46,904 £51,403 £53,575 £53,948 £48,745
Deposits.. 61,959 61,666 55,190 53,371 51,528
Mortgages 114,162 116,462 112,345 104,029 102,225
Besides the advances (mortgages) the co. lias
£18,683 of assets, mostly liquid; so the
security of the depositors is beyond question.
This paper doesn’t know what the future
profits of shareholders will be. The co. will
probably have to make good some losses of
principal when the tide runs out. But the
heaviest losses in such cases only happen
when a society owes so much that it can’t
afford to nurse a property which has been
thrown on its hands'and this society isn’t,
likely to be placed in that position.

“W.O.L.”: If the civilised people of
Europe keep on slaughtering each other
very -much longer, the mining industry of
the world will have to be reorganised, be-
cause tlie proper place to look for most
metals will lie on the battlefields of the Old
World. With the newer and less populous
countries being gutted of their mineral
wealth to be fired away in Europe, that
continent after the war should assume the
aspect of a gigantic scrapheap, only requir-
ing “picking over” to furnish enormous
stores of broken metal obtainable at the
cheapest mining cost imaginable, and the
business of farmer and old metal collector
will become inextricably mixed. In fact,
for some considerable period the mineral
harvest a careful European cocky will obtain
during the process of ploughing should over-
balance the value of the vegetable clean-up
afterwards, even in the best of seasons. It
will be a fine object-lesson on the indestructi-
bility of matter and the vanity of human
wishes if the German, who was largely in-
fiueneed by the thoughts of the iron supply
in his onslaught on Belgium and France,
should be wipoo out by an over-supply of
the metal he desired to obtain, while the
survivors derive great wealth by dry-blowing
and fossicking. Seriously, however, this
new source of supply is goiqg to have an
appreciable influence on several markets
after the guns cool.

“ Don’t wait until you are told by your employer that
you are too old=looking to hold your position, but
commence at once and use Mrs. Mary K. Chapman’s
Wonderful Specific. It will darken your hair natu=
rally and gradually, and you will look years younger.”
THIS GREAT SPECIFIC BY TRUE MERIT
FORCES ITSELF UPON THE PUBLIC.
IN business and professional life, when
energy and clear-headedness are
the measures of success, the busi-
ness man or woman recognises only too
well that the appearance of the first
grey hairs marks the world’s refusal to
recognise him or her any longer as
young enough.
Looked Too Old at Forty.
Numerous instances have come under
my notice of men and women who had
held responsible positions for years, and
who at the age of 40 or thereabouts, be-
cause their hair had turned grey, prob-
ably through mental strain, were
pushed out of their position even
though they were comparatively young
and vigorous. Grey or white hair is
not a definite sign of old age, but un-
fortunately it is considered to be proof
of advanced age by the general public.
Ihere are hundreds of men and women
in all parts of Australia who are con-
siderably over 6 0 and whose hair was
as white as snow, but who now look 20
years younger because they used my
Genuine Specific,
No Dyes or Stains,
My Genuine Specific is not a dye or
stain, and you need not be afraid of
discoloring your scalp, your towel, pil-
low cases, linen, hands, etc. It contains
no stain, paint or dye. This can be
proved by pouring a little on a sheet of
white paper. It is almost as clear as
water and absolutely harmless to the
hair. Once the hair has been restored
to the original color it can be kept so
with but very slight attention.
Gradual Satisfactory Darkening.
The process is a natural, gradual one.
There is no suddenness, and the dark-
ening is not patchy, as is the' case
when applying ordinary dyes.
None Too Aged.
None are too old and no case fails to
respond to this treatment. Of course
the quantity required varies in indi-
vidual cases, for instance, a person who
is only just beginning to turn grey
would not need to use as much as the
person whose hair is white. In every
case where the hair is grey, faded or
bleached, the color can be restored re-
gardless of age or the cause. No
matter how often you have been disap-
pointed in the past, you are invited to
test this new discovery at very small
cost.
How Readers May Test this Discovery
at Small Cost.
One thing to remember is that a
small bottle may be obtained at small
cost by those who are grey, or those
whose hair is faded and lustreless and
this may be done with perfect safety
and certainty in the privacy of their
own home. You will be able to say
that you have at last a perfect remedy.
Post This Coupon Now.
To Mrs. MARY K. CHAPMAN,
Suite 56, Falmouth Chambers, 117 Pitt Street, Sydney
enclose two pence in stamps for postage , etc . Please send
your Genuine Specific, by return post, under plain cover.
(just below G.P.0.).

As events are shaping, Australia will
have to raise another 25 or 30 millions dur-
ing the next 12 months for war purposes;
and it simply must not go to John Bull for
any of the money if it wants to do the
decent tiling. John lias troubles enough of
his own. It is time the financial men of
the Commonwealth put their heads together
and devise* plans for helping to raise this
vast sum of money with the least possible
dislocation of private enterprise. Generally
speaking, the solidest men in finance are
not politicians: they are not built to wave
their arms through electioneering cam-
paigns. A helpful plan might be to consti-
tute a sort of Advisory Board, consisting of
eight or 10 men who have become eminent
in the money-making line —men like
Braddon, of Dalgety’s; Palfreyman, of the
Jam combine; Knox, of the Sugar co.;
Teece, of the A.M.P.; Garvan, of the
M L.C.; Colin Templeton, of Mt. Lyell and
a dozen Melbourne cos.; and a hanker or
two. It seems a waste of material not to
invite the help of men of this stamp. Lloyd
George did it in England long ago.

There is a
GOODYEAR TYRE
made to suit every car, from the cheapest Runabout to the
most expensive Limousine.
Fords, Maxwells, Chevrolets and many other light cars all claim economy on tyres. But the tyre
cost per mile is still further reduced if Goodyear Tyres are used on all four wheels.
Goodyear Tyres are standard equipment on Packard, Pierce Arrow, White and Winton Cars, the highest-grade cars
made in America, and the same supervision is exercised in manufacture, the same high-grade material is used in
making Goodyear Tyres for your Ford, your your Chevrolet, or any other car, as is exercised in making
tyres for those luxurious equipages listed at the top of this paragraph.
There is only one Goodyear quality. It is the same in the little 30 x3|as in the imposing looking 38 xsi All
Weather Tread. And both give equal service under their rated proportionate load.
No other tyre has obtained such universal adoption as Goodyears have, and to those who use Goodyears the reason is
not far to seek.
Not only for inch-size rims, but for all millimeter fiittings there is a Goodyear Tyre - plain casing, or with All
Weather Tread.

iMndmill
Australia's early Windmills were costly and cumbersome, as the illustration of
our first Windmill (part of which still exists) pictures. But Australia’s first
and foremost Windmill —the “Billabong' ’ —is as light-running as a watch, as
graceful as a greyhound, is full of efficiency, and is not costly, considering its
splendid substantial construction, its many improvements and the great amount
of work it will do, as well as the great amount of labour it will save.
The “Billabong" lays claim to the name of “Australia’s First Wind-
mill* —easily first! For instance, the new system of split-bearings obviates the
taking down of the mill when after years of service new bearings may be needed.
The patent Lubricating device is so effective that the mill is only lubricated
when it is turning, and thus there is no waste of lubricant.
The self-regulator is easily first in the automatic sense, as
the effect of cyclones is reduced to that of a strong breeze.
First and foremost, its simplicity of‘construction is its
success. There are less gear parts, and therefore less
trouble. The material &.nd workmanship are the best
possible; in fact, it is “The Mill that Will."

IN
BANANA LAND
The Coalition will have to provide a fresh
candidate for the Lilley seat in the House
of Reps. Strimm, the dear old Liberal who
has snoozed for the constituency for five or
six years, is definitely taking his pillow into
private life.
Two new appointments have been made
in connection with Rananaland’s State enter-
prises. Harry Webber, pubkeeper at Yep-
poon, has been chosen to manage the State
thirst shop at Babinda at £9 a week. He
has been in the drink business for 24 years
and has a first-class reputation in Central
and Western Queensland. A. A. Gilmore,
works-manager of the Queensland Meat
Export Co., has been appointed to control
the State fish business at £lO a week,
although when applications were first called
only £7 was offered.
One important matter was overlooked
\vhen Assistant-Minister for Justice Fi-helly
tackled the problem of lower-court reform.
For years the inquest work at Brisbane was
performed by a J.P. No verdict was ever
returned, the depositions in every case going
to the Crown Law Office. Some time ago
R. D. Neilson, who carried on the work,
readied the age limit and was retired, and
the work was divided amongst the P.M.’s.
But verdicts are not given even by these
trained men, and there is thus the time-
wasting farce of a Court delving for hours
into matters about which it is only allowed
to whisper an opinion to the Crown Law
Office, which hasn’t had the benefit of sizing
up the witnesses.
“L.S.” : The Customs Department has sur-
passed all previous red-tape records. When
Sergeant J. P. Barnes, a Brisbane man, was
killed on the Sinai Peninsula during an
engagement on January 9, his commanding
officer sent the dead soldier’s sword and
bridle to his mother in Brisbane. Before
handing over the gear the Customs Depart-
ment insisted on the payment of 2s. lid.
duty !
“Boro-Belle”: A fisherman up North had a
new home presented to him by the Pacific
the other day. Some time ago a scow was
wrecked in the vicinity, arid one morning the
cabin arrived on the beach opposite his hut.
By evening, with the aid of saplings and a
oxen, the newly-found home
had been hoisted above high-water mark.
Simon Peter, his wife and three little ones
reckon old Neptune the best landlord they
ever had.
“A.C.”: Some of the Kendall River
binghis recently recruited for work on pearl-
ing and beche-de-mer luggers at Thursday
Island don’t seem to be wildly enthusiastic
about the job. One lugger left a few weeks
.ago with a crew composed of master, mate
and four of these natives. Two of the latter
shammed sickness and remained on board
while the captain, mate and the other two
binghis took the dinghy and went on a beche-
de-mer hunt. When the white men were a
good distance on the reef the two blacks
sneaked to the dinghy, rowed to the lugger,
cast away the anchor chain, and sailed off
for the binghis’ Blighty, with the dinghy
trailing behind. The marooned white men’s
only chance to escape starvation was to
reach a small island two miles away. They
swam the distance and existed on oysters
and water for three days, when another
lugger hove in sight. They signalled it, but
were unobserved, so the master took another
plunge and swam six miles*out before he
was observed and picked up. If the tropical
North hadn’t impaired his health he would
probably have swum on to Thursday and
reported the matter to the boss. The stolen
lugger was found deserted at Margaret Bay.
N.Q. coast.
The luck of Thursday Island: —
In the far-off days of peace most of the niotlier-
of-pcarl obtained in Torres Strait went, to Europe
and was converted into ornaments and buttons; but
with the closing of the European ports the bottom
THE SEXES.
HIM: “When a woman says ‘No’ she is always ready to he convinced.”
HER: “Yes. and token a man says ‘No’ he only wants a little persuading to say. ‘I don’t
mind if I do.’”
fell out of the market, and the pearlers watched
their fleets eating their heads off in idleness, and
sighed and spoke of bankruptcy. As the war went
on and the chances of a quick peace disappeared, it
looked as though all hands were going to be dead
broke. Thirsty’s business people even prophesied
the abandonment of the island. Then somebody dis-
covered that there was a market in Japan for troelhis
shell for button-making. Trochus is distributed
more or less plentifully over the reefs of the Strait,
and, being in shallow water, is easily harvested. So
the pearlers took their expensive diving gear ashore,
put swim-diving crews in the luggers, hunted the
outfits to sea and turned their prospective deficit
into a credit balance. The evil-smelling trochus
shells, so recently considered of little or no value,
are now being shipped away by hundreds of tons,
and everybody is happy. The only cockroach in the
molasses is that they are going to a foreign country;
but, of course, Australia, being a country that has
to import its clothes-pegs, couldn’t be expected to
understand button-making.

The Aster Patch.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
That is a lady in delicate mauve,
But she’s not so prim as you think she is !
A wasp in the noon-hour claimed her his,
And goes bristling pride to some listening-
grove,
There to boast through an afternoon
Because of petals that fade—so soon !
’There is a disc where a dawn-flush clung,
A friendly color, like maiden’s lips,
Or the sheaf of her pointed finger-tips
<>n a hand that is soft and young.
Where the blood rushes as though to press
An answering throb from his palm’s caress !
.That is the regal purple shade,
A haughty prelate who governs kings.
And the bee that loves it has gold-dust
wings
And sumptuous baskets with pollen weighed ;
Where the lesser flowers their censers
sway
It lifts its head in a regal way.
This is a white-smocked chorister;
Faint, aloof as a saint it smiles,
Till the foliage arches to unlit aisles
Where flowers lie round the face of her;
And the soul that soars on a shaft of light
Is white....as these aster blooms are
white.
Gather them up in a dream bouquet!
Tie them together with magic strings!
Flutter above them the phantom wings
Of fragile moths of a bygone day,
And tell me....you to your garden wise.
Have I not seen them with Fancy’s eyes V
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, March 10, 1917.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
The outstanding feature of the week was
a fuss in connection with the Women’s
Recruiting Committee. A request had been
made by the committee (or a section of it)
that eligibles in the Public Service should be
“combed out,” so that they might be “in-
duced” to enlist. At a subsequent committee
meeting Mrs. uyan and Mrs. Lennon
resigned, they being opposed to anything
resembling coercion. Incidentally Mrs. Lennon
has three sons in khaki. Before resigning
Mrs. Ryan moved that the committee disso-
ciate itself from the "combing out” idea,
but her proposal was lost by a solid majority.
So the State turns down conscription and
votes for voluntary service; and then we ask
the State to do by backstairs influence what
it will not do openly and above-board. Yet
our men must have reinforcements. A nice
tangle that awful little person in Melbourne
has got us into !
The only newspaper in Queensland which
has not backed up the committee of the
Repatriation Scheme is the Brisbane Tele-
graph. The Repatriation Committee has,
therefore, announced that no more informa-
tion will be supplied to that paper, and that
its reporters will be no longer permitted to
attend the committee’s meetings. And that’s
another way to win the war.
Died in a London Hospital, of wounds,
Lieut. Lionel Barnett, formerly manager of
Stainburn Downs station. He was a well-
known swimmer before the dove of peace
went to the clouds.
~ Major W. E. Markwell, D. 5.0., a young
Queenslander who left with the first L.H.
Itegt. and saw service in Gallipoli and later
in Egypt, has the unique record of having
had 2i years’ active service without once
leaving his regiment. II is parents, the
John Markwells, are well-known residents of
Beaudesert.
Mrs. J. Stodart organised a successful
trip in aid of the War Nurses’ Fund on
Wednesday night. The boys of the Rifle
Range camp put their shoulders to the wheel,
and much money was rolled in.
Margaret Dalrymple and Madge Roe, two
girls who have done exemplary service for
the Soldiers’ Comforts Fund as joint secs.,
are leaving Brisbane. The committee and
intimate supporters of the fund farewelled
them with many gifts.
Last week I mentioned the glut of pine-
apples and the opportunity to get cheap
war-time food. Since then I have learnt
that local canners have done everything
possible to find a market outside Australia.
Samples of pineapple jam have been sub-
mitted to the War Office, and offers to sup-
ply a quantity of the fruit have been cabled,
but no answer has been received. Mean-
while the Chamber of Manufactures is doing
what it can to prevent the disgraceful waste.
Ipswich lost a melodious tenor and a goou
fellow when Capt. W. 11. Nieolls was killed
in France. It was not the first bit of lead
he had stopped, for he was twice wounded
in the Gallipoli unpleasantness. Nieolls
was a Welshman, and left Australia as a
corporal, advancing to the rank of second
lieutenant before the evacuation. In the
Somme argument he was wounded through
the shoulder, and while on furlough found
time to become engaged to a girl in Wales.
Very soon after returning to the firing-line
he was killed while leading a charge.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
W. J. Overell, head of the firm of Overells,
Ltd., Brisbane, has closed his last account.
A quiet, unobtrusive man, he took practi-
cally no part in public affairs, devoting his
entire attention to his large fluff business.
Twelve years ago his premises in Edward-
street were burnt to the ground, some deaths
and many sensational escapes resulting. The
firm then transferred its operations to the
Valley. Two younger members of the family
are in khaki.
Ex-Railways Minister Adamson boasted
the other day that he had controlled the
Department “in harmony with the principles
of the Labor platform.” By a coincidence
the Auditor-General’s report on railway
finance was published the same day. As it
revealed a deficit of £610,506 during the
Adamson regime, despite increased freights
and fares, evidently “the principles of the
Labor platform” and sound finance are not
on speaking terms.
Walter Pickering, clerk of the Woongarra
Shire Council, passed through the boundary
gate last week. The old man had seen some
stirring times in his 50 years in Queensland,
having been sergeant in charge of native and
mounted police in the riotous days of the
Palmer. Twenty years or so back he moulted
the uniform and set up in Bundaberg as a
commission agent.
“Warrego Bill”: For the big-shout com-
petition let me enter Woodhatch, who kept
the Royal Hotel at Adavale in ’BB. Wood-
hatch disposed of the pub at a handsome
figure. On the day of quitting he called in
all hands to imbibe at his expense. The
round-up was about 250. They started Oi!
champagne and went right through the stock.
The fame of Innisfail (once Geraldton)
as the wettest corner of Queensland is almost
obliterated. Three places had heavier falls
than that moist retreat in the last 12 months,
and Gharleville, in the heart of the alleged
dry belt, came out with 1096 points for the
year, as against a 40-year average of 300
points.
For 35 years Maryborough log-rollers per-
suaded Governments to spend tens of thou-
sands in dredging the Mary flats, on the plea
that a splendid river-port would eventuate.
Now the State has ladled out £50,000 for a
pier at Port Maryborough (Urangan), which
makes Maryborough as a port a thing of the
past. Maryborough, Rockhampton, Towns-
ville —what a trio of tragedies for the
Queensland Treasury!

Why Have Grey Hair?
When a simple, old-fashioned and harmless
recipe will correct it.
Few people know that grey hair is not a
necessary feature of age—that it can be
avoided without resorting to hair dyes A
very old. home-made remedy will turn the
hair back to a natural color in a few days.
It is only necessary to get from the chemist
two ounces of concentrate of tammalite and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum. Apply
this simple lotion to the hair for a few
nights with a small sponge and you" will
soon have the pleasure of seeing the greyness
disappear. This recipe is perfectly harmless,
is neither sticky nor greasy, and has given
perfect satisfaction for many generations to
those in possession of the secret. — Advt. [4

£l/10/- per cent, will be paid on 15th June, 1917.
INSTALMENT APPLICATIONS. — If deposit and first instalment paid or or before Bth
March, 1917, interest at £3/14/- per cent, will be paid on 15th December, 1917.
If deposit, first and second instalments are paid after Bth March, but on or before
2nd April, 1917, interest at £3/12/- per cent, will be paid on 15th December, 1917.
All other instalments will be due on the dates shewn in the Prospectus.
After the dates mentioned above, interest will be paid half-yearly at rate of 4J per
cent, per annum.
Applications will be received at all banks, State Savings Banks and Post Offices where
Money Order Business is transacted.

The recruiting effort and the social dash
sometimes get hopelessly tangled. In one
suburb the mayor’s wife, intent on good
deeds and getting her name in the papers,
has been taking a great interest in the re-
cruiting movement. Latterly she has started
to bustle the officials around like pawns in
a game. It didn’t work long with Tomp-
kins. It was Tompkins here and Tompkins
there, until at last Tompkins, a returned
warrior, swelled up and burst. “Aw, blazes!”
he said (or words to that effect). “Stick to
y’r own job!” Mrs. Mayor indignantly fell
back several yards on a front of 30in., and
called for reinforcements; but hubby wisely
went and hid in the cupboard until the
danger was past. Then he came tactfully
out with a box of cigars, and did his bit
to shove the war along.
Another Australian “sub." in the British
Navy has won the Distinguished Service
Cross—Robert Alexander Little, of Windsor
(Vic.), a Scotch College boy. Leaving Mel-
bourne in 1015, he gained a commission in
the Navy and joined the Air Service. One
of his busy days was November 23, .1916.
when, after firing 20 rounds at an enemy
plane, he was driven off' by two others. Spot-
ting a fourth hostile machine less than an
hour later, he signalled two Nieuport planes,
led them against the foe, and brought it
down in flames after firing 250 rounds.
Eleven days later, Little, acting as escort
on a bombing raid, fired one round at a
small hostile scout and then his gun jambed.
To his horror he also found that he could
not climb. Instantly he dived while he
wrenched at his gun, the pursuing scout
meantime firing 400 rounds at him in 30
seconds from two machine guns, reopening
later at 20 yards. Every shot missed, and
Little escaped bv whizzing into a ploughed
field.
William Williamson, who is awaiting his
century in the Vic. Mallee, saw the opening
of the railway between Manchester and
Liverpool in 1830. He remembers how the
crowd called out that the mad thing would
never start, and also how when it did start
they caught , hold of the wheels and yelled
that it would never stop.
“Mark VII.”: I am a bit late, but I want
to put a shell through “E.D.’s” statement
(B. 25/1/T7) that Australia’s first shot in
this war was fired from Queenscliff (Vic.).
One of the guns at Fort Nepean, which is
on the opposite side of Port Phillip Heads,-
roared the warning. I served for some
months afterwards on the same weapon.
The gunlayer who pressed the trigger and'
so fired the gun electrically was Gunner
Jack Purdue, R.A.G.A., and not the late
Sergeant (then Corporal) Young. By the
way, the knowledge of the stay-at-home
citizen about military matters makes me
stagger. When I had received my mobilisa-
tion order during the first week of August,
1914, I went into a big hardware shop in
Melbourne for a couple of things to com-
plete my kit. The news of the famous first
shot had just come through, and the iron-
monger was telling another customer that
he had heard there had been shooting at
the Heads. He said he thought it was the
Cerberus engaging hostile craft. I had not
the heart to disturb his faith in the ancient
warship. She has lain off the mouth of the
Yarra for years, stripped of- engines and
guns, and acts as a storesliip and a “clink”
for bluejackets.
“Marloo”: A few years ago a British
steamer which had stranded on the South
American coast was sold as she lay for
£l5OO. The purchasers repaired her, and
after using her for trading purposes she was
recently sold for £320,000. London reckons
this the record individual shipping invest-
ment.
“8.P.”: In the Winton (Vic.) district
they don’t believe that the deceased ought to
monopolise the local cemetery ; and the liv-
ing are making a pretty good thing out of
it. Pines were planted there for ornament
27 years ago, and now £134 an acre profit is
being made from selling the timber.
Burgoyne and Co., who have a 750,000-
gallon wine vault at Ruthergleii (Vic.), are
going to make real sherry, or know the rea-
son why. They have the right kind of
vine, and lately they imported from Spain
the “sherry fungus” and turned it loose on
the casks, in the hope that it will give the
grape-juice the genuine flavor. So far it
seems to be working all right.
“Ivaree” : Y'ou can do some idiotic things
in a war of concealment. In a warm sector
near us a certain colonel had a fine battery
placed like a pill advertisement on a hill
in full view of Fritz, his sausages and his
’planes. When he was remonstrated with
he replied that he wasn’t dopy, or words to
that effect. lie seemed to be right, too, for
his guns never drew a single shell. The
only precaution taken was never to fire at
night. None could explain his luck until
a Fritz ’plane landed in our lines and its
boss was taken prisoner. On being asked
the reason of the battery’s exemption from
shells he replied knowingly: “The one on
the hilltop? Ach, we saw straight away
she was a dummy. My friendts. if you rill
place dummy guns, vy do you do it so obvi-
ously.” Honesty is the best policy when
you want to pull a strategist’s leg.
F. .T. Riley, who is having a go for Senate
Labor selection in Victoria, is a young and
long-haired Yarra Bank orator who, during
the conscription campaign, led a procession
of Antis out of the Guild Hall in Swanston-
street up to the steps of Parliament House.
He is a builder’s laborer, more forceful than
eloquent, and a very big Note in the reace
Alliance.
Adam Lindsay Gordon’s wife is still living
in South Aus. After the poet’s death, now
42 years ago, she married Peter Low, and
had a large family. Gordon’s only child
died young.
“Constable XXX.”: We were searching
for the body of the old man in the river
where he was supposed to have been drowned,
and someone remarked that he must have
drifted downstream. “I don’t know,” said
one of his sons. “He was such a contrary
old blanker he most likely went up-stream.”
However, it was found that the corpse had
followed ordinary etiquette, and floated down
the river.

The Inky Way.
“Quid” : It was another Bananaland paper
that brought the blush of shame to the local
cheek when it complained that the Railway
Department had refused to attach sleeping-
cars to the trains returning from the Show,
though the Show officals had promised to
guarantee a minimum of 200 births.
“John Inky”: During the recent hearing
ol: the A.J.A.’s claims before the Federal
Arbitration Court, the question of the quali-
fications of sub-editors was intruded. One
witness declared that the sub. of the Age
was. “the best in Australia.” The testi-
monial came from the assistant-sub., and the
arguments and general dogfight that have
ensued can be heard above the howls for
W. M. Hughes’s pelt.

Thomas Burke Daly, the journalist who
was swept out to sea and drowned while
fishing at Bondi (N.S.W.). was a nephew
of Burke the explorer. His brother Will,
who was for years a pressman in Launceston
(Tas.), was lately killed at the Front.

The Vic. T ypographical Society is having
a jubilee. . The present is hardly a time
for uproarious rejoicings, so the society is
merely going to treat itself to a commemora-
tive medal, at a cost of £BO, for little Willie
to drop down the sink. But if it only treats
itself to a medal it is going to treat the Mel-
bourne Hospital to £5OO for the endowment
of a bed—-so it really doesn’t matter what
little Willie does with dad’s medal.
