THE PRESIDENT INACTIVE.
TAKEN OVER BY SWITZERLAND.
Berne, Feb. 8. —Switzerland has agreed to take
charge of American interests in the United States.
Time something definite was done about
that vacillating country.
* * * *
George McGowan, who is having a cut at
Billson, M.L.A., for Labor selection at Fitz-
roy, has pushed his barrow around a bit in
Victoria. From compositor on Kilmore
Advertiser he came to the troubles of
manager and dropped them to take on more
trouble on the Tungamah Independent. He
was printer and publisher of Walhalla
Miner, but got out of the job when the
Long Tunnel mine went smash after paying
about a million in divs. He next edited
Kerrigan Advertiser, and, later, Sheppar-
ton Advertiser. Then he bobbed up as
Labor candidate for Lowan, and was beaten
by Menzies (Lib.). At the last State elec-
tions he walloped Jack Cain for the right
to be outed by Andy Robertson (Lib.) at
Bulla. He is now organiser for the Imple-
ment Makers’ Union, and is young enough
to snap his fingers in the face of Time.
* * * *
“Musketoon”: The Bulletin’s par.
(25/1/T7) about the man who ran Gus
Millar’s paper single-handed, ’ reminds me
of a couple of others. Patterson, of the
Tottenham (N.S.W.) Peer, is editor, type-
setter, business manager, job-printer, proof-
reader and devil combined. He is also the
coroner in his spare time. Sometimes he
has to “sit on the body” when he has no
spare time, as in the case of the murdered
Constable Duncan. On that occasion the
paper was issued three days behind
schedule. Major Keegan acted similarly on
the paper at Nimitybelle (N.S.W.), but the
work was so severe on him that he not long
ago leased Harry Pinkstone’s Hawkesbury
Herald. Pinkstone w T ent up to Coota-
mundra (N.S.W.) to keep his old dad's
paper going.

Black-and-whiter Mick Paul sought to
enlist a week or so ago ; but as one of his
eyes, owing to an accident in childhood, can
be left on the mantelpiece without incon-
venience, the verdict went against him.
Mick said —but perhaps better not repeat it.
THE INFLUENCE OF WASHINGTON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Count Bernstorff said that since he had come to
Washington he had never told am’ lies to newspaper-
men.—Cable.
They say that there are holy wells
That heal men halt and blind from birth,
And saints can still work miracles
With relics that they left on earth;
But what is there beneath the sun
Can beat the power of Washington!
O boy who never told a lie,
How well you played your earthly part!
Let Brother Bernstorff testify
How you have wrought a change of heart!
O sacred town! Long may you keep
Your power of turning goats to sheep!
VICTORIA.
RANN DALY.

The Juggler.
Long ago and long ago, no matter where
nor when,
There lived a Fool who played with words
to charm his fellow men.
JESTER or Juggler, power he had,
A wizard of rare skill was he ;
Whatever might in words be clad
He seized on for his alchemy.
The changes of a tragic day,
The motes that in a sunbeam throng,
Lost leaves or laughter blown his way
Were welded in the gold of song.
Sometimes, may be, sedately sweet
He drew the love-throes of the soil
Through ripening wonder of the wheat,
And kingliness of willing toil;
Then sudden as the wind-vane whirls,
Wearied of chanting graver joys,
With limpid syllables for pearls
He threaded sounds in jewelled toys.
Like hail capricious April slants,
Like apple petals on the torm,
He tossed the clinking consonants.
And velvet vowels into form,
In music bright as fountain spray,
Gentle as frightened Youth’s first kiss;
Folk listened, and then went their way
With, “What an idle Fool is this!”
Oft when his shrewd and merry glance
Saw right by folly overborne
And courage cringing before chance,
His lines were scourges of light scorn,
Flails of fantastic mockery fraught
With subtle force to clarify ;
Men found the harvest of their thought
Winnowed and clean, and guessed not why.
But as with time his cunning grew
More humble and more truly wise,
His only guerdon that he knew
A spell to heal and tranquillise
Poor troubled souls with song, again
He bent his eagle gaze to find
Hid in the very heart of pain
Joy, and more Joy, for sad mankind.
And when Love paused by him, then fled
(Love cannot look on motley long),
He took the smart of tears unshed,
The smothered anguish of his wrong,
His fair hopes crushed in sorrow grim,
And from them, with a twisted smile,
He wrought a splendid bridal hymn
That rang down many a rose-strewn aisle.
The amber witchery of the grape
Sparkled afresh within his rhyme;
For him the curious foxglove’s shape
Bade impish carillons to chime ;
With daydreams of a lonely maid.
With babble of a two-years child
Spun into lullabies, he played
Till even mourning mothers smiled.
He caught the ghost-voice in the shell,
And set the passionate mystery free;
To him the wild fawn of the dell
Revealed her timid ecstasy ;
Romance of every violet
Whispered where dimpling water runs,
All pure young secrecies he set
In folk-songs for the little ones.
So he grew old and grey, alone,
Alone in motley, singing still
With mellow lips that would not moan ;
And sometimes one would praise his skill,
But ere his ears such sweetness drank
They heard poor pride and longing’s knells ;
’Twas but a juggling mountebank.
’Twas but a Fool in cap and bells.
How sore he winced when children fleered ;
Yet toiled untiring to the end ;
But when the dark Deliverer neared
He hailed him thankfully as Friend.
Numbed by the shadow icy-strong.
Tortured, he wove, with failing breath,
A little gay heart-breaking song,
A daisy wreath to pardon Death.
Oh, long ago and long ago, no matter where
nor when,
Died that poor Fool who sought with ivords
to win his fellow men.
VICTORIA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Cash or Kind.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DICK WHITSUNDAY, in the schooner
Three Kings, sailed up to the lagoon
and island of Benedire an hour after
the dawn-wind had begun to crinkle the
water to the surface of a silk chiffon. He
had been in sight of the island the day
before, but too late to come to an anchorage,
and so had stood on and off all night. And
now the Three Kings floated placidly through
the opening in the reef; and, as the day
became majestically complete, she dropped
anchor in 16 fathoms.
Dick Whitsunday had started out, not as
an actively bad man, but as a man careless
of others—an egoist going easily down the
labent way of degeneration. A man taller
than he appeared, the discount of height
being by a fleshiness that yet made him very
active and strong; a round head, hair once
ginger and going grey, a ginger moustache
over a lip so thick that it seemed to have
been punched to swollenness; slanting
corners to the eyes, which were small and
light-blue in a great expanse of dirty white.
A man of strong will and little mind. The
kind 'to whom a little authority is fatal —to
themselves as to others.
He stood by the rail of the Three Kings
looking down at the lucent green water of
the lagoon, and then at the village on the
beach framed in its nipa and cocoanut palms
and breadfruit trees, the palms threshing
their fronds forever in a maddening monot-
ony, although the air was scarcely ruffled by
its own breathing. He noted the natives
ashore, moving and intent on the schooner,
but showing no inclination to meet him at
close quarters: and at this, rare among
peoples with all a monkey’s inquisitiveness,
he was puzzled.
Striding up the beach at a decisive gait
that made the crescent-shaped group of
natives give way, and involuntarily so
straightening the line, he halted before an
old and grizzled man, the chief of Benedire,
now long past his prime, and retaining his
position as many old men in white govern-
ment maintain theirs—by the conservatism
of their middle-aged subjects and the igno-
rance and shyness of youth.
“H’llo, Ringworm,” said Whitsunday, his
quick, pig-like eyes observing with one glance
the condition of the chief’s skin. “Why you
no been sendem out plenty boy longa boat?
I been catch ’em trade ; plenty bead, plenty
’bac-ca, plenty jew’s-harp I stop. Why you
no been sendem plenty stroqg-feller boy?”
The- chief falteringly replied that “all
feller boy no been like go way longa ship ; all
feller boy wantem stop longa boy place” ; and
the murmurs of the crowd told Whitsunday
that he and the Three Kings had a bad name,
and that none of the natives cared to trust
themselves aboard of the schooner.
That made him the more eager to get
them. Their unwillingness to recruit argued
to the blackbirder a tremendous value, and a
Benedire kanaka seemed then to Whitsunday
equal to any two natives of Buka or the
Shortlands, or of any other place from the
Admiralties to the Calvados Chain.
So he argued, and he sent for the trade in
the boat, and tried to dazzle them, and they
still refused, with almost pathetic attempts
at apology, as if shunning quarrel at any
cost. Naturally their deprecation of refusal
made Whitsunday more insistent; and at
last the threats of an old woman on the
outskirts of the crowd —an old woman who
cried that her son had been taken by the
Three Kings and had died, as was reported
by his returned mates, from ill-treatment ai
sea —became general; and the old chief found
his courage again, and told Whitsunday to
his teeth that he was a bad man.
The blackbirder had. discretion as well as
valor; he retreated carelessly and with his
back turned, never looking over his
shoulder at them as if he defied them
to kill him from behind. He entered
the boat, kicking the kanaka crew
left and right, and shouting out to the people
of Benedire that he would return in a moon
and force them to acknowledge his authority.
As the boat was half a cable’s length from
the beach the crowd parted to let through a
big, bronze-black young man, not much more
than a boy in years, but mature at 20 by
reason of climate and training. His face was
transfigured by rage momentarily replaced by
despair; the quickness of his intellect showed
in the capacity of his face to express change
of emotions so rapidly. He dashed through
the crowd, pursued by a beautifully-formed
woman, naked but for her lava-lava and a
string of beads; at the sea edge he turned,
and with uplifted hand cursed and spurned
them all —the woman, the broken semi-circle
of natives and the island itself. He ran into
the water till it was thigh-deep; and then
swam for the boat with a long steady crawl-
stroke that ate the distance.
“Backwater, you cows!” cried Whitsun-
day, seeing the swimmer, his eyes glistening
at the thought of a recruit being given him,
through the caprice of a woman, against all
the opposition of the island. “Backwater!
Jumping Moses! He’s a boy to make a song
about.”
The boat backed and the swimmer quickly
drew up to it, threw himself across the
side and sprawled, earning a curse from
Whitsunday because drops of sea-water
fell from the glistening body on the spotless
raiment of the blackbirder.
“What name you?” asked Whitsunday.
“Madeno name I stop.”
“Why you come? Been makem big-fellow
row alonga wife?”
The big lustrous eyes flashed in passion,
and then were deeply troubled and sorrowful
again.
“That one woman belongem me talk alto-
gether alonga another feller too much.”
“I see—jealous. You’re not the first man
who ran away to the wars to get even. You
catchem plenty other boy friend?”
Madeno answered more spiritedly than
before, as if the remembrance of many
friends brought back most of his native
courage.
“Plenty friend belongem me. When I go
way they cry too much.”
“Good enough !” said Whitsunday, know-
ing that many of Madeno’s friends would
follow him to unnecessary labor and exile.
He counted on those island friendships of
the heroic Roman type—the clean attach-
ment of clean youth.
“Give way, you black cows- —you pull
like a lot of soldiers. Put your backs into
it and make the ship ! Go to it!”
As the boat lifted and surged forward
under the last ounce of power in the sweat-
ing crew, a dozen of Madeno’s friends, dis-
regarding the cries of old men and women,
their fathers and mothers, and the noisy dis-
suasions of younger women, their sweet-
hearts and wives, loosed the silent clinging
of children and broke away from the group
in the village street, ran into the sea, and
swam for the schooner lying within the
entrance of the lagoon.
Whitsunday looked back, counted the
heads rising and falling like cocoanuts on the
long swell suddenly succeeding a stagnant
sea, a'nd grinned. Well had he cause to be
self satisfied. In that jealousy of Madeno’s,
his misery and the self-sacrifice of the 12
steadfast friends, Dick Whitsunday saw but
£9l, the recruiter’s fee of seven pounds on
the thirteen heads.
He got them all aboard safely, and signed
them on, gave them an old pipe, a shred of
tobacco and a few beads, with a mouth-
organ and sarong for Madeno. Then he
put them into the hold and laid nearly all
the hatch-gratings on, for fear they should
lose their courage and change their minds.
But he did not see, when the moon rose,
barred wickedly with black clouds, a tinge
of mephistophelean red in them, that eight
young women gave themselves to the sea-
ward tide that swept them down to the
anchor of the Three Kings. They clasped
their legs round • her chains, and called
softly to the water: and four children of five
years or thereabouts gave themselves over to
the sea in their turn, and so were lifted in

gsd
the wan moonlight from the chains to the
foot-ropes below the bowsprit, and then past
the ghostly white of her figurehead, inboard,
The youngest and handsomest of the women
was Madeno’s wife ; she wrung the water
from her hair, and eased the burden from
her head to her shoulders, so that it could
be seen she had swum from shore with a
two-year-old child tied to her head.
The women slipped along the decks warily,
fearing detection; they came to the
slaver’s hold—-“the blackbirds’ cage”—-
called to the men below, found the hatches
on, and sent their whispers down to the
darkness. The mouth-organ, played execra-
bly by Madeno, answered Madeno’s woman,
She called to him. Madeno put the mouth-
organ into his loin-cloth and answered her
gladly. And then all these voluntary exiles,
finding that the real cause of Madeno’s
trouble was apologetic, and loving, and
anxious to have him at home again, sud-
denly desired to go on shore. But the
hatches were home, and the wedges driven
against the bands at the coaming ; and then
the voice of Whitsunday, raw with brandy,
drove all the women to a huddle oil the fore-
castle head, there to lie low in the half-
darkness that followed the clouds rushing
across the moon.
Whitsunday’s voice followed them for-
ward, calling on the crew to rouse out and
weigh anchor; a sudden falling of the
glass in the cabin had told the one white
man of the ship the danger that threatened
to cut the ship in two, using the two horns
0 t the lagoon as scissors.
The swell rose as if it would lift the reef
with it; huge blacknesses came out of the.
east as if in pursuit of the flying moon; the
clouds thickened until the moon was extin-
guished ; the tide seemed to hang upon itself
as if too thick to pour; and then rain fell
in drenches, roaring witn the sea that
sounded only with its own motion, there
being yet not a breath of wind to lift the
sickly staleness of the air. The anchor came
up by slow links; the clink of the pawls
was lost in the dolorous chant of the crew
and the roar of rain and sea.
Then the tide lifted itself bodily ; the sea
seemed a great liquid plateau hinting at
voraginous depressions far away. Whit-
Sunday knew he was right to weigh anchor,
but now saw that with the shoreward set of
the tremendous tide the right thing had been
half the wrong thing; for there was no wind
to take him to sea from the dangers of
the teeth of coral, all the more terrible
because they were hidden. But he got her
slowly round ; for a puff of wind came and
laid her head towards the opening of the
reef, sufficient air then coming steadily to
give her steerageway.
Fear came in the rising wind and settled
on the ship. The children • wailed their
terror; the women aided them with screams,
and the men, locked up in the threatening
darkness, and hearing the sea hitting the
schooner solidly as if it were not liquid but
a million riveters’ hammers falling on her
sides, began to clamor to be loosed.
The Three Kings felt the rising wind, and
under very light canvas moved more quickly
towards the open seas. Then the wind came
in a shriek as if in pain, and fell again ; but
it was enough. The Three Kings cleared
the lagoon; and Whitsunday was relieved
because now he had a larger and open field
to give battle to the elements.
But two of his crew at the cry of the
women had spoken with the men below ; and
now they knocked out the wedges and helped
Madeno and his twelve friends to the deck.
Whitsunday found them there, cursed them
and struck Madeno to the deck. The clouds
parted and showed the moon clear for a
moment, looking down at that black hell of
tortured water, growing storm and love of
gain and fear of death —looked down calmly,
the eye of a god on the passions of men.
The men cowered ; the women, fearing for
their children, turned faces of menace on
Whitsunday. Madeno’s child cried at his
feet. Sick with it all, mad with the rage of
loss of cargo and vessel, and also insane
with the insanity of the thwarted egoist,
Whitsunday seized Madeno’s child by a leg,
knocked its head against the rail, and threw
it. wailing, overboard. The mother shrieked
and leaping to the rail followed it; Madeno,
dazed, rose to his feet and rushed at Whit-
sunday. He got one blow in and tried for
a fingerhold on the throat; but Whitsunday
beat him off easily, stepped back and drew
his revolver. At that Madeno, knowing that
a revolver can strike further than a hand,
leaped to the rail and went over as the
revolver cracked—and missed.
Then with a scream the wind tore itself
out of the darkness; the moon was again
covered, but came for a moment to show a
wilderness of great waves and dirty spume,
-a-s if that tide had furrowed the ocean floor
and raised the sea-slime to the level of the
air. There was no shriek of wind now ; it
settled in a few minutes into a long and
steady roaring.
The Three Kings plunged into it. The little
sail that was set blew out; the crew had
never had any stomach for Whitsunday, and
now that King Hurricane had deposed King
rirato, they sat down and let King Hurri-
cane have his way. Whitsunday took the
helm; and, through that inky blackness torn
with tremendous sound, but never by light,
steered the Three Kings with such an in-
stinct as bids horses shy at almost anything
m the dark. She shipped a great comber
that came over the fo’c’s’le head, roared
along the decks, carrying most of Madeno’s
friends and their women overboard, and
poured into the open hold. Whitsunday felt
her settling deeper in the water, but in that
hell of sound and blackness he could not
steer but by luck.
’ Another sea climbed aboard, and yet
another, and he could feel her floating more
easily and yet more deeply. He cursed
Madeno and his jealousy; he cursed the
ship and the storm, and felt her settling
down the more. Then a momentary glimpse
of the moon showed him his position almost
as plainly as if he had had a chart. Having
his knowledge of the coast and the lagoon,
he now put the helm to port, deciding to
beach ship, and give himself one more chance.
The Three Kings, turning to port in a long,
flattened arc, _ suddenly met a tongue of
coral, and with that tremendous, driving
Wind, now veering seaward and pressing on
her starboard quarter, she rose at the reef
like a horse at a fence, steeplec-hased it and
collapsed in the mad water behind it like
an empty matchbox broken by a fist. In
that hell of aerial sound and broken water,
the smashing of her could not be heard;
Whitsunday went through it unable to think,
guided only by the instinct to live. A great
wave swept him to the sand that banked
one of the horned beaches of the lagoon,
carrying him far above the shore line, so
that he felt trees about him and was gripped
by the fear that this heavy flotsam would
break him as a snail is crushed underfoot.
But luck was with him, though it might be
the luck that keeps the condemned in good
health for the executioner. The wave broke
and left him; he filled his lungs with air
and staggered forward, thrust at by
branches wind-torn from the trees; the
roaring of the sea drowned in the roaring
of the wind, punctuated by the pattering of
cocoanuts falling from the palms, the crash
of great trees playing skittles with little
trees, and the steady sounds, its of rifle firing,
of cracking and rending timber. At last,
miraculously preserved from all the projec-
tiles of the jungle, he came to an open space
of high tussocky grass- and belts of scrub
so low that they escaped the fury of the
hurricane. Beaten sore and bruised to help-
lessness he crawled into the scrub, lay flat
as a board and sank into the stupor of
weariness.
* * * #
The village did not awake, for it had not
slept. Cowering beneath partly-collapsed
huts it waited for the sun ; its fear connect-
ing the horrors of the night with that bad
white man who had threatened it the day
before. An hour after daylight came
Madeno, his left arm broken, but otherwise
unhurt, and bringing his story of the hell
ship, the Three Kings; of Whitsunday, the
bad white man who murdered a wailing
child; of Madeno’s wife, who could make
Madeno jealous no more. They looked at
their ruined village, at the palm groves
rent and flattened, at the disturbed soil of
the gardens shaven even of grass as by the
razor of Titans. And they charged it all to the
devil Whitsunday, whom the other devils
of the sea had saved, and who sailed some-
where with his stolen 12 men, seven women
and four children. And they cursed Whit-
sunday and their hearts swelled with the
hope of great revenge. .
Before noon they knew that Whitsunday’s
devils had deserted him. Pieces of the
broken ship came ashore; then the bodies
of four men, a‘ woman and a child. But
neither the . woman nor the child had been
Madeno’s. It was at mid afternoon that,
finding other bodies along the shore, Madeno
led a party to search for the body of Whit-
sunday ; and they searched expecting to find
him alive.
They found the after-part of the Three
Kings already partly silted up with the
sand the wiki water had carried in suspen-
sion; and half a mile further in they found
Whitsunday sleeping. Madeno awoke him
with the kick of insult, delivered from the
flat of the foot.
Bloody of eye, skinned by salt, wind and
sun, Bis face bruised by his passage through
sea and forest, Whitsunday sat up, and,
quite beaten, let them take him prisoner.
With loud cries of joy and hatred they
dragged him through the ruined forest,
showed him the destruction of grove and
garden ; told him he should pay. But weari-
ness was stronger than fear, and he walked
in stupor, hoping only to have it done with
soon.
They brought him to the village and threw
him down and he was asleep in an instant.
But Madeno would not trust this devil to
know human weariness, and fastened his
feet with vines. They opened a council as
the sun disappeared, and they finished it as
the moon rose in a clear sky. Then they
lighted the fires and woke him. He saw
and understood, then closed his eyes again,
too weary to fear.
Four men carried him to the fire and laid
him down by it. He set his teeth to endure,
thankful that he was too beaten to feel poig-
nantly. and that the anesthesia of weariness
was coming over him again.
They would have placed him in the flames
then, but Madeno stopped them. Whitsun-
day had paid him, not in cash, but in kind,
so would they pay Whitsunday—with pain.
Therefore, in all justice, must his gifts be
returned to him. Madeno had landed naked,
but the chief possessed a new sarong of
turkey red and a jew’s harp left by a decent
trader a month before. These Madeno begged
of the chief, who gravely agreed, as a man
who loved justice too.
Madeno awakened Dick Whitsunday and
gave him the sarong and mouth-organ. Whit-
sunday, understanding, turned his face from
the fire and closed his eyes.
And then the game of Devil’s Tool began
—-Black on White ; player Bed. And player
Bed was Fire.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD
Hands.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I pity the poor hands so deeply seamed, •
Calloused and warted, and each blunted
nail
Which points such splendid moral to the
tale.
Not hands are they, but implements; and
deemed
As such to raise the bastion heavy-beamed,
To swing the girder, forge the shaft and
rail,
Burr the white rivet or to rend the bale,
And wake to life the dream some dreamer
dreamed !
Hard, gnarlfed hands, brown as the pence ye
earn,
Groping so meekly, while the parapet
Sings to th(> stars for joy at being made!
Poor hands, I weep that ye so mutely yearn!
Fathers of granite palaces, and yet
Wrung in despair within their coldest shade!
MAOKILAND.
